Definition and Ruling of al-Mushtarak (Homonym)
Defines mushtarak and states ruling with ijma proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mushtarak in Hanafi usul, state its ruling that multiple meanings cannot be intended simultaneously, and identify the ijma proof from the word 'quru' that supports this ruling.

Core Idea
A word placed for multiple meanings cannot convey all at once; evidence must select one.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, Imam al-Shashi defines the term 'mushtarak' and clarifies its legal ruling. A mushtarak word is one that has been assigned to two or more distinct meanings without any one being prioritized. The key ruling is that such a word cannot be used to intend all its meanings simultaneously; rather, one meaning must be chosen based on contextual or legal evidence. This ruling is supported by the consensus (ijma) of the scholars regarding the interpretation of the word 'quru' in the Qur'an, as the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools take different meanings but agree it cannot mean both at once.

Guided Path
1
Define al-mushtarak
Identify the definition given by the author: a word placed for two or more different meanings without one being prioritized. Note that the Hanafi usuli definition differs from the linguistic definition that requires multiple original placements.
2
State the ruling
The author states that when one meaning becomes apparent by evidence, consideration of the other meanings falls away. Thus, one cannot intend multiple meanings in a single usage.
3
Locate the proof
The author cites the ijma of scholars on the word 'quru' (Quran 2:228) as proof that a mushtarak cannot be used for all its meanings at once. Note that this is an ijma, making the proof qat'i.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The fact that the scholars differed on which meaning (hayd or tuhr) is correct, yet all agreed it could not mean both, demonstrates the principle that a mushtarak must be restricted to one meaning. The differing interpretations themselves prove the prohibition of simultaneous intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشترك والمؤول: المشترك ما وضع لمعنيين مختلفين أو لمعان لا على سبيل الانتظام. هذا مثال المشترك بين المعاني. وحكم المشترك أنه إذا تعين الواحد أي الظهور بدليل من الدلائل سقط اعتبار إرادة غيره، ولهذا أجمع العلماء رحمهم الله تعالى على أن لفظ القروء المذكور في كتاب الله تعالى محمول إما على الحيض كما هو مذهبنا أو على الطهر كما هو مذهب الشافعي، وقال محمد عليه الرحمة: إذا أوصى لموالي بني فلان ولبني فلان موال من أعلى وموال من أسفل فمات الموصي قبل البيان بطلت الوصية في حق الفريقين لاستحالة بحكمه بعد التأويل، لكن الحكم يضاف إليه لا إلى التأويل كما في حكم المهمل بعد البيان غير أن الحكم وإن أضيف إلى الكتاب فهو ظي لعدم التصريح. ويظهر من الأمثلة أنه ليس مشترك الأصولي ما هو المشهور في العرف واللغة والصناعات الآخر المعتبر فيه تعدّد الوضع الأصلي، كذا في الحصول. والمؤول قسم من المشترك بحسب الظاهر؛ إذ هو المشترك المصروف إلى أحد معانيه بنحو من التأويل وضرب من الاجتهاد، ولعلّ التحقيق أنه قسيم له.
Chapter on the Homonym and the Interpreted: The homonym is that which is placed for two different meanings or for multiple meanings without priority. This is an example of a homonym among meanings. The ruling on the homonym is that when one meaning becomes determined, i.e., apparent by some evidence, consideration of intending others falls away. For this reason, the scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, are in consensus that the word 'quru' mentioned in the Book of Allah is interpreted either as menstruation, as is our school, or as purity, as is the school of al-Shafi'i. And Muhammad, may mercy be upon him, said: If someone bequeaths to the mawali of the tribe of so-and-so, and to the tribe of so-and-so whose mawali are from the higher ones and from the lower ones, and the testator dies before clarification, the bequest is void with respect to both groups because it is impossible to apply the ruling after interpretation, but the ruling is attributed to him, not to the interpretation, just as in the ruling of the ambiguous after clarification, except that the ruling, though attributed to the text, is unclear due to lack of explicit statement. And it appears from the examples that the homonym of the usuli is not what is well-known in custom, language, and other disciplines where multiple original placements are considered; this is in al-Husul. And the interpreted is a division of the homonym apparently, for it is the homonym directed to one of its meanings by some sort of interpretation and ijtihad, and perhaps the investigation is that it is a counterpart to it.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines the term 'mushtarak' (homonym) and clarifies its ruling. The author first gives the definition: a word placed for two or more different meanings without any one being prioritized. Examples include 'jariyah' (meaning slave girl or ship) and 'mushtari' (meaning buyer or the planet Jupiter). The passage then states the ruling: when one meaning becomes determined by evidence, the other meanings can no longer be intended simultaneously. This is supported by the ijma' of the scholars on the word 'quru'' in the Qur'an, which can mean either menstruation (Hanafi view) or purity (Shafi'i view), but cannot be used to mean both at once. Imam Muhammad's example of a bequest to 'mawali' further illustrates that if a word has multiple possible referents, it cannot be applied to all without specification. The commentary also distinguishes between the usuli's definition of mushtarak and the common linguistic definition, and explains that 'mu'awwal' (interpreted) is technically a counterpart to mushtarak, not a sub-type. The overall message is that a mushtarak cannot be used for multiple meanings in a single intention; one must choose based on evidence.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): ما وضع لمعنيين مختلفين أو لمعان لا على سبيل الانتظام
مؤول (mu'awwal): المشترك المصروف إلى أحد معانيه بنوع من التأويل والاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on using a mushtarak word to intend all its meanings in a single usage?
A) Permissible if the meanings are related.
B) Not permissible.
C) Permissible only in poetry.
D) Permissible if the word is from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word cannot convey multiple meanings simultaneously; evidence must select one, as proven by ijma.

Al-Mu'awwal as a Counterpart to Al-Mushtarak
Defines mu'awwal and gives a fiqh example of unresolved mushtarak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mushtarak and mu'awwal in Hanafi usul, and apply Imam Muhammad's example of a bequest to mawali to understand the consequence of leaving a mushtarak unspecified.

Core Idea
Mu'awwal is a counterpart to mushtarak: it is the mushtarak that has been directed to one meaning through interpretation.

This part of the text from Usul al-Shashi explains the term 'mu'awwal' (interpreted) and its relationship to mushtarak. While some view mu'awwal as a type of mushtarak, the author suggests it is a counterpart. The passage also provides a practical example from Imam Muhammad regarding a bequest to 'mawali' (clients) without specification, demonstrating that if a mushtarak remains unresolved at the time of the deceased's death, the bequest becomes void.

Guided Path
1
Define mu'awwal
Understand the definition: mu'awwal is the mushtarak that has been turned to one of its meanings through interpretation and ijtihad.
2
Distinguish mushtarak and mu'awwal
Note that the author posits mu'awwal as a qasim (counterpart) to mushtarak, not a type. This means that a mushtarak is the general term; once it is interpreted to one meaning, it becomes mu'awwal.
3
Apply the concept to the mawali example
Analyze Imam Muhammad's example: if a testator bequeaths to the mawali of two groups, but dies before specifying which meaning of mawali (higher clients or lower clients) he intended, the bequest is void because the mushtarak cannot be resolved after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what happens if a testator bequeaths to 'mawali' (a mushtarak) and dies before specifying the intended meaning?
A) The bequest is valid and can be interpreted by the heirs.
B) The bequest is void for both groups.
C) The bequest is divided equally among all possible meanings.
D) The bequest is valid if a scholar provides an interpretation.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak must be resolved by the speaker; if death prevents clarification, the legal act (e.g., bequest) becomes void.

The Literal vs. Figurative Meaning of 'Bikr' in Bequests
Ruling on who qualifies as 'bikr' in a bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word is preferred over the figurative (majaz) when interpreting a bequest, and determine whether a woman qualifies as a 'bikr' based on physical virginity.

Core Idea
A bequest to 'virgins' includes only those with intact hymen; a fornicator is excluded even if unmarried.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi demonstrates the critical usul principle that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word governs unless there is a valid reason to adopt the figurative (majaz). In the context of a bequest to 'virgin daughters,' the term 'bikr' is examined: does it refer to a woman who has never been married, or one whose hymen is intact? The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the literal meaning is physical virginity, and a woman who has fornicated is not included, even though she may be called a 'bikr' in common speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the bequest phrase and key word
The bequest is to 'the virgin daughters of Bani Fulan' (أبكار بني فلان). The key term is 'bikr' (أبكار).
2
Recognize the two meanings of 'bikr'
The literal meaning (haqiqah) is a woman with an intact hymen. The figurative meaning (majaz) is a woman who has never been married.
In common usage, 'bikr' is often used figuratively for an unmarried woman.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule of preference
Hanafi usul holds that when a word has both a literal and a figurative meaning, the literal is intended unless there is a contextual indicator (qarinah) to shift to the figurative. Here, no such indicator exists.
The rule: الحقيقة مقدمة على المجاز (Haqlqah takes precedence over Majaz).
4
Examine the exceptions
If virginity is lost by accident (jump, menses, injury), the woman is still a bikr literally, so she is included. Only intentional loss through fornication or similar disqualifies.
Accidental loss does not change the literal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: إذا أوصى لأبكار بني فلان لا تدخل المصابة بالفجور في حكم الوصية، لأن البكر حقيقة غير المصابة، وإنما سمي المصابة بالزنا بكراً؛ لأنها لم تزوّج، وإنما قيدت بـ«الفجور»؛ لأنها لو زالت بكارتها بوثبة أو حيضة أو تعنيس جراحة ونحوها تدخل في الوصية؛ لأنها بكر حقيقة.
We say: If one bequeaths to the virgin daughters of Bani Fulan, the one afflicted by fornication does not enter into the rule of the bequest, because the virgin literally is the one not afflicted, and one afflicted by fornication is called virgin only because she has not been married, and it is restricted by 'fornication' because if her virginity was removed by a jump, menstruation, or a wound from old age etc., she enters the bequest because she is a virgin literally.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling in the context of the usul principle that literal and figurative meanings cannot be combined. The example is a bequest to the 'virgin daughters' of a tribe. The ruling states that a woman who has lost her virginity through fornication (zina) does not qualify as a 'bikr' for the bequest, because the literal meaning of 'bikr' is a woman whose hymen is intact. A woman who has fornicated is given the name 'bikr' only figuratively because she hasn't been married. However, if the virginity is lost by other means like a jump, menses, or injury due to old age, she is still considered a bikr literally and thus included. The commentary emphasizes that this is based on the rule that literal and figurative cannot be intended together; since the literal meaning is intended, the figurative is excluded.

Key Terms
البكر (al-bikr): In this context, the literal meaning of a woman whose hymen is intact, not a woman who has never been married.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a woman who lost her virginity by jumping off a wall:
A) Is excluded from the bequest.
B) Is included in the bequest.
C) Is included only if she never married.
D) Is excluded because her virginity is gone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when interpreting a bequest, the literal meaning of a word is definitive unless a contextual indicator demands otherwise.

When the Literal Meaning is Impossible or Abandoned: The Rule of Haqiqah and Majaz in Hanafi Usul
Rule with examples and a madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that when a literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, the metaphorical meaning takes effect, and evaluate the related ikhtilaf regarding agency for litigation.

Core Idea
When haqiqah fails, majaz prevails — for both impossible and abandoned literal meanings.

In Hanafi usul, words are presumed to be used in their literal meaning (haqiqah), but this presumption is rebutted when the literal meaning is impossible (muta'arrid) or abandoned (mahjoor) in custom or Shari'ah. This lesson examines the rule and its application in oaths and agency, including a key dispute with al-Shafi'i and Zufar over the scope of agency for litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two triggers for abandoning haqiqah
Recognise that impossibility (muta'arrid) and abandonment (mahjoor) are distinct grounds that shift meaning from literal to metaphorical. Impossibility may be physical or legal; abandonment may be in custom or Shari'ah.
The text equates 'abandoned Shar'an' with 'abandoned custom' for legal effect.
2
Analyse the oath examples
Work through the examples: swearing not to eat from a tree or pot (impossible) → metaphorical meaning (fruit or cooked food); swearing not to drink from a well (impossible) → drawing water; swearing not to place foot in a house (abandoned in custom) → entering.
Note that lapping (kar') from a well is technically possible but extremely difficult; the rule treats it as impossible due to custom.
3
Extend the rule to agency for litigation
Understand that 'agency for litigation itself' (tawkeel bi nafs al-khusoomah) is abandoned Shar'an because disputation is prohibited (Quran 8:46). Therefore, the literal meaning of 'litigation' is abandoned, and the agent's authority is interpreted broadly to include any response (admission or denial).
This is a direct application of the rule: abandoned Shar'an is like abandoned custom.
4
Examine the madhhab dispute
Compare the Hanafi position (broad authority) with that of al-Shafi'i and Zufar (limited to denial only). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference): if the agent admits a claim, Hanafi holds it binding; others do not.
The Hanafi position is supported by the principle that abandoned literal meaning shifts to the customary metaphorical understanding.
5
Apply the rule to hypothetical cases
Consider other scenarios where literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, such as oaths involving human flesh (included implicitly) or fasting on prohibited days (included within the year). Understand the nuance that implicit inclusion does not override the rule for primary meanings.
The footnote clarifies that human flesh is included implicitly in 'meat' and thus does not contradict the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز المشار إليها. ١٢ المشار إليها. ١٢ حلف لا يأكل. ۱۲ المتعدّرة إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشجرة أو من هذه القدر فإن أكل أي عينها. ١٢ أي عين القدر. ١,١٢ (1) أكل . ١٢ أكل. ١٢ الشجرة أو القدر متعذر فينصرف ذلك إلى ثمرة الشجرة وإلى ما يحل في عادة ولو أمكن عقلا. ١٢ ن اللحم وغيره. ١٢ أكل. ١٢ أي جرمها. ١٢ القدر حتى لو أكل من عين الشجرة أو من عين القدر بنوع (۳) أي من مائها. ١٢ لامتناع إرادتها. ١٢ تكلّف لا يحنث، أيضا. ١٢ (٤) أي حلفه. ١٢ وعلى هذا قلنا : إذا حلف لا يشرب من هذه البير ينصرف ذلك إلى مجازا. ۱۲ بعد الدخول فيها. ١٢ لعدم وجود شرط الحنث وهو الشرب. ١٢ الاغتراف حتى لو فرضنا أنه لو كرع بنوع تكلف لا يحنث بالاتفاق، ونظير الكرع تناول الماء بالفم. ١٢ المهجورة لو حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان، فإن إرادة وضع القدم الأصل وهو أن الحقيقة المهجورة متروكة. ١٢ (^) مهجورة عادة، وعلى هذا قلنا: التوكيل بنفس الخصومة (۱) لأنه لا يتيسر الوصول إليه إلا بكلفة ومشقة. ١٢ (۲) تفريع على ترك الحقيقة والصرف إلى المجاز. ١٢ (۳) أي: على أنّ المتعدّرة والمهجورة يصار فيهما إلى المجاز. ١٢ (٤) قوله : [ ينصرف ذلك إلى الاغتراف دون الكرع وهو تناول الماء بفمه، وإن كان حقيقة الشرب هو الكرع؛ لأنّ «من» لابتداء الغاية، فيقتضي أن يكون ابتداء شربه من البير، وذلك لا يتأتى إلا بالكرع خاصة، لكنه متعدّر فيترك الحقيقة حتى لو كرع بنوع مشقة لا يحنث، فيراد به الاغتراف أو الشرب بالإناء مجازاً، فيحنث بوجود أحدهما. ١٢ (٥) المجاز وهو الاغتراف والجامع وجود أخذ الماء فيهما. ١٢ (٦) قوله : [فإن إرادة وضع القدم... إلخ فيه إشاره إلى أن الهجران إنما يعتبر في الإرادة لا في العمل حتى لو كان معتبراً في العمل لا يستقيم إيراد وضع القدم في مثالها لإمكان وضع القدم في الدار. ١٢ (۷) قوله: [مهجورة عادةً حتى لو وضع القدم من غير دخول لا يحنث، فإن قيل: وضع القــــدم حقيقة مستعملة فكيف أورده عن أمثلة المهجورة، قلت: هو مهجور بالنسبة إلى أحد وهو وضع القدم الذي يحصل بلا دخول لا مطلق وضع القدم فإنّه غير مهجور، فيحنث كيف ما دخل حافياً أو متنعلاً. ١٢ جزئيه ( قوله: [التوكيل بنفس الخصومة] بأن قال لرجل: «وكلتك بالخصومة»، أو قال: «أنت وكيلـــي بالخصومة في هذه الدعوى»، ونحوهما. ١٢ مجلس: "المدينة العلمية" (الدعوة الإسلامية ( ٣٤ ) -  أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي عن قيد الرد والاعتراف. ١٢ أي خصم مؤكل الوكيل. ١٢. فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز خصم مؤكله. ١٢ ينصرف إلى مطلق جواب الخصم حتى يسع للوكيل أن يجيب بـ (۱) يجوز. ١٢ محقا كان المدعي أو مبطلا. ۲ (۳) كما يسعه أن يجيب بـ «لا»؛ لأنّ التوكيل بنفس الخصومة مهجور شر فيما وضع له وضع اللغة. ١٢ أي الحقيقة. ١٢ خصم مؤكله. ١٢ أي أغلب وأكثر. ١٢ من المجاز. ١٢ (1) قوله: [ينصرف إلى مطلق جواب الخصم] مجازاً فإنّ الخصومة نفساً مهجورة شرعاً قال الله تعالى: وَلاَ تَنَازَعُوا [الأنفال : ٤٦]، والمهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً؛ لأنّ الظاهر من حال المسلمين أن يمتنعوا عـــــن مهجور الشرع لديانتهم وعقلهم، فالتوكيل بنفس الخصومة ينصرف إلى مطلق الجواب المحتوي على الردّ والإقرار، حتى لو أقرّ على موكله بشيء جاز، خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله وزفر رحمه الله من أصحابنا. ١٢ (۲) قوله: [كما يسعه أن يجيب بـ «لا»] أي: بإنكار ما ادعاه الخصم، فإنّ حقيقة الخصومة وهي الإنكار محقاً كان المدعى أو مبطلاً حرام شرعاً. ١٢ (۳) قوله: مهجور شرعاً فإن قلت يرد عليه قوله : «إذا حلف لا يأكل لحماً» فإنّه منقوض بكونه حانثاً بأكل لحم الآدمي مع أن أكله مهجور شرعاً، وكذا قوله : «الله على صوم هذه السنة» فإنّه يتناول الأيام المنهية حيث يجب قضاؤها على ما في الفروع، فلو كان المهجور شرعاً كالمهجور عادةً لم يحنث بأكل لحم الآدمي و لم يجب عليه قضاء الأيام المنهية والجواب : أنّ انعقاد اليمين على لحم الآدمي إنما هـو لتضمّن مطلق اللحم المذكور في اليمين إياه فصار ضمنياً، وكذا حال الأيام المنهية فإنها داخلة في ضمن السنة؛ لأنها جزئها والضمني لا يلتقت إليه، فكم من شيء لا يعتبر قصداً ويثبت ضمناً، فافهم.
Section on Literal and Metaphorical Meanings. The impossible: if one swears not to eat from this tree or from this pot, then if he eats from the substance of the tree or the substance of the pot, it is impossible, so it turns to the fruit of the tree and to what is cooked in it customarily, even if possible intellectually. ... And upon this we said: if one swears not to drink from this well, it turns to drawing water metaphorically... And the abandoned: if one swears not to place his foot in so-and-so's house, then intending to place the foot is abandoned in custom, and upon this we said: agency for litigation itself turns to absolute response of the opponent...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a legal rule establishing that when the literal (haqiqah) meaning of an expression is impossible (muta'arrid) or abandoned (mahjur) in practice, the default shifts to the metaphorical (majaz) meaning. The commentator provides several oath examples: swearing not to eat from a tree or pot refers to the tree's fruit and what is cooked, not the inedible wood or pot; swearing not to drink from a well refers to drawing water (ighthiraf) not lapping (kar'), as lapping is physically impossible from a well without extreme difficulty; swearing not to place one's foot in a house refers to entering, since placing the foot without entering is abandoned. The same principle applies to agency: 'agency for litigation itself' (tawkeel bi nafs al-khusoomah) is abandoned in Shari'ah because disputation is discouraged (Quran 8:46), so the agent's authority is interpreted broadly to include any response (admission or denial). This view differs from al-Shafi'i and Zufar. The footnote addresses an objection: if abandoned Shar'an is like abandoned custom, then eating human flesh should not break an oath not to eat meat, but it does. The answer is that human flesh is included implicitly in the general term 'meat' and the implicit is not considered independently.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established in the language for which it was coined.
المجاز (al-majaz): The figurative, secondary meaning of a word used due to a relationship (al-'illaqa) between the literal and figurative sense.
المتعذرة (al-muta'arrid): A literal meaning that is impossible to apply physically or legally in the specific context.
المهجورة (al-mahjura): A literal meaning that is abandoned in custom or in Shari'ah, i.e., not actually used or not intended in practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat from a pot, what does the oath refer to according to the rule of impossible literal meaning?
A) The substance of the pot itself.
B) The food cooked in the pot.
C) Both the pot and its contents.
D) Nothing, the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When a literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, the default shifts to the metaphorical meaning, a key Hanafi usul principle applied in oaths, agency, and beyond.

Conditions for Valid Metaphor in Legal Rulings
Analyzing conditions for valid metaphor using 'my daughter' and 'my son'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for valid metaphorical usage (isti'ara) in Hanafi usul by distinguishing cases of direct conflict versus non-conflict, and apply the rule to a new example.

Core Idea
Metaphor is invalid if the literal meaning directly contradicts the intended legal ruling.

This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul examines a key distinction: when a word's literal meaning (haqiqa) clashes with the legal purpose of the statement, metaphorical use (majaz/isti'ara) is null. The examples of 'my daughter' for divorce and 'my son' for emancipation illustrate the boundary where conflict invalidates or permits the metaphor. Understanding this condition helps us evaluate metaphorical statements in fiqh contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
State the literal meaning (haqiqa) of the word used metaphorically. For example, 'bint' means biological daughter.
2
Identify the intended legal meaning
What legal effect is the speaker trying to achieve? In the first example, divorce (talaq).
3
Check for direct conflict
Does the literal meaning directly contradict the permissibility of the intended legal outcome? Daughter-ship is absolutely contrary to marriage, so the metaphor is invalid.
Conflict (tanafi) here means the literal meaning cannot coexist with the legal ruling.
4
Apply to the son example
Son-ship (bunuwwa) does not directly conflict with ownership; a father owns his young son. Ownership leads to emancipation, so no conflict exists.
Thus 'this is my son' said to a slave validly effects emancipation.
5
Derive the principle
Formulate the rule: Isti'ara is only valid when the literal meaning does not conflict with the intended legal ruling. If conflict exists, the metaphor is ineffective.
This principle governs all metaphorical statements in legal contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحقيقة والمجاز اللفظ لو صح معناه لكان منافيا للنكاح فيكون منافيا لحكمه ذلك اللفظ. وهي أن يذكر البنتية ويراد بها الطلاق. بين البنتية والطلاق. أي المولى لعبده الأكبر. أي حكم النكاح. الطلاق، وهو أي النكاح. ولا استعارة مع وجود التنافي بخلاف قوله: «هذا ابني» فإن البنوة لا تنافي في الابن. أي للأب. أي على الأب. ثبوت الملك للأب بل يثبت الملك له ثم يعتق عليه. في الابن. فلا تكون منافية للعتق فتصح استعارتها للعتق فالفرق ظاهر. (1) قوله: [لو صح] ليس المراد بالصحة هاهنا ما يقابل الفساد، بل هو بمعنى الثبوت أي: لو ثبت مؤداه وهو البنتية، والصحة بمعنى الثبوت جاءت في لغة العرب كما قال الشاعر: «صح عند الناس أني عاشق»، وتمامه: يا صبيح الوجه يا رطب البدن يا قريب العهد من شرب اللبن صح عند الناس أني عاشق غير أن لم يعرفوا عشقي لمن من رأى روحين حلا في البدن روحه روحي وروحي رو. (2) قوله: [بخلاف... إلخ] يرد عليه بأن البنوة مناف للملك فيكون منافياً لحكمه العتق، كما أن البنتية مناف للنكاح فيكون منافياً لحكمه وهو الطلاق، فكيف يصح استعارة البنوة للعتق في قوله: «هذا ابني»، فأجاب بقوله: «بخلاف هذا... إلخ».
Chapter on Reality and Metaphor: The word if its meaning is established would be conflicting with marriage, thus conflicting with its ruling. And it is that one mentions 'daughter-ness' intending divorce. Between daughter-ness and divorce. i.e. the master to his older slave. i.e. the ruling of marriage. The divorce, and it is the marriage. And there is no metaphor with existence of conflict, unlike his statement: 'this is my son' because son-ship does not conflict in the son. i.e. for the father. i.e. upon the father. Establishment of ownership for the father, rather ownership is established for him then he emancipates him. In the son. So it is not conflicting with emancipation, so its metaphor for emancipation is valid, so the difference is apparent. (1) His statement: [if established] 'correctness' here does not mean opposite of corruption, rather it means confirmation, i.e. if its import is confirmed, which is daughter-ness. And correctness in the sense of confirmation occurs in Arabic as the poet said: 'It became established among people that I am a lover' ... (2) His statement: [unlike... etc.] It is objected that son-ship conflicts with ownership, thus conflicts with its ruling of emancipation, just as daughter-ness conflicts with marriage, thus conflicts with its ruling of divorce, so how can metaphor of son-ship for emancipation be valid in his statement 'this is my son'? So he answered with his statement: 'unlike this... etc.'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul al-Fiqh, with the hashya of Ahsan al-Hawashi, discusses the conditions for valid metaphorical usage (isti'ara) in legal rulings. The author begins by stating that if a word's literal meaning (haqiqa) would conflict with the legal ruling, then metaphorical use is invalid. He gives the example: saying 'this is my daughter' (bintiyya) to mean divorce. Here, the literal meaning of daughter-ship directly contradicts the permissibility of marriage (nikah), so the metaphor for divorce is invalid because the conflict is absolute. He then contrasts this with the case 'this is my son' (ibn) to mean emancipation ('itq). Son-ship does not conflict with ownership; rather, a father owns his son by birth, but then ownership is followed by automatic emancipation. Thus, there is no conflict, and the metaphor for emancipation is valid. The footnotes clarify: 'sahh' means 'established' not 'valid', and a poetic citation supports this linguistic point. The second footnote addresses a potential objection: if son-ship leads to ownership and thus emancipation, it still seems to conflict with the declared intent? The rebuttal is that the conflict is not direct—ownership is established first, then emancipation removes it, so the metaphor is acceptable.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning of a word as originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical use of a word for a meaning other than its original, based on a connection.
استعارة (isti'ara): Use of a word for a metaphorical meaning due to a connection (ittisal) between literal and figurative meanings.
بنتية (bintiyya): The quality of being a daughter; here used metaphorically for divorce.
بنوة (bunuwwa): The quality of being a son; here used metaphorically for emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is using 'هذه ابنتي' (this is my daughter) invalid as a metaphor for divorce according to this passage?
A) Because the word 'bint' is not used for divorce in Arabic.
B) Because the literal meaning of daughter-ship conflicts with the permissibility of marriage.
C) Because divorce requires a specific formula like 'anti taliq'.
D) Because the metaphor has no connection (ittisal).

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical usage in legal rulings is valid only when the literal meaning does not directly conflict with the intended legal consequence.

Two Valid Paths for Metaphor in Legal Rulings
Defining the two valid methods for metaphorical reasoning in shari'a: via 'illa and via pure cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid methods for metaphorical reasoning in shari'a rulings and explain the distinction between 'illa and sabab mahd as bases for legal metaphor.

Core Idea
Legal metaphor in shari'a operates via connection to the 'illa or to a pure cause, not just through similarity.

In Hanafi usul, metaphor (isti'ara) is a key tool for extending rulings to new cases. This lesson unpacks the two legitimate paths for such metaphorical reasoning: through the effective cause ('illa) and through a pure cause (sabab mahd). Understanding these paths is essential for sound legal analogy and avoiding arbitrary extensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Paths
The text states that metaphor in shari'a rulings is valid via two paths: connection between the 'illa and the ruling, and connection between the sabab mahd and the ruling. Identify these two in the Arabic.
Look for the phrases 'بين العلة والحكم' and 'بين السبب المحض والحكم'.
2
Distinguish 'Illa from Sabab Mahd
The commentary clarifies that 'illa is the direct effective cause necessitating the ruling, while sabab mahd is a cause that only generally leads to the ruling without being specifically designated for it. Contrast the examples: sale leads to ownership but is not the direct cause.
The word 'mahd' (pure) is used to emphasize that sabab here is not 'illa.
3
Understand the Balagha Context
The footnotes differentiate usul terminology from balagha: in usul, isti'ara is synonymous with majaz; in balagha, it is a subset (based on similarity). Note the classification of linguistic and rational metaphor.
This helps avoid confusion between disciplines.
4
Apply the Two Methods
Consider how a ruling can be extended via either path. For example, the ruling for intoxicants is extended to new substances via the 'illa of intoxication (similarity in cause). A pure cause like sale may lead to ownership but is not analogized directly.
The second path (sabab mahd) is less common but recognized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة (1) أي الطريق الموصل إلى صحة الاستعارة. فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة: اعلم أن الاستعارة في أحكام الشرع مطردة بطريقين أحدهما لوجود الاتصال بين العلة والحكم والثاني لوجود الاتصال بين السبب المحض والحكم فالأول منهما يوجب صحة. (1) قوله: [فصل... آه] لما فرغ المصنف عن التفريعات شرع في بيان علاقات المجاز، فقال: «فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة» وهو في عرف الأصوليين يرادف المجاز، وعند أهل البيان قسم من المجاز فإن المجاز عندهم إن كانت فيه علاقة التشبيه يسمى استعارةً بأقسامها، وإن كانت فيه علاقة غير التشبيه من علاقات الخمس والعشرين مثل: السببية والمسببية والحال والمحل واللازم والملزوم وغيرها يسمى مجازاً مرسلاً، كذا في "نور الأنوار". (2) قوله [الاستعارة] الاستعارة استعمال اللفظ للمعنى المجازي لاتصال ومناسبة بين الحقيقة والمجاز، والمناسبة قد يكون من حيث المعنى كـ«الشجاع» يسمى أسداً لوجود المعنى الخاص للأسد وهو الشجاعة، وقد يكون من حيث الذات كما يسمى الحدث غائطاً، والغائط في كلام العرب المكان المطمئن من الأرض ولكن بينهما اتصال من حيث الذات؛ لأن كل من أراد الحدث يختار مكاناً مطمئناً فيسمى باسم الغائط لاتصال ذاتيهما مجاورة، ثم الاستعارة والمجاز على نوعين لغوي وعقلي: أما اللغوي فهو استعمال في غير ما وضع له، والعقلي هو نسبة الحكم إلى غير من هي له، كما يقال: «أنبت الربيع البقل»، ثم اللغوي على نوعين: مستعار ومرسل فالمستعار استعمال اللفظ إلى غير ما وضع له لعلاقة التشبيه، والمرسل: استعمال اللفظ في غير الموضوع له لعلاقة غير التشبيه، ثم المستعار على أربعة أوجه: (1) مصرحة: وهي ذكر المشبه به وإرادة المشبه نحو أسد في الحمام و (2) كناية: وهي أن يذكر المشبه ويراد به المشبه به أي عكس المصرحة، و (3) تخييلية: وهي إثبات لازم المشبه به المتروك للمشبه المذكور، (4) وترشيحية: وهي ذكر ملائم المستعار منه مثال هذه الثلاثة في قول الشاعر: وإذا المنية أنشبت أظفارها ألفيت كل تميمة لم ينفع فذكر «المنية» وإرادة «السبع» مثال الكناية، وذكر اللوازم وهي الألفاظ مثال التخييلية وذكر «النشب» وهو الملائم مثال الترشيحية. (3) قوله: [بين السبب المحض] هو ما يكون مفضياً إلى الحكم في الجملة بدون أن يكون موضوعاً له، مثل: البيع فإنه يفضي إلى ثبوت ملك المتعة إذا صادف الجواري وإن لم يكن موضوعاً له، ومثل الإعتاق فإنه يفضي إلى إزالة ملك المتعة إذا صادف الجواري وإن لم يكن موضوعاً له، وأكد السبب بـ«المحض»؛ لأن السبب قد يطلق على العلة مجازاً، يقال: الشراء سبب للملك والنكاح سبب للحل، كذا في "المعدن".
Chapter on defining the method of metaphor: (1) i.e. the path leading to validity of metaphor. Chapter on defining the method of metaphor: Know that metaphor in the rulings of shari'a is consistently valid via two paths: one due to existence of connection between the cause ('illa) and the ruling, and the second due to existence of connection between the pure cause (sabab mahd) and the ruling. The first of them necessitates validity. (1) His statement: [Chapter... etc.] When the author finished from the subsidiary branches, he began explaining the relations of metaphor, so he said: 'Chapter on defining the method of metaphor' and it in the terminology of usuliyyun is synonymous with majaz, and in the view of balagha scholars it is a division of majaz, because metaphor according to them if it contains a relationship of similarity is called isti'ara with its divisions, and if it contains a relationship other than similarity from the twenty-five relationships such as causality, effect, time, place, necessary and necessary-of, etc. it is called mursal majaz, as in "Nur al-Anwar". (2) His statement: [metaphor] Metaphor is using a word for a metaphorical meaning due to connection and appropriateness between reality and metaphor. And appropriateness may be in terms of meaning, like 'the brave' is called a lion due to existence of the specific meaning of lion which is bravery; and it may be in terms of the essence, as one calls the event a ghait, and ghait in Arab speech is a low-lying place, but between them there is a connection in terms of essence because everyone who wants to relieve himself chooses a low-lying place, so it is named with the name ghait due to connection of their essences with adjacency. Then metaphor is of two types: linguistic and rational. Linguistic is usage in other than what it was coined for, and rational is attributing a ruling to other than its owner, like saying 'the spring caused the plants to grow'. Then linguistic is of two types: musta'ar (borrowed) and mursal (free). Musta'ar is using a word for other than its coined meaning due to a similarity relationship; mursal is using a word for other than its subject matter due to a non-similarity relationship. Then musta'ar is of four types: (1) Explicit: mentioning the thing compared to and intending the thing compared, like 'lion in the bathroom'; (2) Allusive: mentioning the thing compared and intending the thing compared to, i.e. opposite of explicit; (3) Imaginative: affirming a necessary concomitant of the thing compared to that is absent from the mentioned thing compared; (4) Elaborative: mentioning something suitable to the thing from which the borrowing is taken. An example of these three is in the poet's saying: 'And when death sinks its claws, you find every amulet useless.' The mention of 'death' intending 'beast' is example of allusive; the mention of the necessary concomitants which are the words is example of imaginative; and the mention of 'sinking' which is suitable is example of elaborative. (3) His statement: [between the pure cause] It is that which leads to the ruling in general without being made specifically for it, such as sale, for it leads to establishing ownership of pleasure (milk al-mut'a) if it meets slave-girls, even though it was not specifically established for that; and like emancipation, for it leads to removal of ownership of pleasure if it meets slave-girls, even though it was not specifically established for that. And he emphasized the cause with 'pure' because cause may be applied to 'illa metaphorically, it is said: purchase is a cause for ownership, and marriage is a cause for permissibility, as in "al-Ma'din".
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul al-Fiqh, with the hashya, defines the method (tariq) of valid metaphorical reasoning (isti'ara) in shari'a rulings. The author states that legal metaphor operates through two paths: (1) connection between the 'illa (effective cause) and the hukm (ruling), and (2) connection between the sabab mahd (pure cause) and the hukm. The first necessitates validity. The footnotes expand: In usul terminology, isti'ara is synonymous with majaz, while in balagha it is a subset based on similarity. The second footnote defines isti'ara as using a word for a metaphorical meaning due to connection (ittisal) and appropriateness (munasaba) between the literal and metaphorical meanings, giving examples like 'lion' for a brave person (meaning similarity) and 'ghait' for relieving oneself (essence similarity through adjacency). It categorizes metaphor into linguistic (lafzi) and rational ('aqli), and linguistic into musta'ar (based on similarity) and mursal (based on other relations). Musta'ar further divides into four types: explicit, allusive, imaginative, and elaborative, illustrated with a poetic example. The third footnote clarifies 'sabab mahd' as a cause that generally leads to a ruling without being specifically designated for it, like sale leading to ownership of pleasure, or emancipation leading to removal of ownership. The term 'mahd' emphasizes that 'sabab' is sometimes used metaphorically for 'illa.

Key Terms
استعارة (isti'ara): Using a word for a metaphorical meaning due to a connection and appropriateness between literal and figurative meanings.
علة (illa): Effective cause that directly necessitates the ruling.
سبب محض (sabab mahd): A pure cause that leads to a ruling in general without being specifically designated for it, e.g., sale leads to ownership.
مجاز مرسل (majaz mursal): Metaphor based on a relationship other than similarity, from the twenty-five recognized relations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the two ways that metaphor (isti'ara) is valid in shari'a rulings?
A) Connection between subject and predicate, and connection between verb and object.
B) Connection between the 'illa and hukm, and connection between the sabab mahd and hukm.
C) Connection between literal and figurative meaning, and connection between word and its opposite.
D) Connection through similarity and connection through contiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, legal metaphor rests on either a direct effective cause ('illa) or a general pure cause (sabab mahd), both providing valid connections to extend rulings.

The Two Ways of Metaphorical Usage (Isti'ara) in Hanafi Usul
Defining the two ways of metaphorical usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two ways of isti'ara in Hanafi usul and apply them to determine whether metaphorical usage is bidirectional or unidirectional.

Core Idea
Isti'ara via 'illa is bidirectional; via sabab mahd is only from cause to ruling.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author explains that metaphorical usage (isti'ara) in legal rulings follows two distinct paths depending on the nature of the connection between the cause and the ruling. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Hanafi fiqh. We will break down the two types—through the effective cause ('illa) and through the pure cause (sabab mahd)—and see how they apply to practical examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways of isti'ara
Read the opening sentence to find the division into two types based on the nature of the connection. Note the key terms 'illa and sabab mahd.
2
Understand the first way (through 'illa)
This connection makes isti'ara valid from both sides (cause to ruling and ruling to cause). The cause directly necessitates the ruling.
3
Understand the second way (through sabab mahd)
This connection is weaker; isti'ara is only valid from the origin (cause) to the branch (ruling), not vice versa, because the pure cause is not intrinsically tied to the ruling.
4
Analyze the examples
The author provides two examples with slave manumission. In the first, 'ownership' (malak) is treated as an 'illa because it requires full possession; partial ownership does not trigger the condition. In the second, 'purchase' (shira') is a pure cause; partial purchase followed by another purchase does trigger the condition for the second half, showing unidirectional usage.
5
Apply the concept
Consider how this distinction affects legal reasoning when interpreting conditional statements. If the cause is an 'illa, metaphorical substitution works both ways; if sabab mahd, only one way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعريف طريق الاستعارة : اعلم أن الاستعارة في أحكام الشرع مطردة بطريقين أحدهما لوجود الاتصال بين العلة والحكم والثاني لوجود الاتصال بين السبب المحض والحكم فالأوّل منهما يوجب صحة الاستعارة من الطرفين والثاني يوجب صحتها من أحد الطرفين وهو استعارة الأصل للفرع. مثال الأوّل فيما إذا قال: إن ملكت عبدا فهو حرّ فملك نصف العبد فباعه ثم ملك النصف الآخر لم يعتق؛ إذ لم يجتمع في ملكه كل العبد. ولو قال: إن اشتريت عبدا فهو حرّ فاشترى نصف العبد فباعه ثم اشترى النصف الآخر عتق النصف الثاني. ولو عنى بالملك الشراء أو بالشراء الملك؛ لأنهما من الطرفين.
Chapter on Defining the Way of Metaphorical Usage: Know that metaphorical usage in the rulings of the Sharia is consistently found through two ways. One is due to the connection between the effective cause and the ruling. The second is due to the connection between the pure cause and the ruling. The first of these two necessitates the validity of metaphorical usage from both sides. The second necessitates its validity from one side only, and it is the usage of the origin for the branch. An example of the first: if someone says, 'If I own a slave, he is free,' then he owns half the slave, sells it, then owns the other half: the slave is not freed because he did not own the entire slave in his possession at one time. And if he says, 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' then he buys half the slave, sells it, then buys the other half: the second half is freed. And if he intended by 'ownership' purchase or by 'purchase' ownership, that is permissible because they are from both sides.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the way (tariq) of isti'ara (metaphorical usage) in usul al-fiqh. The author divides the ways into two types based on the connection between the cause and the ruling. The first type is where the connection is between the 'illa (effective cause) and the hukm (ruling). Here, isti'ara is valid from both sides: you can use the 'illa to refer to the hukm or the hukm to refer to the 'illa, because each depends on the other. The second type is where the connection is between the sabab mahd (pure cause) and the hukm. This only allows isti'ara from one side: using the origin (asl) for the branch (far'), i.e., using the cause to refer to the ruling, but not vice versa. The reason is that the sabab mahd is not legislated solely for that ruling; its connection is incidental. The author then gives an example using a slave's manumission. In the first case, using 'ownership' (malak) as a cause: if someone says 'if I own a slave, he is free,' partial ownership does not trigger the condition because 'ownership' requires full possession in the custom. In the second case, using 'purchase' (shira') as a cause: it is a pure cause, and partial purchase followed by another purchase triggers the condition for the second half, because purchase is not customarily tied to full ownership. The last sentence notes that if the speaker intended to use one term for the other (e.g., 'ownership' meaning 'purchase'), that is valid because these terms are connected from both sides.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical usage in legal rulings, a type of majaz (figurative speech) used in legal reasoning
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause that directly necessitates the ruling, used in the first way of isti'ara
السبب المحض (al-sabab al-mahd): A pure cause that leads to the ruling only incidentally, not as its direct purpose

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of connection allows metaphorical usage from both sides (the cause and the ruling)?
A) The connection through the pure cause (sabab mahd)
B) The connection through the effective cause ('illa)
C) Both types allow bidirectional usage
D) Neither type allows bidirectional usage

Key Takeaway
The direction of metaphorical usage in legal rulings depends on whether the connection is through an effective cause (bidirectional) or a pure cause (unidirectional).

Isti'ara in 'I Freed You' for Divorce
Applying isti'ara to a divorce scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi usul uses isti'ara (cause-effect metonymy) to derive that saying 'I freed you' with intent of divorce results in an irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
Mentioning the cause stands for the effect: 'freeing' causes divorce by removing the right to intercourse.

The phrase 'I freed you' (حررتك) literally refers to freeing a slave, not divorce. Yet in Hanafi usul, isti'ara allows the cause to stand for the effect. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: freeing removes ownership of the slave (raqaba), which necessarily removes the husband's right to sexual access (milk al-bud'). Thus, mentioning the cause (freeing) can metaphorically indicate the effect (removal of marital rights), making it a valid expression of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning of 'tahrir'
The literal meaning of تحرير is freeing a slave, i.e., removing ownership of the slave (ملك الرقبة).
2
Trace the causal link to marital rights
Freeing a slave removes the master's ownership of the slave's person, which in turn removes any right to sexual access the master had. By analogy, marital rights are also a form of ownership (ملك المتعة), and their removal is the effect of divorce.
This causal link is considered pure (سبب محض) in that freeing automatically leads to removal of the right to intercourse.
3
Apply isti'ara: cause for effect
Since freeing is a cause for removing the right to intercourse, the word 'freeing' can be used metaphorically for the effect—the removal of that right. When the husband intends divorce, he is using the cause to express the effect.
Isti'ara here is a transfer from literal to figurative meaning based on causation.
4
Address the objection about revocability
One might object: if it's a metaphor for divorce, the divorce should be revocable (رجعي) like a clear pronouncement. The response: the metaphor is not for the word 'divorce' but for the underlying meaning—absolute removal of the right to intercourse—which results in an irrevocable divorce (بائن).
The intent is to sever the marriage permanently, so the divorce is ba'in.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, when a man says 'حررتك' intending divorce, the divorce is valid and irrevocable (ba'in) according to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قال لامرأته: «حرّرتك» ونوى به الطلاق يصح؛ لأن التحرير بحقيقته يوجب زوال ملك الرقبة بواسطة زوال ملك الرقبه فكان سببا محضا لزوال ملك المتعة فجاز أن يستعار عن الطلاق الذي هو مزيل لملك المتعة بطريق ذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب. ولا يقال: لو جعل مجازا عن الطلاق لوجب أن يكون الطلاق الواقع به رجعيا بل هو بائن؛ لأن المسبب ليس هو الطلاق الصريح حتى يقع به الرجعي بل زوال ملك المتعة فيكون استعارة عن معنى المزيل.
And if he says to his wife, 'I freed you' and intends divorce, it is valid because freeing in its literal sense necessitates removal of ownership of the right of possession (raqaba) and through that removal necessitates removal of ownership of the right of intercourse (bud'), so it is a pure cause for the removal of ownership of the right of intercourse. Thus it is permissible to use it metaphorically for divorce, which removes ownership of the right of intercourse, by way of mentioning the cause and intending the effect. And it cannot be said: if it is made as a metaphor for divorce, the divorce falling by it would be revocable (raj'i), but it is irrevocable (ba'in). Because the effect is not the explicit divorce that would make it revocable, but rather the removal of ownership of the right of intercourse, so it is a metaphor for the meaning of the remover.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of isti'ara by applying it to a specific fiqh example: a husband saying to his wife 'I freed you' (حررتك) intending divorce. The author argues that this is a valid isti'ara because the literal meaning of 'freeing' (tahrir) is the removal of ownership of the slave (raqaba), which indirectly causes removal of the husband's right to sexual access (milk al-bud'). Since the effect (removal of the right of intercourse) is what happens in divorce, it is permissible to use the cause (freeing) to refer to the effect (divorce). The author addresses a potential objection: if 'freeing' is a metaphor for divorce, then the divorce should be revocable (raj'i) like a clear pronouncement of divorce. But the response is that the metaphor is not for the word 'divorce' itself but for the underlying meaning—the removal of the right of intercourse—which in this context results in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in). This is because the husband's intent is to sever the marriage bond completely, not to initiate a revocable divorce. Thus, the ruling is that it is an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) when the husband so intends.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metonymic usage where the cause is mentioned to refer to the effect, due to their connection.
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return during the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife 'I freed you' intending divorce, what type of divorce occurs according to this passage?
A) Revocable divorce
B) Irrevocable divorce
C) No divorce
D) It depends on the wife's response

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, isti'ara allows a cause to denote its effect, so 'I freed you' intended as divorce yields a ba'in divorce.

Abandoning Haqiqah by Speaker's Context
States a usul rule with Quranic and fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that haqiqah is abandoned when the speaker's attributes (such as wisdom) contradict the literal meaning, and distinguish this from arbitrary reinterpretation.

Core Idea
Speaker's context overrides literal meaning when the attribute contradicts the apparent command.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi usul principle: the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word can be set aside when the speaker's context provides a clear indication that the literal sense is not intended. The passage uses a powerful Quranic example—Allah's statement 'let him disbelieve'—which appears to command disbelief, but since Allah is wise and cannot command evil, the literal imperative is reinterpreted as a threat. The same logic applies in fiqh, such as determining the scope of an agent's authority based on the principal's situation. Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to derive rulings from texts by considering the speaker's attributes.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the core rule: haqiqah may be abandoned due to a dalalah from the speaker's context (qibal al-mutakallim). This means the speaker's known attributes (e.g., wisdom, justice) can override the apparent literal meaning of his words.
The rule is about abandoning haqiqah, not about rejecting the text entirely.
2
Analyze the Quranic example
Read the verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' The literal imperative (li-yakfur) appears to command disbelief. But because the speaker is Allah, who is wise and does not command evil, the imperative is reinterpreted as a threat (tawbikh) or permission, not a command.
The dalala is 'zanni' (speculative) here—the verse itself is qat'i in transmission, but the meaning is open to interpretation.
3
Identify the applied fiqh example
Consider the case of an agent appointed to buy meat. If the principal is a traveler stopping on the road, his context (need for immediate food) indicates he means cooked/roasted meat. If he is a householder, his context indicates raw meat for cooking. The literal word 'meat' (lahm) is general, but the speaker's context restricts it.
This shows the same principle working in mundane legal transactions.
4
Derive the usul principle
The principle is: when the speaker's attribute (e.g., wisdom) makes the literal meaning impossible or inappropriate, the word is interpreted in a way consistent with that attribute. This is distinct from arbitrary interpretation; it is a reasoned departure based on a contextual indicator.
Hanafi usul calls this 'dalalah min qibal al-mutakallim'—a type of contextual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يترك به حقائق الألفاظ الرابع: قد تترك الحقيقة بدلالة من قبل المتكلم، مثالـه قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن شَاء فَلْيُؤْمِن وَمَن شَاء فَلْيَكْفُرْ﴾ [الكهف: ٢٩]؛ وذلك لأنّ الله تعالى حكيم والكفر قبيح والحكيم لا يأمر به، فيترك دلالة اللفظ على الأمر بحكمة الأمر، وعلى هذا قلنا: إذا وكل بشراء اللحم فإن كان مسافرا نزل على الطريق فهو على المطبوخ أو المشوي، وإن كان صاحب منزل فهو على النيّ.
Chapter on how literal meanings are abandoned. Fourth: The literal meaning may be abandoned by indication from the speaker's context. Example: His statement, exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' [Al-Kahf 29]; that is because Allah is wise and disbelief is ugly, and the wise does not command ugliness, so the literal indication of the word is abandoned for the command of wisdom. And based on this we said: If someone appoints an agent to buy meat, if he is a traveler alighting on the road, then it is [interpreted as] cooked or roasted; if he is a householder, then it is raw.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental usul rule: the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word may be set aside due to an indication from the speaker's context. The author begins by stating the rule, then supports it with a Quranic example: Allah's words 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' appear to command disbelief, but since Allah is wise and cannot command evil, the literal imperative is abandoned in favor of a threatening or permissive meaning. This is not arbitrary; it is driven by the attribute of the speaker (hukm al-hakim). The author then applies the same principle to a fiqh example: an agent appointed to buy meat. If the principal is a traveler stopping on the road, his situation indicates he needs ready-to-eat meat (cooked or roasted); if he is at home, his situation indicates raw meat for cooking. Thus, the agent's authority is restricted by the inferred intention, not the literal words of the agency.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as established by linguistic convention.
بدلالة من قبل المتكلم (bi-dalālat min qibal al-mutakallim): An indication arising from the speaker's attributes (e.g., wisdom, justice) that overrides the literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is abrogated by another verse
B) Because Allah is wise and does not command evil
C) The imperative form is not genuine
D) It is a metaphorical expression

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning can be abandoned when the speaker's known attributes (like wisdom) contradict the apparent meaning, as seen in the Quranic example of 'let him disbelieve' and the fiqh case of agency for meat.

Fifth Way: Abandoning Haqiqah by Context of Speech
Rule statement with two illustrative examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the context of speech (mahall al-kalam) restricts literal meaning and apply this rule to oaths and conditional statements in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Context can override literal meaning: an oath or condition is limited to the immediate situation indicated by the speech context.

In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a statement is not always absolute. One of the ways it is abandoned is by the context in which the speech occurs (mahall al-kalam). This lesson examines that rule and its application to two concrete fiqh examples: an oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr) and a husband's conditional divorce. Understanding this principle sharpens your ability to interpret legal speech accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The author states: 'The literal meaning may be abandoned by indication of the context of the speech.' This is the fifth method. Recognize that context (mahall) is a valid indicator (dalil) that restricts the scope of words.
This is a usul rule, not a fiqh ruling itself.
2
Analyze the first example – oath of immediacy
Consider the invitation scenario: someone is invited to a specific midday meal and swears not to eat. The context shows the oath is limited to that invitation, not all meals. Therefore, eating later elsewhere does not break the oath.
This is called yamin al-fawr – the oath is tied to the immediate situation.
3
Analyze the second example – conditional divorce
A wife stands up to leave; the husband says, 'If you go out, you are divorced.' The context – her immediate intention to go out – limits the condition to that moment. A later departure does not trigger divorce.
The husband's words are interpreted based on the immediate situation, not as a general condition.
4
Extract the underlying principle
Both examples show that the literal generality of words (e.g., 'not eat' or 'go out') is narrowed by the context. The principle: mahall al-kalam acts as a qarinah (contextual clue) that overrides the apparent literal meaning.
This principle is distinct from other ways of abandoning haqiqah, such as by shari'ah or custom.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
To test understanding, consider if someone says, 'I will not give charity' while being asked for a specific donation. The context restricts the oath to that request; giving charity elsewhere does not break the oath.
The context must be clear and immediate for the rule to apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس: قد تترك الحقيقة بدلالة محل الكلام، ومن هذا النوع يمين الفور مثاله إذا قال: تعال تغد معى فقال: والله لا أتغدي ينصرف ذلك إلى الغداء المدعو إليه حتى لو تغدى بعد ذلك في منزله معه أو مع غيره في ذلك اليوم لا يحنث، وكذا إذا قامت المرأة تريد الخروج فقال الزوج إن خرجت فأنت كذا كان الحكم مقصورا على الحال، لو خرجت بعد ذلك لا يحنث.
And the fifth: The literal meaning may be abandoned by indication of the context of the speech. From this type is the oath of immediacy (yamin al-fawr). Example: If one says, 'Come eat the midday meal with me,' and he says, 'By Allah, I will not eat the midday meal,' this is restricted to the midday meal to which he was invited, so even if he eats the midday meal later in his own home with him or with someone else on that day, he does not break his oath. Similarly, if a woman stands up wanting to go out, and her husband says, 'If you go out, you are such-and-such,' the ruling is limited to that moment; if she goes out afterwards, she does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth way in which the literal meaning (haqiqah) is abandoned: by the context of the speech (mahall al-kalam). The author first names the rule, then provides two illustrative fiqh examples. The first is the 'oath of immediacy' (yamin al-fawr). When a person is invited to a specific meal and swears 'I will not eat the midday meal,' the context shows his refusal is only for that particular invitation, not for all meals. Hence, if he eats later elsewhere, he does not violate his oath. The second example involves a husband's conditional divorce. If a woman signals her intention to go out immediately, and the husband says 'If you go out, you are divorced,' the context restricts the condition to that immediate departure; a later departure does not trigger divorce. In both cases, the literal generality of the words is abandoned because the situation (mahall) dictates a specific, restricted meaning.

Key Terms
محل الكلام (mahall al-kalam): The context or situation in which speech occurs; it provides an indication that restricts the literal meaning.
يمين الفور (yamin al-fawr): An oath that is understood to apply only to the immediate situation due to the context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the woman wanting to go out, why is the husband's conditional statement limited to that moment?
A) Because the husband's anger was immediate
B) Because the context indicates she intended to leave right then
C) Because the woman did not actually go out
D) Because the husband's words were ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Context of speech (mahall al-kalam) is a valid indicator that restricts the literal meaning of oaths and conditions to the immediate situation.

Abandoning Literal Meanings: Haqiqa and Majaz in Hanafi Usul
States the principle of abandoning literal meanings with examples and a dispute on majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a literal meaning (haqiqa) must be abandoned in favor of a metaphorical one (majaz), apply the Hanafi rule that majaz is a substitute in the word, and analyze the hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi-n-niyyat' using this principle.

Core Idea
When the subject cannot bear the literal meaning, the word must be taken metaphorically.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi usul: if the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word is impossible given the subject (mahall), we must turn to a metaphorical (majazi) interpretation. The passage from Usul al-Shashi illustrates this with examples like 'I sold myself' (for marriage) and 'This is my son' (for freeing a slave). It then delves into a key dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding whether majaz is a substitute in the word or in the ruling, and concludes with a detailed analysis of the famous hadith on intentions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of abandoning haqiqa
The text states: when the subject (mahall) does not accept the literal meaning, we leave the literal sense. Identify the trigger: impossibility of literal interpretation.
The examples make this concrete: a free woman cannot be sold literally, so 'sale' means marriage.
2
Understand the examples
The passage gives two examples: (1) a free woman saying 'I sold myself to you' is a valid marriage contract; (2) saying 'This is my son' to a slave known to be another's child or older than the master is a metaphor for freeing. Explain why the literal is impossible in each case.
For the slave example, note that claiming paternity where it is impossible (known lineage or age) forces a metaphorical reading.
3
Analyze the dispute on the nature of majaz
Abu Hanifa (ra) holds that majaz is a substitute for haqiqa in the word itself (lafz). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it is a substitute in the ruling (hukm). Understand the consequence: for Abu Hanifa, the ruling is derived from the metaphorical use; for the companions, the metaphorical use depends on the validity of the ruling.
The text says 'khilafan lahu' meaning contrary to Abu Hanifa. The basis is mentioned: 'based on what we mentioned that metaphor is a successor to the literal meaning in terms of the word according to him, and in terms of the ruling according to them.'
4
Apply the principle to the hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi-n-niyyat'
The literal meaning would imply that no action occurs without intention, which is false. Therefore, the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically as referring to 'reward' or 'judgment' of actions by intention. Walk through the reasoning: if 'reward' is intended, it does not affect worldly validity; if 'judgment' is intended, it can be split into worldly (validity) and otherworldly (reward). By consensus, otherworldly judgment is intended, so the hadith does not obligate intention for worldly acts like wudu.
This shows the practical importance of the principle: it prevents over-extending the hadith to make intention obligatory in all acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يترك به حقائق الألفاظ كقولنا بعت نفسي منك. كان المحل لا يقبل حقيقة اللفظ، ومثاله انعقاد نكاح الحرة بلفظ البيع والهبة والتمليك والصدقة، وقوله لعبده وهو معروف النسب من غيره: «هذا ابني» وكذا إذا قال لعبده وهو أكبر سناً من المولى: «هذا إبني» كان مجازا عن العتق فمعناه هذا معتقي. عند أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه خلافا لهما بناء على ما ذكرنا أنّ المجاز خلف عن الحقيقة في حق اللفظ عنده وفي حق الحكم عندهما. فيشترط صحة الحكم في التجوز. إلى المجاز لا محالة، كقوله عليه السلام: «إِنَّمَا الأَعْمَالُ بِالنِّيَّاتِ»، فإن معناه الحقيقي أن لا توجد أعمال الجوارح إلا بالنية وهو كذب؛ لأنّ أكثر ما يقع العمل منا في وقت خلو الذهن عن النية، فلا بد أن يحمل على المجاز أي: ثواب الأعمال أو حكم الأعمال بالنيات، فإن قدر «الثواب» فظاهر أنه لا يدلّ على أن جواز الأعمال في الدنيا موقوف على النية، وإن قدر «الحكم» فهو نوعان: دنيوي كالصحة وأخروي كالثواب والعقاب، والأخروي مراد بالإجماع بيننا وبين الشافعي رحمه الله، فلا يجوز أن يراد الدنيوي أيضاً إما عنده فلأنه يلزم عموم المجاز وإما عندنا فلأنه يلزم عموم المشترك، فلا يدلّ على أن جواز العمل موقوف على النية، فلا تكون النية فرضاً في الوضوء، فتذكر.
Chapter on what causes the abandonment of literal meanings of words. Like our saying: 'I sold myself to you.' The subject cannot bear the literal meaning. Its example is the validity of marriage of a free woman with the word 'sale', 'gift', 'transfer of ownership', and 'charity', and his saying to his slave who is known to be the child of another: 'This is my son,' and likewise if he says to his slave who is older than the master: 'This is my son,' it is a metaphor for freeing, so its meaning is 'this is my freedman.' According to Abu Hanifa - may Allah be pleased with him - contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), based on what we mentioned that metaphor is a successor to the literal meaning in terms of the word according to him, and in terms of the ruling according to them. So validity of the ruling is required in using metaphor. Necessarily leading to metaphor, like his saying peace be upon him: 'Indeed actions are by intentions.' Its literal meaning is that actions of the limbs do not occur except with intention, which is false because most actions occur when the mind is free from intention. So it must be interpreted metaphorically, i.e., reward of actions or judgment of actions by intentions. If 'reward' is intended, it is apparent that it does not indicate that the permissibility of actions in this world is dependent on intention. If 'judgment' is intended, it is of two types: worldly like validity, and otherworldly like reward and punishment. The otherworldly is intended by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. So it is not allowed that the worldly also be intended, either according to him because it would necessitate universal metaphor, or according to us because it would necessitate universal homonymy. So it does not indicate that the permissibility of action is dependent on intention, so intention is not obligatory in wudu. Reflect.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains the principle of abandoning literal (haqiqi) meanings when the subject (mahall) cannot bear the literal interpretation. The chapter begins with the rule: if the subject does not accept the literal sense of the word, the word is taken metaphorically (majaz). Examples follow: a free woman cannot literally be sold, so saying 'I sold myself to you' is a metaphor for marriage; similarly, 'This is my son' said to a slave who is known to be another's child or is older is a metaphor for freeing the slave. The passage then records a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the nature of majaz. Abu Hanifa holds that majaz is a substitute for haqiqa in the word itself (i.e., the word is used metaphorically), while the two companions hold that it is a substitute in terms of the ruling (i.e., the metaphorical meaning is the intended legal effect). Consequently, Abu Hanifa requires that the metaphorical application be valid in terms of the ruling. The passage then provides a detailed analysis of the famous hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi-n-niyyat' as a case where metaphorical interpretation is necessary. The literal reading would be false because actions often occur without concurrent intention. Therefore, the hadith must be taken as referring to 'reward' or 'judgment' by intention, and the author shows that it cannot imply that intention is obligatory for the validity of every action like wudu.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning of a word used as originally coined.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning used when literal is impossible; it is a substitute (khalaf) for haqiqa.
محل (mahall): The subject or locus of the word; the entity to which the word is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Usul al-Shashi, when is it necessary to abandon the literal meaning of a word?
A) When the word is ambiguous.
B) When the literal meaning is too broad.
C) When the subject (mahall) cannot bear the literal meaning.
D) When the speaker is uncertain.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the subject cannot bear the literal meaning of a word, it must be interpreted metaphorically, as shown by the examples of marriage by sale and the hadith on intentions.

The Difference Between Divine and Human Command Based on Ownership
Stating the difference in absolute command based on ownership and power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi usul principle that God's absolute ownership makes His command more binding than any human authority, and reconstruct the a fortiori (qiyas awla) argument used to support it.

Core Idea
God’s absolute ownership makes His command categorically more binding than any human authority's command.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi addresses a foundational usul point: the nature of absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq). The author distinguishes between the command of Allah, who has complete ownership and power over all creation, and the command of a human master, who has only limited ownership. This distinction has implications for the weight of obligation and the severity of disobedience. The commentary (hashiya) refutes a philosophical objection about creation from nothing and explains a key term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Principle
Recognize that the passage sets forth a principle: the binding force of a command varies with the commander's ownership and power. The absolute command of Allah is more binding than any human command because Allah's ownership is complete and unlimited.
2
Trace the A Fortiori Analogy
Work through the author's reasoning: if a human master with limited ownership (e.g., a slave owner) can justly punish disobedience, then the One who created you from nothing and has complete ownership deserves even greater obedience and imposes harsher consequences for disobedience.
This is qiyas awla (a fortiori analogy), which is valid in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the Hashiya's Philosophical Refutation
Read the commentator's response to the objection: how can something be created from nothing? The answer is that creation from nothing means the act of bringing into existence coincides with the thing in time but is prior in essence. This defends the concept of divine creation ex nihilo.
This objection is from philosophy; its refutation preserves the doctrine of creation.
4
Note the Lexical Clarification
Understand the term 'shawayib al-ni'am' as 'first rains of blessings' – a metaphor for the foremost and most excellent favors, emphasizing the enormity of God's generosity.
This highlights the magnitude of ingratitude in disobedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في اختلاف الناس في الأمر المطلق من حيث الخلق والقدرة الكاملة. ولاية الآمر ، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن الله تعالى ملكا كاملا في كل جزء من أجزاء العالم وله التصرف كيف ما شاء وأراد ، وإذا ثبت أن من له الملك القاصر في العبد كان ترك الايتمار سببا للعقاب فما ظنك في ترك أمر من أوجدك من العدم وأدر عليك شآبيب النعم . (۱) قوله: [أوجدك] أي: أنشأك وخلقك وأخرجك من ظلمة العدَم ، ولقائل أن يقول: إن الإيجاد من العدم لا يخلو إما أن يكون حالة الوجود أو حالة العدَم على التقدير الأوّل يلزم إيجاد الموجود وهي محال، وعلى التقدير الثاني يلزم الجمع بين الضدين، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه : بأنّ المراد من الإيجاد حالة الوجود، والمحال إيجاد الموجود بوجود حاصل قبل الإيجاد، وهو غير لازم غاية الأمر أن إيجاده يقارن الموجـــــود في الزمان، وهذا لا ينافي الإيجاد متقدماً على الموجود في الذات، كذا قيل. (۲) قوله: [شآبيب النعم] الشآبيب جمع شؤبوب وهو أوّل المطر :أي: أوّل النعم وأشرفها، كذا في "المعدن".
A chapter on the disagreement of people regarding the absolute command, in terms of creation and complete power. The authority of the commander: when this is established, we say: Indeed Allah has complete ownership in every part of the parts of the world and He has disposal however He wills and intends. And when it is established that the one who has limited ownership in the servant, leaving compliance with his command was a cause of punishment, then what is your opinion regarding leaving the command of the One who created you from non-existence and poured upon you the showers of blessings. (1) His statement: [أوجدك] i.e., He brought you into existence, created you, and brought you out of the darkness of nonexistence. And it may be said: bringing into existence from non-existence is either a state of existence or a state of non-existence. On the first assumption, it necessitates bringing into existence something that already exists, which is impossible. On the second assumption, it necessitates the combination of two opposites. It can be answered that what is meant by bringing into existence is the state of existence, and the impossible is bringing into existence something that already exists with an existence preceding the bringing into existence. This is not necessary; at most, His bringing into existence coincides with the thing in time, and this does not negate that the bringing into existence is prior to the thing in essence. This is what is said. (2) His statement: [شآبيب النعم] 'Shawayib' is the plural of 'shu'bub' which is the first of rain, i.e., the first of blessings and the most noble. This is from "al-Ma'dan".
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is a ruling passage (usul rule) about the difference between the absolute command of Allah and the command of a human authority. The author, based on the matn, first establishes that Allah has complete ownership and power over all parts of the universe, and He acts as He wills. Then he reasons that if a human master with limited ownership (like a slave owner) can make disobedience a cause of punishment, then how much more severe is the punishment for disobeying the command of Allah who created you from nothing and gave you all blessings. The commentary (hashiya) then explains two points: (1) The philosophical objection about creation from nothing—whether it is creation of something existing or non-existing—and refutes it by stating that creation from nothing means the act of bringing into existence coincides with the thing in time while being prior in essence. (2) The word 'shawayib' means the first of rain, indicating the first and most excellent of blessings.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): The command considered in its absolute sense, without qualification by condition or description, as applied to the command of Allah or a human authority.
الملك القاصر (al-milk al-qasir): Limited or deficient ownership, such as a human's ownership of a slave, which is not absolute like God's ownership.
شآبيب النعم (shawayib al-ni'am): The first and most excellent showers of blessings, like the first rain, indicating the choicest favors from Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because Allah's command is more difficult to obey
B) Because Allah has complete ownership and created man from nothing, while a human master has only limited ownership
C) Because Allah threatens more punishment in the Hereafter
D) Because human masters are not real owners

Key Takeaway
The absolute command of Allah carries greater binding force than any human command because of His complete ownership and creative power, established through an a fortiori rational argument.
