Usurpation Transformations: When Ownership Transfers and When It Does Not
Explaining when the usurper becomes owner of the changed property and the types of compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on ownership transfer in ghasp and apply the usul principle that rulings change with change of description.

Core Idea
When a usurped item is completely transformed, the usurper becomes owner; if the name and essential benefit remain, the owner's right continues.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi presents a classic Hanafi fiqh ruling with an embedded usul principle: 'Rulings change with change of description.' The author systematically lists cases where the usurper becomes owner due to complete transformation, and notable exceptions where the original owner's right persists. We will examine the reasoning, the Shafi'i counterargument, and the types of compensation that arise. This is a perfect illustration of how usul principles directly shape substantive fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the main rule
Identify the cases where the usurper becomes owner: grinding wheat, building a house with a beam, slaughtering a sheep, squeezing grapes, planting wheat that grows. The reason given: 'the original substance does not remain, and rulings change with change of description.'
The usul principle is 'الأحكام تتبدل بتبدل الأوصاف'.
2
Identify the exceptions
Note the cases in Zahir al-Riwayah where the owner's right does not cease: striking silver into dirhams, making gold nuggets into dinars, slaughtering a sheep (if the name remains), spinning cotton, weaving yarn. The key is that the name and essential benefit are preserved.
This shows that not every change triggers ownership transfer; the change must be comprehensive.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if the original property (e.g., a slave) appears after compensation, ownership reverts. The Hanafi response: ownership does not revert because it is 'payment after adjudication' (الأداء بعد القضاء). This reflects a difference in usul: priority of the usurper's act of transformation vs. the original ownership.
The practical consequence (الثمرة) is that in the Hanafi view, compensation finalizes the matter.
4
Distinguish two types of compensation
Complete compensation (كامل) applies to fungibles (مثليات): exact like-for-like. Deficient compensation (قاصر) applies to non-fungibles (قيميات): payment of value because exact replacement is impossible. This distinction derives from the nature of the obligation.
This explains why for a consumed sheep, value is paid, but for wheat, the same amount of wheat is owed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا لو غصب حنطة فطحنها أو ساجة فبنى عليها دارا أو شاة فذبحها أو عنبا فعصرها أو حنطة فزرعها ونبت الزرع كان ذلك ملكا للغاصب لأنه لم يبق عين المغصوب ويتبدل الأحكام بتبدل الوصف. وقلنا: جميعها للغاصب ويجب عليه رد القيمة. ولو غصب فضة فضربها دراهم أو تبرا فاتخذها دنانير أو شاة فذبحها لا ينقطع حق المالك في ظاهر الرواية، وكذلك لو غصب قطنا فغزله أو غزلا فنسجه لا ينقطع حق المالك في ظاهر الرواية، ويتفرع من هذا مسألة المضمونات. ولذا قال الشافعي رحمه الله: لو ظهر العبد المغصوب بعد ما أخذ المالك ضمانه من الغاصب كان العبد ملكا للمالك والواجب على المالك رد ما أخذ من قيمة العبد، وعندنا لا يعود ملكه بعد ملكه للغاصب لأنه الأداء بعد القضاء. وأما القضاء فنوعان: كامل وقاصر فالكامل منه تسليم مثل الواجب صورة ومعنى كمن غصب قفيز حنطة فاستهلكها ضمن قفيز حنطة ويكون المؤدى في النوع والصفة وفي التقوم وهو المماثل المعنوي. وأما القاصر فهو تسليم ما لا يماثل الواجب صورة ويماثل معنى كمن غصب شاة فهلكت.
And upon this, if he usurps wheat and grinds it, or a beam and builds a house upon it, or a sheep and slaughters it, or grapes and squeezes them, or wheat and plants it and it grows, that becomes ownership of the usurper because the substance of the usurped object does not remain, and rulings change with change of description. And we say: all of these belong to the usurper and he must pay the value. And if he usurps silver and strikes it into dirhams, or gold nuggets and makes them into dinars, or a sheep and slaughters it, the right of the owner does not cease in the apparent narration. Similarly, if he usurps cotton and spins it, or yarn and weaves it, the right of the owner does not cease in the apparent narration. And from this branches the issue of guaranteed items. Thus al-Shafi'i said: if the usurped slave appears after the owner has taken compensation from the usurper, the slave is the owner's property and the owner must return what he took of the slave's value. And according to us, his ownership does not return after it has become the usurper's ownership because it is payment after adjudication. And adjudication is of two types: complete and deficient. The complete is delivery of the like of the obligatory in form and meaning, like one who usurps a qafiz of wheat and consumes it, he is liable for a qafiz of wheat, and what is delivered is in type and quality and in value, which is the conceptual similitude. As for the deficient, it is delivery of that which does not resemble the obligatory in form but resembles in meaning, like one who usurps a sheep and it perishes.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on usurpation (ghasp) when the usurper transforms the property. The author states that if the usurper changes the property so completely that the original name and greatest benefits are lost (e.g., grinding wheat into flour, building with a beam, slaughtering a sheep, squeezing grapes into juice, or planting wheat that grows), the new product becomes the property of the usurper, and he must pay the value to the original owner. The reasoning is that the original substance is no longer present, and legal rulings change with change of description. This is the Hanafi position. However, there are exceptions: if the property is gold or silver and it is merely shaped into coins, or if a sheep is slaughtered but retains its name (still called 'sheep'), or if cotton is spun but retains its name (still 'cotton thread'), the owner's right does not cease in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). The basis for this distinction is that in these cases, the material remains and the name is not lost; the primary benefit (being a medium of exchange for gold/silver, or the usability of wool/clothing) is still present. The passage then contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's view, who holds that if the owner receives compensation for the usurped property and later the original property (e.g., a slave) appears, the owner's ownership is restored and he must return the compensation. The Hanafi school rejects this, stating that once the usurper's ownership is established through transformation or compensation after a court ruling, the original owner's right does not return. Finally, the passage discusses two types of compensation: complete (paying exactly the same item in kind) for fungible goods (mithliyyat) like wheat, and deficient (paying value) for non-fungible goods (qimiyyat) like sheep, because exact resemblance is impossible. This is a characteristic Hanafi approach that balances the rights of both parties with practical considerations.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation: taking another's property without right and without permission.
عين المغصوب (ayn al-maghsub): The specific substance of the usurped property; if it perishes or changes completely, the usurper becomes owner.
مثليات (mithliyyat): Fungible goods that have exact counterparts in type and quality, like wheat, barley, gold, silver; compensation is by like item.
قيميات (qimiyyat): Non-fungible goods without exact counterparts; compensation is by market value.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The 'apparent narration' — the authoritative transmitted opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani in six core books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which of the following cases does the owner's right NOT cease in Zahir al-Riwayah?
A) Usurping wheat and grinding it into flour.
B) Usurping a sheep and roasting it whole.
C) Usurping silver and striking it into dirhams.
D) Usurping a beam and building it into a house.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the usul principle 'rulings change with change of description' to determine when a usurper becomes owner, with complete transformation transferring ownership and mere change preserving the original owner's right.

The Waw Dispute: Absolute Conjunction or Order?
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on waw's function in legal language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school interprets waw as absolute conjunction rather than order, and distinguish between its literal use as conjunction and its metaphorical use as a circumstantial accusative.

Core Idea
Waw means absolute conjunction; order is not inherent. The Shafi'i hadith is a practical directive, not a linguistic rule.

In legal texts, the humble conjunction waw (و) can change the entire meaning of a condition. This lesson examines the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute: does waw imply sequential order or mere connection? We'll explore how the Hanafi school holds that waw is for absolute conjunction, while Imam al-Shafi'i interprets it as indicating order. The practical stake: rulings on wudu order and conditional divorces hang on this linguistic choice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that waw (و) indicates absolute conjunction (jama' mutlaq), meaning it connects items without implying order or simultaneity. The condition is fulfilled regardless of sequence.
Example: 'If you speak to Zayd and Amr' – speaking to either first still triggers the condition.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that waw indicates order (tartib). His proof is the hadith 'Start with what Allah started with' regarding Safa and Marwa, which he interprets as a textual indication that the waw in the Quranic verse implies order.
The Shafi'i school also uses this principle to require sequential order in wudu.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's command is about the ritual practice of sa'y, not about the linguistic meaning of waw. The verse only affirms that Safa and Marwa are symbols of Allah; order is not conceivable in that context. The Hanafis rely on the consensus of Arabic linguists that waw is for absolute conjunction.
The Hanafi argument is supported by examples where order is clearly irrelevant, e.g., 'Zayd and Amr came' – they could have come together or separately.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference appears in wudu: the Shafi'i school requires washing face, then hands, etc., in order because the verse uses waw. The Hanafi school does not require order beyond the stated sequence (face, hands, etc.) but considers order to be established by the Prophet's practice, not by waw. In conditional divorces, Hanafis do not require sequence.
Another consequence: in the phrase 'If you enter the house and you are divorced,' the Hanafis interpret 'and' as immediate divorce because it cannot be a condition (the consequence is missing).
5
Examine waw as circumstantial accusative
When literal conjunction (عطف) is impossible, waw can be interpreted metaphorically as a circumstantial accusative (حال), introducing a condition. This requires two conditions: (1) the speech must be capable of that meaning, and (2) evidence must show that literal conjunction is impossible.
Example: 'Pay me a thousand and you are free' – a master cannot impose a debt on his own slave, so the waw must be for hal, meaning 'if you pay, you are free'.

Therefore, when the waw is taken as circumstantial accusative, it is necessary that the speech accept the meaning of the circumstantial accusative, i.e., that it be simultaneous with its owner, and it is necessary that there be evidence of the impossibility of conjunction and the determination of the circumstantial accusative. That is established because the waw in his saying 'Pay me a thousand and you are free' if it were for conjunction, that would be imposing wealth upon the slave, and that is not for the master with the existence of slavery in him, so conjunction is impossible and the circumstantial accusative is determined; understand this, as in al-Fusul. 12 Assembly: 'al-Madinah al-Ilmiyyah' (al-Da'wah al-Islamiyyah) (1ε⋅) —

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Arabic particle 'waw' (و) as used in legal language, extracted from the text of Usul al-Shashi with its commentary. It addresses two main issues: the primary meaning of waw in conjunction (عطف) and its secondary use as a circumstantial accusative (حال). The central dispute is between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether waw inherently indicates order (ترتيب) or mere conjunction (جمع مطلق). The author begins by stating the Shafi'i position: waw indicates order, citing the Prophet's command 'Start with what Allah started with' regarding Safa and Marwa, which is interpreted as a textual proof for order. The Hanafis respond by asserting that waw is for absolute conjunction, as established by Arabic linguists, and that the Prophet's instruction was about practical order in the ritual, not about the linguistic meaning of waw. They provide examples: if a husband says 'If you speak to Zayd and Amr, you are divorced,' the divorce occurs whether she speaks to them in order or simultaneously; similarly, entering two houses in any order triggers the condition. They also bring a case from Imam Muhammad: if a man says 'If you enter the house and you are divorced,' the divorce occurs immediately, which would not happen if waw implied order. The passage then turns to another usage of waw: when it functions as a circumstantial accusative (حال), indicating that the following clause is a condition for the preceding. This is a metaphorical use, requiring a contextual clue that the literal meaning (conjunction) is impossible. Examples include a master telling a slave 'Pay me a thousand and you are free' (interpreted as 'if you pay me a thousand, you are free') and an imam telling enemies 'Open the door and you are secure' (interpreted as 'if you open the door, you are secure'). The commentary explains why literal conjunction is impossible here: a master cannot impose a debt on his own slave, and security cannot be given without condition. Thus, the waw is necessarily taken as a circumstantial accusative, which introduces a condition. The passage concludes by noting that every metaphorical interpretation requires the word to be capable of that meaning and evidence that the literal meaning is impossible. Throughout, the author relies on linguistic authorities and early Hanafi sources such as al-Ma'din and al-Fusul.

Key Terms
الواو (al-wāw): The Arabic letter waw, used as a conjunction. In Hanafi usul, its primary meaning is absolute conjunction (الجمع المطلق), neither order nor simultaneity.
العطف (al-‘aṭf): Grammatical conjunction. The literal meaning of waw is conjunction. When conjunction leads to impossibility, waw may be interpreted as circumstantial accusative.
الحال (al-ḥāl): The circumstantial accusative. When waw is used for hal, it introduces a clause that is the condition or circumstance for the main action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife, 'If you speak to Zayd and Amr, you are divorced,' and she speaks to Amr first, then Zayd, what is the ruling?
A) She is not divorced because she did not speak to them in the order mentioned.
B) She is divorced because the waw does not require order.
C) She is divorced only if she speaks to both simultaneously.
D) The divorce is suspended until she speaks to Zayd first.

Key Takeaway
Waw (و) in Hanafi usul is for absolute conjunction; order must be established by separate evidence, and when conjunction is impossible, waw can indicate a condition via metaphor.

intermediate

Waw al-Hal: When the Particle Indicates Condition
Usul al-Shashi on waw al-hal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a waw in a legal utterance is interpreted as hal (circumstance) and distinguish it from literal conjunction using the Hanafi conditions of impossibility and evidence.

Core Idea
When literal conjunction with waw is legally impossible, it shifts to indicate a condition (shart).

The particle waw ('and') typically functions as a conjunction. However, in certain legal contexts, the Hanafi school recognizes that waw can metaphorically indicate a concurrent circumstance (hal), which effectively turns the first clause into a condition for the second. This lesson unpacks the two conditions for this interpretation and walks through classic examples from the Hanafi corpus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the waw and the two clauses
In a given legal utterance, locate the waw and the two statements it connects. For example, 'Give me a thousand and you are free' – the waw connects 'give' and 'you are free'.
2
Check if literal conjunction is possible
Ask: can the two clauses be true simultaneously without one being a condition? In the slave example, literal conjunction would mean the slave pays and is free concurrently, but legally the slave cannot owe a debt to his master. So conjunction is impossible.
Legal impossibility arises from the slave's lack of capacity to owe money.
3
Verify wording can admit hal and evidence exists
The wording must allow a circumstantial reading (i.e., the second clause describes a concurrent state), and there must be evidence (dalil) that literal meaning is not intended. Here, the master cannot obligate payment from his own property, proving the waw must indicate hal.
Evidence can be rational or legal impossibility.
4
Conclude that waw indicates hal and thus condition
When both conditions hold, the waw is interpreted metaphorically as hal, making the first clause a condition for the second. Thus 'Give me a thousand and you are free' means 'If you give me a thousand, you become free'.
The waw now conveys shart (condition).
5
Contrast with cases where conjunction is valid
In 'You are divorced and you are sick', there is no legal impossibility: the wife can be both divorced and sick simultaneously. So the waw remains literal conjunction, and divorce is effective immediately, even if the husband intended suspension.
Intention cannot override the default literal meaning without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تقرير حروف المعاني
الواو
وقد يكون «الواو» للحال فتجمع بين الحال وذي الحال، وحينئذ تفيد معنى الشرط، مثاله ما قال في المأذون إذا قال لعبده: «أد إلي ألفا وأنت حر» يكون الأداء شرطا للحرية، وقال محمد في "السير الكبير: إذا قال الإمام للكفار «افتحوا الباب وأنتم آمنون» لا يأمنون بدون الفتح، ولو قال للحربي: «إنزل وأنت آمن» لا يأمن بدون النزول، وإنّما تحمل الواو على الحال بطريق المجاز فلا بد من احتمال اللفظ ذلك وقيام الدلالة على ثبوته، كما في قوله المذكور للمولى لعبده: «أد إلى ألفا وأنت حر»، فإن الحرية يتحقق حال الأداء وقامت الدلالة على ذلك فإن المولى لا يستوجب على عبده مالا مع قيام الرق فيه أي على تعيين الحال.
وقد صح التعليق به فحمل عليه ولو قال: «أنت طالق وأنت مريضة أو أنت مصلية» تطلق في الحال ولو نوى التعليق لعدم الحالية صحة العطف.
Chapter on clarifying the particles of meanings.
The waw.
And the waw may be for the state (hal), so it joins the state and the subject of the state, and then it conveys the meaning of condition. Its example is what was said in [the book] al-Ma'dhun: when he says to his slave: 'Give me a thousand and you are free', the giving becomes a condition for freedom. And Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: when the imam says to the disbelievers: 'Open the door and you are safe', they are not safe without opening. And if he says to a fighter: 'Descend and you are safe', he is not safe without descending. The waw is only taken as indicating the state (hal) by way of metaphor, so it is necessary for the wording to admit that meaning and for evidence to exist for its establishment, as in the aforementioned statement of the master to his slave: 'Give me a thousand and you are free', because freedom is realized at the time of giving, and evidence for that exists because the master does not have the right to demand money from his slave while slavery exists. That is, [the evidence confirms] the specification of the state (hal).
And indeed suspension (ta'liq) is established by it, so [the waw] is interpreted accordingly. But if he says: 'You are divorced and you are sick, or you are praying', she is divorced immediately, even if he intended suspension, because there is no [evidence for] the state (hal) due to the validity of conjunction.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi discusses the waw of hal (state/circumstance). The author explains that waw can function metaphorically to indicate a concurrent circumstance, and when so used, it conveys the meaning of a condition (shart). He provides two key examples from Hanafi fiqh: first, a master's statement to his slave 'Give me a thousand and you are free' – the giving is a condition for freedom; second, from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir, a commander's statement to the enemy 'Open the door and you are safe' – safety depends on opening. The waw is interpreted as hal only when two conditions are met: (1) the wording can possibly bear the meaning of hal (i.e., the action and the outcome are concurrent), and (2) evidence exists that the literal meaning (conjunction) is impossible. In the slave example, the literal reading would obligate the slave to pay, but since the slave and his property belong to the master, such obligation is legally impossible – hence the waw must indicate hal, making the giving a condition for freedom. The passage then contrasts with statements like 'You are divorced and you are sick': here the waw is taken literally as conjunction, so the divorce is effective immediately, because nothing prevents the literal meaning; the wife's illness does not make conjunction impossible. Even if the husband intended suspension, it is not accepted without evidence. This distinction is crucial for legal interpretation of such utterances.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic particle waw, which can function for conjunction (عطف) or for circumstantial state (حال) metaphorically.
الحال (al-hal): A circumstance that is concurrent with the action of the subject of the state.
العطف (al-'atf): Grammatical conjunction where two elements are linked by the waw without implying condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the waw in 'A'dh ila alfan wa anta hurr' interpreted as hal?
A) Because the word 'hurr' is a state (hal).
B) Because the master cannot legally obligate the slave to pay since the slave's property belongs to the master.
C) Because the slave asked for a condition.
D) Because the waw always indicates hal in legal contexts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, waw shifts to indicate a condition only when literal conjunction is impossible and evidence supports the circumstantial reading.

Determining Waw as Ḥāl: The Slave Manumission Case
Definition of waw al-ḥāliyya through a legal example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that waw must be interpreted as ḥāl when conjunction leads to legal impossibility.

Core Idea
When waw as conjunction produces legal impossibility, Hanafi usul requires interpreting it as ḥāl to give the utterance meaningful effect.

This lesson examines a classic usul example: a master tells his slave 'Pay a thousand dirhams and you are free.' The question is whether the waw connects two separate commands or describes the state of freedom concurrent with payment. The Hanafi school uses the principle of avoiding impossibility to determine the meaning. This case illustrates a core method in Hanafi usul: when literal meaning leads to absurdity or legal nullity, the metaphorical meaning (majāz) is adopted.

Guided Path
1
State the case and identify the two clauses
The master's statement has two parts: 'Pay a thousand dirhams' and 'you are free.' Identify which word joins them (waw).
2
Recognize the two possible functions of waw
Waw can be conjunction (ʿāṭifa) linking two independent commands, or state (ḥāliyya) describing the circumstance of the subject.
Hanafi usul prioritizes the literal meaning (ḥaqīqat al-ʿaṭf) unless it leads to impossibility.
3
Test the conjunction interpretation for legal feasibility
If waw is conjunction, the master commands the slave to pay a debt. But a master cannot impose a debt on his slave because the slave's property belongs to the master—imposing a debt on oneself is impossible.
Legal basis: the master does not own a claim against his own property.
4
Apply the principle of avoiding impossibility
Since conjunction would render the statement legally invalid (the master cannot enforce a debt), that interpretation is impossible. Therefore, the waw must be interpreted as ḥāl.
The principle: 'When the literal meaning is impossible, resort to the metaphorical meaning (majāz).'
5
Derive the ruling
Freedom occurs simultaneous with payment. The slave becomes free at the moment of payment, not as a separate command.
This avoids the impossibility and gives the statement a valid legal effect.

أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي
فصل في تقرير حروف المعاني

المولى لعبده: «أد إلى ألفا وأنت حر»، فإن الحرية يتحقق حال الأداء وقامت الدلالة على ذلك فإن المولى لا يستوجب على عبده مالا مع قيام الرق فيه أي على تعيين الحال حتى لو أتلف من ماله شيئا لا يضمن. وقد صح التعليق به فحمل عليه ولو قال: «أنت طالق وأنت مريضة أو أنت مصلية» تطلق في الحال ولو نوى التعليق لعدم الحالية صحة العطف.

(1) قوله: [مع قيام الرق فيه] أي: في العبد يعني لو كان الواو للعطف كان هذا الكلام لإيجاب الألف على العبد ابتداء، و ليس للمولى ذلك مع قيام الرق فيه؛ لأنّ المولى لا يستوجب ديناً على عبده؛ لأنّ العبد وما في يده ملك المولى، فيكون إيجاب المولى عليه كإيجابه على نفسه والإيجاب على نفسه لا يمكن، فكذا الإيجاب على عبده، ولا يصلح أن يجعل ذلك ضريبة؛ لأنّ الضريبة لم يجز بهذا القدر عرفاً فتعدّر العطف، كذا في "المعدن".

(2) قوله: [تطلق في الحال... إلخ] لانتفاء الدليل على ثبوت الحال وتعدّر العطف؛ لأنّ ظاهر حال الزوج يشهد بأنه لا يطلّقها في حال مرضها؛ لأنّ المرض سبب التعطف والترحّم، فأمكن العمل بحقيقة العطف، فحمل عليه فتطلق في الحال فلا يكون الطلاق معلّقاً بالمرض والصلاة، وعورض بأنّ الزوج لا يستمتع بها في مرضها فربما يطلقها في هذه الحالة تضرراً أو توحّشاً منها، فينبغي أن يكون «الواو» للحال على أن حمل الواو على العطف يستلزم إلغاء قوله: «وأنت مريضة»، فوجب أن يحمل على الحال تحرزاً عن الإلغاء؛ لأنّ كلام العاقل البالغ يحمل على الصحة بقدر الإمكان، والجواب عن الأوّل ظاهر وكذا عن الثاني؛ لأنّ إلغاء يلزم باختياره فلا يجب التحرّز عنه، كذا في "المعدن".

(3) قوله: [ولو نوى] قال صاحب "المغني" انتهى مجموع ما ذكر من أقسامها إلى أحد عشر: الأول العاطفة، والثاني والثالث «واوان» يرتفع ما بعدهما «واو» الاستيناف، والرابع والخامس واوان ينتصب ما بعدهما، «واو» المفعول معه كسرتُ والنيل»، و«الواو» الداخلة على المضارع المنصوب لعطفه على اسم صريح أو مؤوّل والحق أنّ هذه «واو» العطف، والسادس والسابع واوان» ينجر ما بعدهما وهما «واو» القسم و «واو رُبِّ»، والصحيح أنها «واو» العطف وأنّ الجرّ بـ «رُبّ» محذوفة، والثامن «الواو» الزائدة، والتاسع «واو» الثمانية، والعاشر «الواو» الداخلة على الجملة الموصوف بها لتأكيد لصوقها بموصوفها وإفادتها أن اتصافه بها أمر ثابت، وهذه «الواو» أثبتها الزمخشري وحمل على ذلك مواضع «الواو» فيها للحال والحادي عشر (واو) ضمير الذكور، نحو: الرجال قالوا.

ثم على الصفحة التالية: صحت نيته فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ لأن اللفظ وإن كان يحتمل معنى الحال إلا أن الظاهر خلافه وإذا تأيد ذلك بقصده ثبت، ولو قال: «خذ هذه الألف مضاربة واعمل بها في البز» لا يتقيد العمل في البز ويكون المضاربة عامة؛ لأن العمل في البز لا يصلح حالا لأخذ الألف مضاربة فلا يتقيد صدر الكلام به وعلى هذا قال أبو حنيفة : إذا قالت لزوجها: «طلقني ولك ألف» بالعمل في البز.

(4) قوله: [صحت نيته ... إلخ] لأنّ الكلام يحتمل معنى الحال فكأنه نوى محتمل كلامه فيصدّق ديانةً أي: فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى ولا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنّ المجاز خلاف الظاهر ففي كلّ صورة يدعي المتكلّم خلاف الظاهر كان متهماً بادعاء خلاف الظاهر فيصدق ديانةً لا قضاءً لمكان التهمة أي: القاضي لا يصدقه في هذه النية، بخلاف المسئلة الثالثة وهي قوله: «خُذ هذه الألف واعمل بها في البز» فإنّ المحل لا يصلح للحال؛ لأنّ العمل في البز متأخر عن أخذ الألف، والحال يجامع ذا الحال ويقارنه فلا يكون أخذ الألف مقيّداً به فحمل على الإطلاق والعموم كما هو الأصل في المضاربة؛ وذلك لأنّ الغرض منها حصول الربح وذا إنما يحصل بالعموم والإطلاق، كذا في "الفصول".

(5) قوله: [إلا أن الظاهر خلافه] أي خلاف احتمال اللفظ لإمكان العمل بحقيقة الواو، فكان إرادة المجاز خلاف الظاهر، كذا في "الفصول".

(6) قوله : [ ثبت] أي خلاف الظاهر وحمل «الواو» على الحال ويصدّق ذلك ديانةً لا قضاء لمكان التهمة؛ لأنه يدعي خلاف الظاهر، كذا في "المعدن".

(7) قوله : [في البز ... إلخ قيل: إنّما لا يصلح عمل البرّ حالاً عن أخذ الألف؛ لأنه متأخر عن أخذه، والحال واجبة المجامعة والمقارنة للعامل قلت لي هاهنا نظر من وجوه أمّا أوّلاً فلأنّ الواجب للحالية هو القران ابتداءً أو بقاء لأخذ يقارنه العمل، وأما ثانياً فلأنه يصلح أن يعتبر حالاً مقدّرة كما قلتم في المثال السابق وهو قوله: «أدّ إلي ألْفاً وأنتَ حرّ»، وأما ثالثاً فلأنّ قوله : واعمل بها في البز» جملة انشائية لا تصلح حالاً، فالأحسن أن يقال : إنه لا يصلح حالاً عنه من حيث إنه ليس بخبر وه حيث إنّ الشرط الحالية تعدّر العطف وهو غير متعدّر بين الإنشائين فيكون معطوفاً على الأوّل، فيكون العقد قبل ربِّ المال مطلقاً فافهم كذا في "الحصول".

(8) قوله: [وعلى هذا] أي: على أنّ ما لا يصلح حالاً لا يجعل الواو فيه للحال، قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: من
The master says to his slave: 'Pay a thousand (dirhams) and you are free.' Then freedom is realized at the time of payment. And the proof of that is that the master does not owe a debt on his slave while slavery exists in him, i.e., at the very moment. Even if he destroys something from his property, he is not liable. And the suspension (of freedom) is valid with it, so it is carried upon it. And if he said: 'You are divorced and you are sick' or 'and you are praying,' she is divorced immediately even if he intended suspension, because of the impossibility of (interpreting it as) a state (ḥāl) and the validity of conjunction. (Then follow the footnotes elaborating on these points, the classification of waw into eleven types, and the discussion of intention and the example of the mudāraba contract. The final note states: 'And upon this, Abu Hanifa said: if a woman says to her husband: "Divorce me and you have a thousand," with the work in cloth.' The footnotes further explain the criteria for interpreting waw as ḥāl or ʿaṭf, and the distinction between what is believed religiously (diyāna) versus judicially (qaḍāʾ).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage from 'Uṣūl al-Shāshī' with the commentary 'Aḥsan al-Ḥawāshī' focuses on the semantic determination of the particle 'waw' (واو) in legal utterances. The text opens by presenting a paradigmatic example: a master says to his slave, 'Pay a thousand dirhams and you are free.' The issue is whether the waw functions as a simple conjunction (عاطفة) indicating two separate commands, or as a waw of state (حالية) indicating that the freedom occurs concurrent with the payment. The commentator proves it must be the latter: if it were conjunction, it would require the master to impose a debt on the slave, which is legally impossible because a slave cannot be indebted to his master—the slave and his property belong to the master. Therefore, the only valid interpretation is that the waw indicates the state (ḥāl) of the slave at the time of payment, i.e., he becomes free upon paying. This is corroborated by the principle that a master cannot enforce a financial obligation against his slave. The text then extends this analysis to divorce statements: if a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced and you are sick' or 'and you are praying,' the default interpretation (if the waw is taken as conjunction) leads to immediate divorce, even if the husband intended to condition the divorce on the illness or prayer. The commentator explains that the reason is that the apparent situation of a husband does not support the idea that he would divorce his wife while she is sick, as illness typically evokes compassion. Thus, the literal conjunction (ḥaqīqat al-ʿaṭf) can be applied, resulting in immediate divorce. The opposing view—that the waw should be taken as ḥāl to avoid making the clause redundant—is rebutted because redundancy is chosen by the speaker and hence acceptable. A significant portion of the commentary (footnote 3) provides a comprehensive enumeration of eleven distinct uses of waw: (1) conjunction (عاطفة), (2–3) waw of istiʾnāf (subject of a new sentence), (4–5) waw of maʿiyya (accompaniment) and waw attached to naṣb, (6–7) waw of oath and waw of rubba, (8) extra waw, (9) waw of eight, (10) waw affirming the attribute's permanence (as argued by Zamakhsharī), and (11) waw as a pronoun for masculine plural. On the next page, the text addresses the scenario where a man says to his wife: 'Take this thousand as a mudāraba and work with it in cloth.' The waw here cannot be ḥāl because the actions are sequential (working with cloth occurs after taking the money), so the contract is unrestricted, and the mudāraba is general. Abu Hanifa applies the same logic to a wife saying: 'Divorce me and you have a thousand'—the waw is not ḥāl, so the condition is not attached. The footnotes clarify that when the waw cannot be ḥāl, it must be ʿaṭf, and the legal effect follows accordingly. The commentator also distinguishes between religious sincerity (diyāna) and judicial proof (qaḍāʾ): if a speaker intends a meaning that goes against the apparent function, he is believed religiously but not in court.

Key Terms
الواو العاطفة (al-wāw al-ʿāṭifa): The coordinating conjunction that links two equal items without implying condition
الواو الحالية (al-wāw al-ḥāliyya): The wāw that introduces a circumstantial clause describing the state of the subject concurrent with the main action
التعليق (al-taʿlīq): Suspending a legal effect (like divorce or manumission) on a future condition
حقيقة العطف (ḥaqīqat al-ʿaṭf): The literal meaning of the waw as a conjunction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis in this lesson, why is the waw in 'Ad ilā alfan wa-anta ḥurr' interpreted as ḥāl rather than conjunction?
A) Because the master intended it as a condition.
B) Because conjunction would require the master to impose a debt on his slave, which is legally impossible.
C) Because the waw appears after a command, and waw after commands is always ḥāl.
D) Because the slave cannot be freed before paying.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the waw particle is given its literal meaning as conjunction unless that leads to legal impossibility, in which case it is interpreted as ḥāl to preserve the utterance's validity.

Waw as Conjunction: The Divorce Example and Intention
Application of waw interpretation rules to divorce and contract examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when waw is interpreted as conjunction in divorce statements and distinguish between religious sincerity (diyāna) and judicial proof (qaḍāʾ) regarding the speaker's intention.

Core Idea
When waw as conjunction is possible and not impossible, it is the default, and the speaker's contrary intention is believed only religiously, not in court.

This lesson continues the analysis of waw by examining a divorce statement: 'You are divorced and you are sick.' Unlike the slave example where conjunction was impossible, here conjunction is perfectly possible because a husband can divorce a sick wife. The Hanafi ruling is that the divorce is immediate, even if the husband intended suspension. This shows that the default interpretation of waw as conjunction is strong. However, the speaker's subjective intention is considered in religious matters (diyāna) but not in judicial proceedings (qaḍāʾ). The lesson also touches on the mudāraba example to show when waw cannot be ḥāl because the actions are sequential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divorce statement and its clauses
The husband says: 'You are divorced and you are sick.' The waw joins two clauses: the divorce announcement and the description of her condition.
2
Test whether conjunction is possible
Unlike the slave case, there is no legal impossibility in a husband divorcing his wife while she is sick. Therefore, conjunction is possible.
The apparent situation does not prevent divorce; the husband might divorce out of harm or disgust.
3
Apply the default: waw as conjunction
Since conjunction is possible, the waw is taken as conjunction. This means the divorce is immediate, not conditional on her being sick.
The clause 'and you are sick' is simply descriptive, not a condition.
4
Consider the speaker's intention
If the husband intended to make divorce conditional on sickness (taʿlīq), his intention does not change the apparent meaning in court. Religiously (diyāna) he is believed, but judicially (qaḍāʾ) he is not.
This distinction protects legal certainty.
5
Apply the same logic to mudāraba
In 'Take this thousand as mudāraba and work with it in cloth,' the waw cannot be ḥāl because working in cloth is a future action, not simultaneous. Therefore, it is conjunction, and the partnership is unrestricted.
This illustrates that ḥāl requires simultaneity.

أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي

المولى لعبده: «أد إلى ألفا وأنت حر»، فإن الحرية يتحقق حال الأداء وقامت الدلالة على ذلك فإن المولى لا يستوجب على عبده مالا مع قيام الرق فيه أي على تعيين الحال حتى لو أتلف من ماله شيئا لا يضمن. وقد صح التعليق به فحمل عليه ولو قال: «أنت طالق وأنت مريضة أو أنت مصلية» تطلق في الحال ولو نوى التعليق لعدم الحالية صحة العطف.

(1) قوله: [مع قيام الرق فيه] أي: في العبد يعني لو كان الواو للعطف كان هذا الكلام لإيجاب الألف على العبد ابتداء، و ليس للمولى ذلك مع قيام الرق فيه؛ لأنّ المولى لا يستوجب ديناً على عبده؛ لأنّ العبد وما في يده ملك المولى، فيكون إيجاب المولى عليه كإيجابه على نفسه والإيجاب على نفسه لا يمكن، فكذا الإيجاب على عبده، ولا يصلح أن يجعل ذلك ضريبة؛ لأنّ الضريبة لم يجز بهذا القدر عرفاً فتعدّر العطف، كذا في "المعدن".

(2) قوله: [تطلق في الحال... إلخ] لانتفاء الدليل على ثبوت الحال وتعدّر العطف؛ لأنّ ظاهر حال الزوج يشهد بأنه لا يطلّقها في حال مرضها؛ لأنّ المرض سبب التعطف والترحّم، فأمكن العمل بحقيقة العطف، فحمل عليه فتطلق في الحال فلا يكون الطلاق معلّقاً بالمرض والصلاة، وعورض بأنّ الزوج لا يستمتع بها في مرضها فربما يطلقها في هذه الحالة تضرراً أو توحّشاً منها، فينبغي أن يكون «الواو» للحال على أن حمل الواو على العطف يستلزم إلغاء قوله: «وأنت مريضة»، فوجب أن يحمل على الحال تحرزاً عن الإلغاء؛ لأنّ كلام العاقل البالغ يحمل على الصحة بقدر الإمكان، والجواب عن الأوّل ظاهر وكذا عن الثاني؛ لأنّ إلغاء يلزم باختياره فلا يجب التحرّز عنه، كذا في "المعدن".

(3) قوله: [ولو نوى] قال صاحب "المغني" انتهى مجموع ما ذكر من أقسامها إلى أحد عشر: الأول العاطفة، والثاني والثالث «واوان» يرتفع ما بعدهما «واو» الاستيناف، والرابع والخامس واوان ينتصب ما بعدهما، «واو» المفعول معه كسرتُ والنيل»، و«الواو» الداخلة على المضارع المنصوب لعطفه على اسم صريح أو مؤوّل والحق أنّ هذه «واو» العطف، والسادس والسابع واوان» ينجر ما بعدهما وهما «واو» القسم و «واو رُبِّ»، والصحيح أنها «واو» العطف وأنّ الجرّ بـ «رُبّ» محذوفة، والثامن «الواو» الزائدة، والتاسع «واو» الثمانية، والعاشر «الواو» الداخلة على الجملة الموصوف بها لتأكيد لصوقها بموصوفها وإفادتها أن اتصافه بها أمر ثابت، وهذه «الواو» أثبتها الزمخشري وحمل على ذلك مواضع «الواو» فيها للحال والحادي عشر (واو) ضمير الذكور، نحو: الرجال قالوا.

ثم على الصفحة التالية: صحت نيته فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ لأن اللفظ وإن كان يحتمل معنى الحال إلا أن الظاهر خلافه وإذا تأيد ذلك بقصده ثبت، ولو قال: «خذ هذه الألف مضاربة واعمل بها في البز» لا يتقيد العمل في البز ويكون المضاربة عامة؛ لأن العمل في البز لا يصلح حالا لأخذ الألف مضاربة فلا يتقيد صدر الكلام به وعلى هذا قال أبو حنيفة : إذا قالت لزوجها: «طلقني ولك ألف» بالعمل في البز.

(4) قوله: [صحت نيته ... إلخ] لأنّ الكلام يحتمل معنى الحال فكأنه نوى محتمل كلامه فيصدّق ديانةً أي: فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى ولا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنّ المجاز خلاف الظاهر ففي كلّ صورة يدعي المتكلّم خلاف الظاهر كان متهماً بادعاء خلاف الظاهر فيصدق ديانةً لا قضاءً لمكان التهمة أي: القاضي لا يصدقه في هذه النية، بخلاف المسئلة الثالثة وهي قوله: «خُذ هذه الألف واعمل بها في البز» فإنّ المحل لا يصلح للحال؛ لأنّ العمل في البز متأخر عن أخذ الألف، والحال يجامع ذا الحال ويقارنه فلا يكون أخذ الألف مقيّداً به فحمل على الإطلاق والعموم كما هو الأصل في المضاربة؛ وذلك لأنّ الغرض منها حصول الربح وذا إنما يحصل بالعموم والإطلاق، كذا في "الفصول".

(5) قوله: [إلا أن الظاهر خلافه] أي خلاف احتمال اللفظ لإمكان العمل بحقيقة الواو، فكان إرادة المجاز خلاف الظاهر، كذا في "الفصول".

(6) قوله : [ ثبت] أي خلاف الظاهر وحمل «الواو» على الحال ويصدّق ذلك ديانةً لا قضاء لمكان التهمة؛ لأنه يدعي خلاف الظاهر، كذا في "المعدن".

(7) قوله : [في البز ... إلخ قيل: إنّما لا يصلح عمل البرّ حالاً عن أخذ الألف؛ لأنه متأخر عن أخذه، والحال واجبة المجامعة والمقارنة للعامل قلت لي هاهنا نظر من وجوه أمّا أوّلاً فلأنّ الواجب للحالية هو القران ابتداءً أو بقاء لأخذ يقارنه العمل، وأما ثانياً فلأنه يصلح أن يعتبر حالاً مقدّرة كما قلتم في المثال السابق وهو قوله: «أدّ إلي ألْفاً وأنتَ حرّ»، وأما ثالثاً فلأنّ قوله : واعمل بها في البز» جملة انشائية لا تصلح حالاً، فالأحسن أن يقال : إنه لا يصلح حالاً عنه من حيث إنه ليس بخبر وه حيث إنّ الشرط الحالية تعدّر العطف وهو غير متعدّر بين الإنشائين فيكون معطوفاً على الأوّل، فيكون العقد قبل ربِّ المال مطلقاً فافهم كذا في "الحصول".

(8) قوله: [وعلى هذا] أي: على أنّ ما لا يصلح حالاً لا يجعل الواو فيه للحال، قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: من
The master says to his slave: 'Pay a thousand (dirhams) and you are free.' Then freedom is realized at the time of payment. And the proof of that is that the master does not owe a debt on his slave while slavery exists in him, i.e., at the very moment. Even if he destroys something from his property, he is not liable. And the suspension (of freedom) is valid with it, so it is carried upon it. And if he said: 'You are divorced and you are sick' or 'and you are praying,' she is divorced immediately even if he intended suspension, because of the impossibility of (interpreting it as) a state (ḥāl) and the validity of conjunction. (Then follow the footnotes elaborating on these points, the classification of waw into eleven types, and the discussion of intention and the example of the mudāraba contract. The final note states: 'And upon this, Abu Hanifa said: if a woman says to her husband: "Divorce me and you have a thousand," with the work in cloth.' The footnotes further explain the criteria for interpreting waw as ḥāl or ʿaṭf, and the distinction between what is believed religiously (diyāna) versus judicially (qaḍāʾ).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage from 'Uṣūl al-Shāshī' with the commentary 'Aḥsan al-Ḥawāshī' focuses on the semantic determination of the particle 'waw' (واو) in legal utterances. The text opens by presenting a paradigmatic example: a master says to his slave, 'Pay a thousand dirhams and you are free.' The issue is whether the waw functions as a simple conjunction (عاطفة) indicating two separate commands, or as a waw of state (حالية) indicating that the freedom occurs concurrent with the payment. The commentator proves it must be the latter: if it were conjunction, it would require the master to impose a debt on the slave, which is legally impossible because a slave cannot be indebted to his master—the slave and his property belong to the master. Therefore, the only valid interpretation is that the waw indicates the state (ḥāl) of the slave at the time of payment, i.e., he becomes free upon paying. This is corroborated by the principle that a master cannot enforce a financial obligation against his slave. The text then extends this analysis to divorce statements: if a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced and you are sick' or 'and you are praying,' the default interpretation (if the waw is taken as conjunction) leads to immediate divorce, even if the husband intended to condition the divorce on the illness or prayer. The commentator explains that the reason is that the apparent situation of a husband does not support the idea that he would divorce his wife while she is sick, as illness typically evokes compassion. Thus, the literal conjunction (ḥaqīqat al-ʿaṭf) can be applied, resulting in immediate divorce. The opposing view—that the waw should be taken as ḥāl to avoid making the clause redundant—is rebutted because redundancy is chosen by the speaker and hence acceptable. A significant portion of the commentary (footnote 3) provides a comprehensive enumeration of eleven distinct uses of waw: (1) conjunction (عاطفة), (2–3) waw of istiʾnāf (subject of a new sentence), (4–5) waw of maʿiyya (accompaniment) and waw attached to naṣb, (6–7) waw of oath and waw of rubba, (8) extra waw, (9) waw of eight, (10) waw affirming the attribute's permanence (as argued by Zamakhsharī), and (11) waw as a pronoun for masculine plural. On the next page, the text addresses the scenario where a man says to his wife: 'Take this thousand as a mudāraba and work with it in cloth.' The waw here cannot be ḥāl because the actions are sequential (working with cloth occurs after taking the money), so the contract is unrestricted, and the mudāraba is general. Abu Hanifa applies the same logic to a wife saying: 'Divorce me and you have a thousand'—the waw is not ḥāl, so the condition is not attached. The footnotes clarify that when the waw cannot be ḥāl, it must be ʿaṭf, and the legal effect follows accordingly. The commentator also distinguishes between religious sincerity (diyāna) and judicial proof (qaḍāʾ): if a speaker intends a meaning that goes against the apparent function, he is believed religiously but not in court.

Key Terms
الواو العاطفة (al-wāw al-ʿāṭifa): The coordinating conjunction that links two equal items without implying condition
الواو الحالية (al-wāw al-ḥāliyya): The wāw that introduces a circumstantial clause describing the state of the subject concurrent with the main action
ديانة (diyāna): Religious sincerity; what one believes privately before Allah; accepted even if contrary to apparent meaning.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Judicial ruling based on apparent evidence; does not consider hidden intentions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the waw in 'Take this thousand as mudāraba and work with it in cloth' interpreted as conjunction rather than state?
A) Because the work in cloth is mentioned after the main command.
B) Because working in cloth cannot occur simultaneously with taking the money; it is a future action.
C) Because the mudāraba contract requires generality.
D) Because the clause after waw is a command, not a statement.

Key Takeaway
When waw as conjunction is possible (no impossibility), it is the default interpretation; the speaker's contrary intention is relevant only religiously (diyāna), not in court (qaḍāʾ).

Ruling on the Waw as Circumstantial vs Conjunctive in Business Contracts
Explaining the ruling on interpreting waw in a partnership contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a clause after waw is interpreted as conjunctive versus circumstantial in Hanafi usul, and apply the rule to similar contractual phrases.

Core Idea
A clause after waw is circumstantial only if concurrent and declarative; otherwise it is conjunctive.

In Hanafi usul, the particle waw can join two clauses or indicate a circumstantial state (حال). The correct interpretation affects the legal scope of contracts. This lesson examines a key example from Al-Shashi's Usul to derive the conditions for حال and the default rule for waw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its two possible interpretations
Look at the phrase 'خذ هذه الألف واعمل بها في البز'. The waw could be conjunctive (عطف) joining two independent commands, or circumstantial (حال) describing the state of taking the money.
The ruling states that the work is not restricted to cloth, indicating the waw is conjunctive.
2
Understand the condition for a circumstantial clause (حال)
For a clause after waw to be حال, it must be concurrent with the main action and must be a declarative (خبر) sentence, not performative (إنشاء).
The hashya emphasizes that الحال واجبة المجامعة والمقارنة للعامل (must accompany and be concurrent).
3
Apply the condition to the phrase
The clause 'واعمل بها في البز' is a command (performative), and the work occurs after taking the money, so it cannot be concurrent. Thus it fails both conditions.
The hashya also addresses an objection that it could be an estimated حال, but rejects it because the clause is not declarative.
4
Address counterarguments
The hashya considers the counterexample 'أد إلي ألفا وأنت حر' where the second clause is حال. The key difference is that 'وأنت حر' is declarative (خبر), while 'واعمل بها' is performative.
This shows that the nature of the sentence (declarative vs performative) is crucial.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
Since the clause is not suitable as حال, the waw is conjunctive. Therefore, the partnership contract is absolute (مطلقا), and the agent is not restricted to working only in cloth.
The final phrase 'فيكون العقد قبل رب المال مطلقا' indicates the practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: «خذ هذه الألف واعمل بها في البز» لا يتقيد العمل في البز ويكون المضاربة عامة. قوله: [في البز ... إلخ] قيل: إنما لا يصلح عمل البرّ حالاً عن أخذ الألف؛ لأنه متأخر عن أخذه، والحال واجبة المجامعة والمقارنة للعامل قلت لي هاهنا نظر من وجوه أما أولا فلأن الواجب للحالية هو القران ابتداء أو بقاء لأخذ يقارنه العمل، وأما ثانيا فلأنه يصلح أن يعتبر حالا مقدرة كما قلتم في المثال السابق وهو قوله: «أد إلي ألفا وأنت حر»، وأما ثالثا فلأن قوله: واعمل بها في البز جملة إنشائية لا تصلح حالا، فالأحسن أن يقال: إنه لا يصلح حالا عنه من حيث إنه ليس بخبر وه حيث إن الشرط الحالية تعذر العطف وهو غير متعذر بين الإنشائين فيكون معطوفا على الأول، فيكون العقد قبل رب المال مطلقا فافهم.
And if he said: 'Take this thousand and work with it in cloth,' the work is not restricted to cloth, and the partnership is general. Commentary: [Regarding 'in cloth' etc.] It is said: The work of cloth is not suitable as a circumstantial clause for taking the thousand because it is subsequent to taking it, and the circumstantial clause must accompany and be concurrent with the agent. I say: Here there is consideration from several aspects. Firstly, what is required for the circumstantial is conjunction initially or remaining, so that the work accompanies the taking. Secondly, it can be considered an estimated circumstantial as you said in the previous example of 'Pay me a thousand and you are free.' Thirdly, the phrase 'and work with it in cloth' is a performative sentence, not suitable as a circumstantial. So it is better to say: It is not suitable as a circumstantial because it is not declarative, and since the condition for circumstantial is that conjunction is impossible, but it is not impossible between two performatives, so it is conjunctive to the first, and thus the contract with the capital owner is absolute. Understand.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul discusses the interpretation of the particle waw in the phrase 'Take this thousand and work with it in cloth.' The ruling is that the waw is for conjunction (عطف), not for indicating a circumstantial clause (حال). The reason is that the work (working in cloth) occurs after taking the money, so it cannot be a circumstantial clause concurrent with the taking. The commentary addresses an objection that perhaps it could be an estimated circumstantial, like in the manumission example, but rejects it because the clause is performative (إنشاء) not declarative (خبر). Thus, the waw is conjunctive, and the partnership contract is absolute, not restricted to cloth trading. This illustrates a rule in Hanafi usul about when a clause after waw can be interpreted as حال.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The particle waw that can indicate conjunction (عطف) or circumstantial clause (حال) depending on context.
الحال (al-hal): A circumstantial clause that describes the state of the agent concurrent with the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashya, which characteristic of the phrase 'واعمل بها في البز' prevents it from being a circumstantial clause?
A) It is a performative sentence.
B) It is a declarative sentence.
C) It is a conditional sentence.
D) It is an imperative sentence but considered declarative.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a clause after waw is conjunctive unless it is both concurrent and declarative, ensuring contractual clarity.

The Particle Fa: Immediate Succession and Its Legal Impact
Defining the particle fa as indicating immediate succession with connection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the particle fa as used in Hanafi usul, distinguish it from waw, and apply its meaning to derive rulings in contracts and conditional statements.

Core Idea
The particle fa demands immediate succession with connection; any delay changes the legal ruling.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the precise meaning of particles is crucial for deriving rulings from texts. This lesson focuses on the particle fa (ف), which according to the consensus of linguists and jurists indicates immediate succession (ta'qib) with connection (wasl). We will examine its definition, contrast it with other particles like waw, and see how its presence or absence directly affects legal outcomes in contracts, liability, and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic rule
Understand that fa indicates immediate succession (ta'qib) with connection (wasl). This is the core definition from Usul al-Shashi, agreed upon by consensus.
Ta'qib excludes simultaneity; wasl excludes delay.
2
Distinguish from other particles
Contrast fa with waw (و), which indicates mere conjunction without immediacy. The sale example shows that using waw changes the legal effect from acceptance to rejection.
This distinction is critical in contracts.
3
Apply to contractual examples
In the sale scenario, the buyer's response 'He is free' with fa counts as constructive acceptance, effecting emancipation immediately. With waw, it is a rejection.
Emancipation occurs immediately after the sale due to fa.
4
Apply to conditional scenarios
In the conditional divorce, fa requires that the entry be immediately followed by the divorce. A delayed entry does not fulfill the condition, so no divorce occurs.
The condition is not met if there is any interval.
5
Note the causal use
Fa can also indicate cause (ta'lil), as in 'Pay me one thousand', where the payment is the cause for something else. Be aware of this secondary function.
Context determines which meaning applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي
فصل الفاء للتعقيب
أي تعقيب المعطوف عليه بالمعطوف. جمع جزاء. فصل الفاء للتعقيب مع الوصل ولهذا تستعمل في الأجزية لما أنها بإجماع أيمة اللغة والفقه. تعقبا ذاتيا لا زمانيا.

قوله: [قوله: «الفاء» للتعقيب] أي: مع الوصل يعني موجبه وجود الثاني بعد الأوّل بغير مهلة حتى لو قال: «ضربتُ زيداً فعمراً كان المعنى إنّ ضرب عمرو وقع عقيب ضرب زيد و لم تطاولت المدة بينهما، ولفظ «التعقيب» يشير إلى أنه ليس للمقارنة، ولفظ «الوصل» يشير إلى أنه ليس للتراخي كذا في "الفصول".

قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله : إذا قال: «بعت منك هذا العبد بألف» فقال الآخر: «فهو حر» يكون ذلك قبولا للبيع اقتضاء ويثبت العتق منه عقيب البيع بخلاف ما لو قال: «وهو حر» أو «هو حر» بالواو بدون الفاء فإنه يكون ردا للبيع، وإذا قال للخياط: انظر إلى هذا الثوب أيكفيني قميصا فنظر فقال: نعم فقال صاحب الثوب فاقطعه فقطعه، فإذا هو لا يكفيه كان الخياط ضامنا ؛ لأنه إنما أمره بالقطع عقيب الكفاية بخلاف ما لو قال: اقطعه أو بالواو بلا تفريع واقطعه فقطعه فإنه لا يكون الخياط ضامنا، ولو قال: «بعت منك هذا الثوب بعشرة فاقطعه فقطعه ولم يقل شيئا كان البيع تاما، ولو قال: «إن دخلت هذه الدار فأنت طالق فالشرط دخول الثانية عقيب دخول الأولى متصلا به حتى لو دخلت الثانية أولا أو آخرا لكنه بعد مدة لا يقع الطلاق، وقد يكون «الفاء» لبيان العلة، مثاله إذا قال لعبده : «أد إلي ألفا» لبيان علة ما بعدها لما قبلها.
Foundations of al-Shashi with the Best Annotations. Chapter: The fa is for immediate succession. Meaning: the successor is connected to the predecessor. Combined parts. Chapter: The fa is for immediate succession with connection, and for this reason it is used in conditions because it is by consensus of linguists and jurists. An essential succession, not temporal. He said: [His saying: 'The fa is for immediate succession'] i.e., with connection, meaning it requires the existence of the second after the first without delay, so if he says: 'I hit Zaid then Amr', the meaning is that the hitting of Amr occurred immediately after the hitting of Zaid and the time between them did not prolong, and the word 'immediate succession' indicates that it is not for simultaneity, and the word 'connection' indicates that it is not for delay, as in al-Fusul. Our companions, may Allah have mercy on them, said: If he says: 'I sold you this slave for one thousand' and the other says: 'He is free', then that is a constructive acceptance of the sale and the emancipation is established immediately after the sale, unlike if he said: 'And he is free' or 'He is free' with waw without fa, then it is a rejection of the sale. And if he says to the tailor: 'Look at this cloth: will it suffice me for a shirt?' and he looks and says: 'Yes', then the owner says: 'Cut it', and he cuts it, then it turns out it does not suffice, the tailor is liable because he only ordered the cutting immediately after sufficiency, unlike if he said: 'Cut it' or with waw without subordination and 'cut it' and he cuts it, then the tailor is not liable. And if he says: 'I sold you this cloth for ten, so cut it' and he cuts it and says nothing, the sale is complete. And if he says: 'If you enter this house, you are divorced', then the condition is the entering of the second immediately after the entering of the first connected to it, such that if she enters the second first or last but after a while, divorce does not occur. And sometimes fa is for indicating the cause, for example if he says to his slave: 'Pay me one thousand' to indicate the cause of what comes after for what came before.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi defines the linguistic and legal meaning of the particle 'fa' (ف). The author states that 'fa' indicates immediate succession (ta'qib) with connection (wasl), meaning that the second action follows the first without any interval. This is a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh for interpreting conditional statements and contracts. The commentary elaborates that 'ta'qib' excludes simultaneity, while 'wasl' excludes delay. The author then provides several Hanafi fiqh examples. First, in a contract of sale, if the buyer responds to the offer by saying 'He is free' (with fa), this counts as acceptance of the sale and immediately effects emancipation; if he uses 'waw' (and), it is a rejection. Second, with a tailor: if the owner says 'Cut it' (with fa) conditioned on sufficiency, and the tailor cuts without sufficiency, he is liable; if he says 'Cut it' without fa (absolute permission), there is no liability. Third, in a conditional divorce: if the husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', the divorce occurs only if the entry follows immediately; a delayed entry does not trigger divorce. Finally, 'fa' can also indicate cause (ta'lil), as in 'Pay me one thousand' where the payment is the cause for something else. The passage demonstrates how a seemingly linguistic rule has direct legal consequences.

Key Terms
الفاء (al-fa): The Arabic letter fa used as a particle to indicate immediate succession with connection (ta'qib ma'a al-wasl).
التعقيب (al-ta'qib): The meaning of fa that the second action follows the first without any interval; it excludes simultaneity.
الوصل (al-wasl): Connection between the two actions, meaning no delay or separation; the second is directly linked to the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the tailor, why is the tailor liable when the cloth does not suffice?
A) Because he cut the cloth without permission.
B) Because the owner's command 'Cut it' (with fa) implied immediate succession conditioned on sufficiency, and when sufficiency was absent, the cut was unauthorized.
C) Because the tailor did not check the cloth properly.
D) Because the owner did not pay him.

Key Takeaway
The particle fa requires immediate succession with connection, and its presence or absence in legal statements can change the ruling entirely.

Tailor's Liability with Unconditional Permission
Ruling on tailor's liability when buyer says 'cut it' without condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that an unrestricted permission does not create liability, and distinguish between cases of mere news and conditional contracts.

Core Idea
Unrestricted permission (إذن مطلق) does not impose liability; only a restricted or conditional command may create guarantee.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi examines the ruling on a tailor's liability when he is told 'cut it' without any condition. The text distinguishes between unconditional permission—where the tailor is not liable—and a case where the instruction was given with a condition (e.g., 'cut it such that it suffices you'). The footnote addresses a potential objection based on deception (taghrir). By analysing the legal reasoning, you will learn how Hanafi usul categorises permissions and the limits of liability for mere statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative phrase and its textual category
The key phrase is 'اقطعه فقطعه' (cut it and he cuts it). This is a command (amr) without any qualifier. In Hanafi usul, an amr mutlaq indicates permission unless there is a qarina (indication) of obligation. Here, the context shows it is mere permission.
The fa indicates immediate sequence; the action follows the command.
2
Classify the permission as unrestricted (إذن مطلق)
The text calls it an 'unrestricted permission'. In usul, an unrestricted permission does not carry an implied guarantee. The tailor is simply following the owner's instruction; he does not warrant the cloth's fitness.
Compare with a conditional permission (e.g., 'cut it provided it suffices you') which creates a guarantee if the condition fails.
3
Apply the usul rule that general permission does not create liability
The footnote states: 'لعموم الإجازة بلا تقييد' (due to the generality of permission without restriction). In Hanafi usul, when a permission is general, it covers all outcomes; thus the tailor's cutting is an act of compliance, not a guarantee.
This is a direct application of the principle that amm (general) includes all its instances.
4
Address the objection of deception (taghrir) using analogy (qiyas)
The objector says: the tailor's statement 'يكفيك' (it will suffice you) may deceive the owner, so the tailor should be liable. The response: mere news (خبر) in a non-exchange context does not create liability, analogised to someone saying 'this road is safe' – if thieves attack, the informant is not liable.
This shows the distinction between liability in exchange contracts (where misrepresentation may invalidate) and mere information.
5
Distinguish from the conditional case
The text mentions that if the command was 'cut it with the condition of sufficiency' (بشرط الكفاية), then liability would arise if the condition is absent. This is because the permission is restricted; the tailor implicitly guarantees that the cloth will suffice.
The illa (effective cause) for liability is the restriction in the permission, not the act of cutting itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الفاء للتعقيب
واقطعه فقطعه فإنّه لا يكون الخياط ضامنا، ولو قال: «بعت منك هذا الثوب بعشرة فاقطعه فقطعه ولم يقل شيئا كان البيع تاما،
(1) قوله: [فإنّه لا يكون... إلخ] لأنّ قوله: «اقطعه» إذن مطلق فلا يكون القطع بعده موجباً للضمان لعموم الإجازة بلا تقييد قيد فيه، فإن قيل: إنّ الخياط قد غرّ صاحب الثوب بقوله: «يكفيك» فينبغي أن يجب الضمان على الخياط، قلنا الغرور بمجرد الخبر إذا لم يكن في عقد المعاوضة لا يوجب على الغار، كما لو قال الرجل: «هذا طريق آمن فسلك فيه فأخذ اللصوص متاعه لا يضمن، "المبسوط"، بخلاف ما تقدّم فإنّ الأمر بالقطع بشرط الكفاية فيضمن عند عدم الشرط، كذا في "المعدن".
Section: The Fa for Immediate Sequence. And if he said, 'Cut it,' and he cut it, then the tailor is not liable. And if he said, 'I sold you this cloth for ten dirhams, so cut it,' and he cut it and said nothing, the sale is complete. (Footnote 1): His statement: 'Then he is not liable...' because his statement 'Cut it' is an unrestricted permission, so cutting afterwards does not impose liability due to the generality of permission without any restriction. If it is said: The tailor deceived the owner of the cloth by saying 'It will suffice you,' so liability should be imposed on the tailor, we respond: Deception by mere news, if not in a contract of exchange, does not impose liability on the deceiver, as if a man said, 'This road is safe,' and he traveled on it and thieves took his goods, he is not liable, from al-Mabsut. Unlike the previous case, where the command to cut with the condition of sufficiency imposes liability if the condition is absent, as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the section on the fa of immediate sequence. The main ruling: If a buyer tells a tailor 'cut it' without any further condition, and the tailor cuts the cloth, he is not liable for any defect that may be discovered later. The reason is that the permission is unrestricted (إذن مطلق). The footnote addresses a potential objection: what if the tailor said 'it will suffice you' (يكفيك) and the cloth turned out defective? The answer is that mere deception via a statement (غرور بالخبر) outside an exchange contract does not create liability. The example given is someone saying 'this road is safe' – if thieves then attack, the informant is not liable. This is from al-Mabsut and al-Ma'din. The distinction is made with a case where the command to cut was given with a condition of sufficiency (بشرط الكفاية); then if the condition fails, the tailor would be liable.

Key Terms
فاء التعقيب (fā' al-ta'qīb): The letter fa that indicates immediate sequence without delay
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or defect
إجازة (ijāzah): Permission, especially in sales and contracts
إذن مطلق (idhn muṭlaq): Unrestricted permission; permission without any conditions or limitations

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a buyer tells a tailor 'اقطعه فقطعه' (cut it) without any condition, and the tailor cuts the cloth, what is the ruling on the tailor's liability for any defect?
A) The tailor is liable for any defect because he performed the action
B) The tailor is not liable because the permission was unrestricted
C) The tailor is liable only if the cloth is totally ruined
D) The tailor is liable if he said 'يكفيك' (it will suffice you) and the cloth was defective

Key Takeaway
The tailor's liability depends on whether the permission was restricted or unconditional; the principle of 'إذن مطلق' shields the artisan from liability.

Immediate Sequence (Ta'qib) with Fa in Conditional Divorce
Ruling on divorce when condition uses fa requiring immediate sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that fa indicates immediate sequence (ta'qib) to determine whether a conditional divorce is triggered.

Core Idea
The particle fa in a condition requires immediate sequence — the second event must follow the first without delay.

In legal language, conjunctions are not merely grammatical tools; they carry specific meanings that shape rulings. The Hanafi school analyses the particle fa as indicating both connection (wasl) and immediate sequence (ta'qib). This lesson examines a conditional divorce statement where fa links two house entries, and explains why the divorce only occurs if the second entry follows immediately after the first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional structure
The husband says: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then you are divorced.' Note the two house entries linked by fa. The condition is the second entry after the first.
The fa between the first and second entry is the particle under analysis.
2
Determine the meaning of fa in Hanafi usul
The footnote clarifies that 'al-fā' al-waṣl ma'a al-ta'qīb' — fa indicates connection with immediate sequence. This means the second event must follow right after the first, with no substantial gap.
This is a linguistic principle (wad' al-lugha) accepted by the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the meaning to the condition
The condition is defined as 'dakhūl al-thāniyati 'uqayba dukhūl al-ūlā muttaṣilan bihī' — entry of the second immediately after the first, connected to it. So the condition is not just any sequence, but specifically immediate sequence.
The phrase 'muttaṣilan bihī' reinforces that there must be no interval.
4
Identify unsatisfied scenarios
If the wife enters the second house first (before the first), or enters the second house after a delay, the condition is not fulfilled. The fa requires a specific order and timing.
The text explicitly mentions: 'if she enters the second first or later but after a period, the divorce does not occur'.
5
Derive the ruling
Since the condition is not met, the divorce does not occur. The ruling is that the suspended divorce is not triggered because the condition (immediate sequence) is absent.
This illustrates the general principle that a conditional divorce only takes effect when the condition is exactly fulfilled as specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: «إن دخلت الدار هذه الدار فهذه الدار فأنت طالق فالشرط دخول الثانية عقيب دخول الأولى متصلاً به حتى لو دخلت الثانية أولاً أو آخراً لكنه بعد مدة لا يقع الطلاق لعدم وقوع الشرط.
(2) قوله: [لا يقع الطلاق لعدم وجود الشرط وهو دخول الثانية عقيب دخول الأولى متصلاً به؛ لأنّ الفاء الوصل مع التعقيب، فيقتضي أن يكون الشرط دخول الثانية بعد دخول الأولى، فإذا دخلت الثانية أوّلاً لم يوجد الاتصال، كذا في "المعدن".
And if he said: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then you are divorced,' the condition is the entry of the second house immediately after the entry of the first, connected to it, so that if she enters the second first or later but after a period, the divorce does not occur due to the condition not existing. (Footnote 2): His statement: '[The divorce does not occur due to the condition not existing, and it is the entry of the second immediately after the entry of the first, connected to it; because the fa indicates connection with sequence, so it necessitates that the condition be the entry of the second after the entry of the first. If she enters the second first, the connection does not exist,' as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage explains the requirement of immediate sequence (تعقيب) when the particle 'fa' is used in a conditional sentence. The husband says: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then you are divorced.' The condition for divorce is that the second entry must occur immediately after the first, without any intervening gap or delay. If the wife enters the second house first (i.e., before the first), or after a long delay, the divorce does not take place because the condition is not met. The footnote from al-Ma'din clarifies that the fa indicates both sequence and connection, so the condition is exactly that: the second entry following the first in immediate succession.

Key Terms
فاء الوصل مع التعقيب (fā' al-waṣl ma'a al-ta'qīb): Fa that indicates both connection and immediate sequence
شرط (sharṭ): Condition that suspends the occurrence of a legal effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Divorce occurs because she entered both houses.
B) Divorce does not occur because the condition is not met.
C) Divorce occurs only if she enters the first house first, regardless of timing.
D) The condition is invalid because it has two houses.

Key Takeaway
When fa links two events in a conditional divorce, the second event must occur immediately after the first; any delay or reversal prevents the divorce from taking effect.
