Fa as a Particle of Causality: Immediate Effect Dawām Condition
Ruling that fa can indicate cause, making a slave free or a harbi safe immediately

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the particle 'fa' indicates causality versus sequence, apply the Hanafi condition of continuity (dawām) to derive the ruling, and distinguish the immediate effect from a conditional interpretation.

Core Idea
When 'fa' links an imperative to a declarative, it signals immediate effect if the cause is continuous, not suspension.

This passage from the foundations of Hanafi usul examines a key linguistic principle: the particle 'fa' can indicate causality, not just sequence. Through two cases—a master's promise of freedom and a harbi's promise of safety—the text reveals how the continuity of the cause triggers immediate legal effect, overriding a literal conditional reading. Understanding fā' al-ta'līl is essential for deriving rulings from ambiguous speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and the two clauses
Look for the letter 'fa' (ف) between two sentences. In the slave example: 'Give me a thousand dirhams, then you are free'. Recognize that the first clause is a command (imperative) and the second is a statement (declarative).
This grammatical structure is central; the conjoining of imperative and declarative is problematic for 'fa' as a simple conjunction.
2
Recognize the grammatical problem
In Arabic, 'fa' normally functions as a conjunction linking two similar clauses. However, conjoining an imperative (طلبي) with a declarative (خبري) is not allowed. Therefore, 'fa' cannot be taken literally as 'then'.
The text argues that this impossibility forces a reinterpretation of 'fa' as indicating causation (التعليل).
3
Apply the principle of fā' al-ta'līl
Understand that 'fa' can indicate causality, meaning 'because' or 'for the reason that'. In the slave example, the intended meaning is 'Give me a thousand dirhams because you are free'.
The second clause (freedom) becomes the cause for the first clause (payment).
4
Apply the condition of continuity (dawām)
The cause must be continuous, i.e., exist both before and after the effect. Freedom is a continuous state—it existed before payment (as a cause) and remains after. If the cause were non-continuous (e.g., a one-time event), 'fa' of causality would not apply.
Imam Fakhr al-Islam (Sarakhsi) explicitly states: 'إنما تدخل الفاء على العلل إذا كانت مما تدوم'.
5
Derive the ruling
Since freedom is continuous, it serves as the cause for the command. Thus, the slave becomes free immediately upon utterance, and the payment becomes a debt. The same applies to the harbi: safety is continuous, so he becomes safe immediately.
This derivation shows how linguistic analysis directly generates a fiqh ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون «الفاء» لبيان العلة، مثاله إذا قال لعبده : «أدّ إلي ألفا فأنت حر» كان العبد حرا في الحال وإن لم يؤدّ شيئا، ولو قال للحربي: إنزل فأنت آمن كان آمنا وإن لم ينزل، وفي "الجامع" ما إذا قال: أمر الوكيل بالطلاق...
قوله: [لبيان العلّة :أي لإظهار أنّ المذكور في بعدها أو قبلها علّة وهو أعم من أن تكون داخلة على الحكم أو العلة وكلاهما يوجدان في كلام العرب، فالأوّل قولهم: «أطعمتُه فاشبعته» أو «سقيتُه فأرويته» أي: سقيته بسبب هذا السقي، والثاني كما يقال للأسير: «أبشر فقد أتاك الغوث فقد نجوت»، فـ الفاء دخلت على العلة في هذا المثال؛ لأنّ لحوق الغوث علّة البشارة ويسمى هذا الفاء فاء التعليل، كذا في "المعدن"، لأنها بمعنى لام التعليل، قال الإمام فخر الإسلام رحمه الله : إنما تدخل الفاء على العلل إذا كانت مما تدوم فتكون موجودة بعد الحكم كما كانت موجودة قبل الحكم فيحصل التعقيب الذي كان مدلول «الفاء» وإن لم يشترط الدوام في العلة لا يحسن دخول «الفاء» عليها؛ لأنها تتقدّم الحكم فكيف تكون محل الفاء وهذا كما يقال : «أبشر فقد أتاك الغوث» فإن إتيان الغوث وإن كان آتياً لكن ذاته دائمة تبقى إلى مدّة فيكون سابقاً على البشارة لاحقاً عنها يتحقق معنى التعقيب... إلخ.
(1) قوله: [كان العبد حرًا في الحال] فالحرّية دائمة الوجود حيث كانت موجودةً قبل الأداء وتبقى بعده إلى مدة فلا يتوقف على أداء الألف بل يكون حراً ويصير الألف ديناً على العبد الذي صار حرا، فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون تقديره : إن أديت فأنتَ حرّ، فيصير جواباً للأمر ويتوقف الحرّية على الأداء لتحقق معنى التعقيب بلا تكلّف أجيب بأنّ الأمر إنّما يستحق الجواب بتقدير كلمة «إن» إنّما تجعل الماضي والجملة الاسمية بمعنى المستقبل إذا كانت ظاهرة، أما إذا كانت مقدّرة فلا تجعلها بمعنى المستقبل، فلا يقال : «آتني أكرمتُك» أو «أنت مكرم»، كذا في "نور الأنوار".
(2) قوله: [كان العبد حرا... إلخ] لأنّ «الفاء» دخلت على العلة؛ لأنّ الحرّية دائم فيصير متراخياً عن الأداء نظراً إلى البقاء، فأشبه التراخي في الابتداء، فيصح دخول «الفاء» عليه، فصار معناه أدّ إلي ألفا؛ لأنك حرّ، فلا يتعلّق العتق بالأداء ويتنجز العتق؛ لأنه لا دلالة في الكلام على التعليق، وإنما حملت الفاء على العلة لتعذر حقيقتها وهو العطف لما سبق أن عطف الخبرية على الطلبية غير جائز وكذلك المسئلة الآتية، كذا في "المعدن".
And the 'fa' may be for clarifying the cause, for example if he said to his slave: 'Give me a thousand dirhams, then you are free,' the slave becomes free immediately even if he did not give anything. And if he said to a harbi: 'Descend, then you are safe,' he becomes safe even if he did not descend. And in al-Jami' what if he said: the agent for divorce... His statement: [For clarifying the cause: i.e., to show that what is mentioned after it or before it is the cause, and it is more general than whether it enters on the ruling or the cause; both occur in Arabic speech. The first is their saying: 'I fed him so he became full' or 'I gave him drink so he quenched his thirst,' meaning I gave him drink because of this drink. The second is as said to a captive: 'Be glad, for relief has come to you, so you have been saved.' So fa entered on the cause in this example because the arrival of relief is the cause of the glad tidings, and this fa is called fa of explanation, because it is in the meaning of lam of explanation. Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Fa only enters on causes if they are continuous, such that they exist after the ruling as they existed before it, so that the sequence which is the meaning of 'fa' occurs. If continuity is not required in the cause, then entry of 'fa' upon it is not appropriate because it precedes the ruling, so how can it be a place for 'fa'? This is as is said: 'Be glad, for relief has come to you' for the arrival of relief, although it is coming, its essence is continuous and remains for a period, so it is prior to the glad tidings and later than them, and the meaning of sequence is realized... etc. (1) His statement: [The slave is free immediately] because freedom is continuous in existence, as it existed before the payment and remains after it for a period, so it is not dependent on the payment of the thousand. Rather he becomes free and the thousand becomes a debt on the slave who became free. If it is said: Why is it not possible that the meaning is: 'If you pay, then you are free,' so that it becomes a response to the command and freedom depends on payment to achieve the meaning of sequence without difficulty? The answer is that the command is only entitled to a response with the estimation of 'if' if it makes the past tense and nominal sentence future in meaning when they are apparent. But if they are estimated, they do not become future in meaning, so it is not said: 'Come to me, I honored you' or 'you are honored,' as in Nur al-Anwar. (2) His statement: [The slave becomes free... etc.] because the fa entered on the cause; because freedom is continuous, so it becomes delayed after payment with respect to continuity, resembling the delay in the beginning, so entry of 'fa' upon it is valid. Thus its meaning becomes: 'Give me a thousand because you are free,' so freedom is not dependent on payment and it is actualized because there is no indication in the speech of suspension. Rather, fa was interpreted as for the cause because its literal meaning (conjunction) is impossible, since conjunction of a declarative sentence with an imperative sentence is not allowed, as in the following case, as in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage explains the use of the particle 'fa' to indicate causality (فاء التعليل). It begins by giving two examples: (1) a master telling his slave 'Give me a thousand dirhams, then you are free' – the slave becomes free immediately even without payment; (2) telling a harbi (non-Muslim from a warring territory) 'Descend, then you are safe' – he becomes safe immediately even without descending. The key principle, explained by Imam Fakhr al-Islam (Abu Bakr al-Sarakhsi), is that 'fa' can only enter on a cause that is continuous (دائم). The cause must exist both before and after the effect so that sequence (تعقيب) can be realized. For example, freedom is a continuous state; it existed before payment and remains after, so it can be the cause for the command to pay. Similarly, safety is continuous. The commentary from Nur al-Anwar and al-Ma'din elaborates on why the slave cannot be interpreted as having his freedom suspended on payment (i.e., 'if you pay, you are free'). The reason is that the imperative 'give' cannot have its response estimated as a conditional because the estimated 'if' only works with apparent future meaning expressions, not with estimated ones. Also, the literal meaning of 'fa' as conjunction is impossible here because conjoining a declarative (you are free) to an imperative (give) goes against Arabic grammar. Therefore, 'fa' is necessarily interpreted as for causality, resulting in immediate freedom.

Key Terms
فاء التعليل (fā' al-ta'līl): The letter fa that indicates the cause or reason for what precedes or follows
علة (ʿillah): A cause or reason that brings about a ruling
دوام (dawām): Continuity; the condition that the cause must be continuous before and after the effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his slave: 'أد إلي ألفا فأنت حر' (Give me a thousand dirhams, then you are free). According to the Hanafi analysis in this lesson, what is the status of the slave?
A) The slave becomes free only after paying a thousand dirhams
B) The slave becomes free immediately, and the thousand dirhams becomes a debt
C) The slave is not free until he actually pays
D) The statement is invalid because fa cannot follow an imperative

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' indicates causality if the cause is continuous; otherwise it indicates sequence or conditionality. Recognizing the condition of dawām is essential for correct derivation.

Delegation of Divorce with Fa: How Kinayah Determines Ruling
Ruling on divorce by delegation when the agent uses fa, resulting in one irrevocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the particle fa in delegating divorce and derive the resulting ruling (one irrevocable divorce) by applying Hanafi usul principles on kinayah and causality.

Core Idea
Fa on the ruling of the cause makes the divorce immediate and irrevocable when delegation is by kinayah.

When a husband delegates divorce to his wife using the phrase 'امرأتي بيدك فطلقها', the particle fa indicates that the divorce is the immediate effect of the delegation. The resulting divorce is one irrevocable (bain) divorce because the delegation phrase is a kinayah (ambiguous expression). In Hanafi usul, kinayah divorce always yields a single bain divorce. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrases and the role of fa
The husband says “امرأتي بيدك فطلقها”. The fa in “فطلقها” links the command to the delegation.
2
Classify the delegation phrase
“بيدك” is kinayah (ambiguous), not sarih (explicit). Hanafi law treats kinayah as only producing one bain divorce.
3
Apply the usul rule for kinayah
In Hanafi, a kinayah expression for divorce yields one irrevocable (bain) divorce. The fa here is for “clarifying the ruling of the cause” (بيان حكم العلة), so the divorce is immediate.
4
Handle the objection about sarih
The agent’s explicit utterance “طلقت” might suggest a revocable divorce, but the answer is that we consider the actual words of delegation, not the estimated meaning.
5
State the final ruling
One bain divorce occurs; any further pronouncement is not a separate delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرأتي بيدك فطلقها فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقة بائنة ولا يكون الثاني توكيلا بطلاق غير الأول فصار كأنه قال: «طلقها»
(3) قوله: [وفي الجامع... آه] ما سبق من الأمثلة من نظائر دخول الفاء على العلة، وهذه المسائل من نظائر دخول الفاء على حكم العلّة، ولهذا يقع الطلاق الواحد إذا طلقها؛ لأنّ «الفاء» لبيان حكم العلة فكان قوله: «فطلقها» أمراً بمباشرة ما فوّض إليه من الأمر باليد، كذا في "المعدن".
(4) قوله: [بائنة] لأنّ المفوّض بالأمر باليد هو البائن؛ لأنه كناية، ولا يقع بالكناية إلا الواحد البائن، كذا في "المعدن".
(5) قوله: [طلّقها ... إلخ] ولقائل أن يقول: إذا كان معنى هذا الكلام بسبب ... إلخ، فإذا طلقها فينبغي أن يكون الواقع رجعيًّا؛ لأنّ قوله: «طلّقها» صريح وأجيب بأنّ العبرة للكلام الملفوظ دون المقدّر.
My wife is in your hand, so divorce her. So he divorced her in the session; she is divorced with one irrevocable divorce, and the second [statement] is not a delegation for a different divorce, so it is as if he said: 'Divorce her.' (Footnote 3): His statement: [And in al-Jami'... etc.] The previous examples are from the analogues of the entry of fa upon the cause, and these issues are from the analogues of the entry of fa upon the ruling of the cause. For this reason, one divorce occurs when he divorces her, because the 'fa' is for clarifying the ruling of the cause, so his statement 'so divorce her' is a command to directly carry out what was delegated to him of the matter by hand, as in al-Ma'din. (Footnote 4): His statement: [Irrevocable] because what is delegated by the matter by hand is irrevocable divorce; because it is a kinayah (ambiguous expression), and only one irrevocable divorce occurs by kinayah, as in al-Ma'din. (Footnote 5): His statement: [Divorced her... etc.] And someone could say: If the meaning of this speech is 'because... etc.' then if he divorces her, the divorce should be revocable (raj'i) because his statement 'Divorce her' is explicit (sarih). The answer is that consideration is given to the spoken words, not the estimated ones.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage deals with the delegation (tafwid) of divorce. A husband says to his wife: 'My wife is in your hand, so divorce her' (امرأتي بيدك فطلقها). The word 'فطلقها' uses the fa of immediate sequence/causality. When the agent (in this case, the wife herself? Actually the delegation is to the wife; 'بيدك' means 'in your control' and 'فطلقها' is a command to divorce herself) then says 'I divorce her' in the same session, one irrevocable divorce (تطليقة بائنة) occurs. The second 'طلاق' (if she says it again) is not considered a separate delegation. The footnote from al-Ma'din explains that this falls under the entry of fa on the ruling of the cause. The fa in 'فطلقها' indicates that the divorce is the immediate ruling due to the delegation. The divorce is irrevocable (بائن) because the phrase 'بيدك' is a kinayah (ambiguous expression) in Hanafi law, and by kinayah only one irrevocable divorce takes effect. The fifth footnote addresses an objection: If the meaning is 'because of the delegation, divorce her,' then the divorce should be revocable (رجعی) because the explicit command 'طلقها' (divorce her) is sarih (explicit). The answer is that we consider the actual words spoken, not the estimated meaning; the delegation was already a kinayah, so the resulting divorce is bain.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the power to divorce to the wife or another person
كناية (kināyah): An ambiguous expression that can imply divorce but is not explicit
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without possibility of revocation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband tells his wife 'امرأتي بيدك فطلقها' and she says 'طلقت نفسي' in the same session, what type of divorce occurs according to the Hanafi position in this text?
A) One revocable (raj'i) divorce
B) One irrevocable (bain) divorce
C) Two divorces
D) No divorce because the command was not followed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the delegation of divorce by kinayah with fa always yields one irrevocable bain divorce, regardless of the agent's explicit utterance.

The Principle of Fa (فاء) Indicating Cause (Ta'lil) in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the usul rule of fa for cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the particle fa (فاء) indicates cause (illa) rather than sequence (ta'qib) in legal statements and derive the immediate ruling accordingly.

Core Idea
If literal sequence is impossible, fa indicates cause, making the ruling effective immediately without condition.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces a key Hanafi principle regarding the particle fa (فاء). While fa commonly denotes sequence or consequence, the Hanafis recognize that it can also indicate cause (illa) when the literal meaning of sequence is impossible. This has significant legal consequences, as demonstrated in two examples: manumission and safe conduct. Understanding this principle is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts where fa appears between a command and a declarative statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fa in the legal statement
Locate the particle fa and note its grammatical position—it typically connects a command to a declarative sentence.
2
Assess the possibility of literal sequence
Determine if the declarative sentence can logically follow the command as a consequence. If the declarative describes a continuous or immediate state (like freedom or safety), sequence is impossible because the state would have to pre-exist the command.
In the example, freedom is continuous, so it cannot begin after payment.
3
Reinterpret fa as indicating cause
When literal sequence is impossible, the Hanafi rule interprets fa as indicating cause (illa). The meaning becomes: 'Do X because Y is already true'.
Thus 'Pay a thousand, fa you are free' means 'Pay because you are free'.
4
Derive the immediate ruling
Since fa indicates cause, the ruling in the declarative sentence is immediate and unconditional. Any action mentioned in the command becomes a consequence of that existing state (e.g., freedom), often as a debt or obligation.
The slave is free immediately; the thousand becomes a debt.
5
Confirm by excluding conditional interpretation
Ensure that a conditional interpretation (with an implicit 'in') is not feasible. According to Hanafi usul, an implicit condition is only recognized if the fa would otherwise yield an impossible sequence. Here, an explicit condition is absent, so the fa must be for cause.
The commentary argues that an imperative does not automatically make the following statement conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الفاء للتعقيب

مسلم.

فأنت حر كان العبد حرا في الحال وإن لم يؤد شيئا، ولو قال للحربي: لأن المعنى إنزل من جهة أنك آمن. إنزل فأنت آمن كان آمنا وإن لم ينزل.

(1) قوله: [ كان العبد حرًا في الحال] فالحرية دائمة الوجود حيث كانت موجودة قبل الأداء وتبقى بعده إلى مدة فلا يتوقف على أداء الألف بل يكون حراً ويصير الألف ديناً على العبد الذي صار حرا، فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون تقديره : إن أديت فأنت حر، فيصير جواباً للأمر ويتوقف الحرية على الأداء لتحقق معنى التعقيب بلا تكلف أجيب بأن الأمر إنما يستحق الجواب بتقدير كلمة إن إنما تجعل الماضي والجملة الاسمية بمعنى المستقبل إذا كانت ظاهرة، أما إذا كانت مقدرة فلا تجعلها بمعنى المستقبل، فلا يقال : آتني أكرمتك أو أنت مكرم، كذا في نور الأنوار.

(2) قوله: [كان العبد حرا... إلخ] لأن الفاء دخلت على العلة؛ لأن الحرية دائم فيصير متراخياً عن الأداء نظراً إلى البقاء، فأشبه التراخي في الابتداء، فيصح دخول الفاء عليه، فصار معناه أد إلي ألفا؛ لأنك حر، فلا يتعلق العتق بالأداء ويتنجز العتق؛ لأنه لا دلالة في الكلام على التعليق، وإنما حملت الفاء على العلة لتعذر حقيقتها وهو العطف لما سبق أن عطف الخبرية على الطلبية غير جائز وكذلك المسألة الآتية، كذا في المعدن.
Chapter: The Fa for Sequence. Muslim. 'You are free' - the slave is free immediately even if he does not pay anything. And if he said to a warring enemy: because the meaning is 'descend because you are safe'. 'Descend, you are safe' - he is safe even if he does not descend. (1) He said: [the slave is free immediately] For freedom is continuous in existence, being present before payment and remaining afterwards for a duration, so it is not dependent on paying the thousand; rather he becomes free and the thousand becomes a debt on the slave who became free. If it is said: Why not interpret it as 'If you pay, you are free', making it a conditional response and freedom dependent on payment to achieve the meaning of sequence without strain? The answer is that a command only merits a response by the condition 'if' when it is explicit; if implicit, it does not turn the past tense or nominal sentence into future meaning. Thus one does not say 'Come to me, I honored you' or 'you are honored'. From Nur al-Anwar. (2) He said: [the slave is free...] Because the fa entered on the cause, since freedom is continuous, so it becomes delayed after payment with respect to continuance, resembling delay in initiation, so it is valid for fa to enter upon it. Thus its meaning becomes 'Pay me a thousand, because you are free', so the manumission is not conditional on payment and becomes effective immediately, because there is no indication in the speech of conditioning. The fa is only taken as indicating cause because its literal meaning (conjunction) is impossible, since conjunction of a declarative sentence after an imperative is not permissible, and similarly in the coming issue. From al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains the usul rule that the particle fa (فاء) can indicate cause (illa) rather than sequence (ta'qib). The author gives two examples: in 'if you pay a thousand, you are free', the fa is not for conditionality but for cause, so the slave becomes free immediately and the payment becomes a debt. Similarly, in 'descend, you are safe', the fa indicates that safety is the cause for descending, so even if he does not descend, he is safe. The commentary (hashiya) defends this interpretation by showing that the literal meaning of fa (consequence/sequence) is impossible here because the cause would have to follow the effect, but here the cause is continuous. Therefore, the fa must be for cause. The argument uses linguistic principles: an imperative does not automatically make a following statement conditional unless 'in' is explicit.

Key Terms
فاء (fā'): A particle that can indicate cause (illa) or sequence (ta'qib) depending on context; in legal language, it is interpreted as cause when literal sequence is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the fa indicates cause, not conditionality.
B) Because the payment is a condition and freedom is immediate.
C) Because the slave is already free by default.
D) Because the hadith of the Prophet proves it.

Key Takeaway
The particle fa can indicate cause (illa) when literal sequence is impossible, making the ruling effective immediately and unconditionally.

How the Particle Fa Determines the Number of Divorces in Tafwid al-Talaq
Cases where the use of fa versus waw determines the number and type of divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle that 'fa' indicates consequence to determine the number and type of divorces in delegation scenarios.

Core Idea
The particle 'fa' makes the second clause a consequence of the first; 'waw' makes them independent, changing the count of divorces.

In legal drafting, a single particle can change the entire outcome. The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'fa' and 'waw' in delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq). This lesson examines a passage from Usul al-Shashi that shows how the choice of conjunction determines whether one or two divorces occur, and whether they are revocable or irrevocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance structure
Read the husband's speech to the agent. It consists of two parts: the delegation phrase (e.g., 'your matter is in your hand') and the command phrase (e.g., 'divorce her'). Note the particle linking them (fa or waw).
The first phrase is the cause ('illa), the second is the ruling.
2
Apply the rule for 'fa'
If the particle is 'fa', the second phrase is a direct consequence (hukm al-illa) of the first. It does not create an independent authorization; it merely explains the first delegation. Thus, only one divorce is intended.
The text says: 'fa is for clarifying the ruling of the cause.'
3
Apply the rule for 'waw'
If the particle is 'waw', the two phrases are independent conjunctions. Each is a separate authorization, so the agent is authorized to perform two divorces.
Waw does not imply subordination; both phrases stand alone.
4
Determine the type of divorce
The first phrase (allusion/kinaya) yields one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The second phrase (explicit/sareeh) yields one revocable (raj'i) divorce. With 'waw', both are executed, but the revocable becomes irrevocable because it is combined with an irrevocable.
Explicit divorce is revocable by default; allusion is irrevocable.
5
Derive the final ruling
With 'fa': one irrevocable divorce. With 'waw': two divorces, both ultimately irrevocable. The commentary emphasizes that even if the revocable was intended, it transforms upon combination.
The outcome depends on the particle, not the agent's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع ما إذا قال: أمر الحربي. أي زوجتي. الوكيل بالطلاق. قيد به لأن التفويض مقصور على المجلس.

(3) قوله: [وفي الجامع... آه] ما سبق من الأمثلة من نظائر دخول الفاء على العلة، وهذه المسائل من نظائر دخول الفاء على حكم العلة، ولهذا يقع الطلاق الواحد إذا طلقها؛ لأن الفاء لبيان حكم العلة فكان قوله: فطلقها أمراً بمباشرة ما فوض إليه من الأمر باليد، كذا في المعدن.

(4) قوله: [بائنة] لأن المفوض بالأمر باليد هو البائن؛ لأنه كناية، ولا يقع بالكناية إلا الواحد البائن، كذا في المعدن.

(5) قوله: [طلقها ... إلخ] ولقائل أن يقول: إذا كان معنى هذا الكلام بسبب ... إلخ، فإذا طلقها فينبغي أن يكون الواقع رجعياً؛ لأن قوله: طلقها صريح وأجيب بأن العبرة للكلام الملفوظ دون المقدر.

بسبب أن أمرها بيدك، ولو قال: طلقها فجعلت أمرها بيدك فطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقة رجعية، ولو قال: طلقها وجعلت أمرها بيدك بالواو وطلقها في المجلس طلقت تطليقتين، وكذلك لو قال: طلقها وابنها أو أحدهما رجعي في الصريح والآخر بائن بالكناية.

ابنها وطلقها فطلقها في المجلس وقعت تطليقتان.

(1) قوله [بسبب أن أمرها بيدك ] فكان الثاني وهو قوله: فطلقها هو الطلاق المفوض ولا يكون توكيلاً بالطلاق غير الأول فلا تقع إلا واحدة ولو كان للعطف تقع تطليقتان.

(2) قوله : [ طلقت... إلخ] أي: يقع في هذه الصورة الطلاق الواحد الرجعي؛ لأنه تفويض لصريح الطلاق وهو رجعي والأمر باليد بيان له.

(3) قوله: [تطليقتين] لأن قوله : طلقها توكيل الصريح الطلاق، وقوله : جعلت أمرها بيدك تفويض الطلاق إليه لكنه ليس بحكم الأول؛ لأن الواو لا يحتمل ذلك فكان الثاني غير الأول فصار المأمور وكيلاً بتطليقتين إحداهما بائن وهو الأمر باليد كناية والأخرى رجعي؛ لأنه صريح فإذا قال في المجلس: طلقتها فقد أتى بما وكل به وهو الطلقتان فوقعتا، لكنه يكون كلاهما بائنين؛ لأن الرجعي يصير بائناً مع البائن؛ لأنه إذا وقع البائن فلا رجعة بعده ترجيحاً للمحرم، كذا في المعدن.
And in al-Jami' concerning when he said: the matter of the warring enemy. i.e., my wife. the agent with divorce. restricted because delegation is confined to the assembly. (3) He said: [And in al-Jami'...] The preceding examples are similar to fa entering on the cause, and these issues are similar to fa entering on the ruling of the cause, and therefore one divorce occurs if he divorces her; because the fa is for clarifying the ruling of the cause, so his saying 'so divorce her' is a command to directly execute what was delegated to him of the matter by hand. From al-Ma'din. (4) He said: [irrevocable] because what is delegated by the matter by hand is irrevocable, since it is by allusion (kinaya), and by allusion only one irrevocable divorce occurs. From al-Ma'din. (5) He said: [divorce her...] One might say: if the meaning of this speech is 'because...', then when he divorces her, it should be revocable because his saying 'divorce her' is explicit. The answer is that consideration is given to the spoken words, not the implied. ... because her matter is in your hand.' And if he said: 'Divorce her and I have placed her matter in your hand' with the waw, and he divorces her in the assembly, two divorces occur. Similarly, if he said: 'Divorce her and her son and divorce her' in the assembly, two divorces occur. (1) He said [because...]: So the second, which is his saying 'so divorce her', is the delegated divorce and is not a different agency for divorce, so only one occurs; if it were for conjunction, two would occur. (2) He said [divorced...]: i.e., in this case one revocable divorce occurs, because it is a delegation for explicit divorce which is revocable, and the matter by hand explains it. (3) He said [two divorces]: because his saying 'divorce her' is agency for explicit divorce, and his saying 'I have placed her matter in your hand' is a delegation of divorce to him, but it is not a ruling of the first because the waw does not imply that, so the second is different from the first. Thus the agent has agency for two divorces: one irrevocable (the allusion) and one revocable (the explicit). When he says in the assembly 'I have divorced her', he has executed what he was authorized for, so both occur. However, both become irrevocable because the revocable becomes irrevocable with the irrevocable, since when the irrevocable occurs, there is no return thereafter, giving precedence to prohibition. From al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage applies the usul rule about fa to specific fiqh cases of divorce delegation (tafwid al-talaq). When a husband tells his wife's agent 'Your matter is in your hand, so divorce her' (with fa), the fa indicates that the second phrase ('so divorce her') is the consequence of the first. Thus, one irrevocable divorce falls because the delegation is by allusion (kinaya). If instead he uses 'and' (waw), both parts are independent, resulting in two divorces (one by explicit word, one by allusion). The commentary explains the reasoning: with fa, the second clause is the delegated divorce itself, not an additional authorization. With waw, both clauses are separate authorizations. This shows how the choice of particle affects the legal outcome.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkeel): Appointing an agent to perform an act; in this context, appointing someone to divorce on one's behalf.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority to another, particularly the power to divorce; in the text, it refers to giving the wife or agent the choice to divorce.
صريح (sareeh): Explicit wording for divorce (e.g., 'I divorce you'), which produces a revocable divorce.
كناية (kinaya): Allusive wording for divorce (e.g., 'your matter is in your hand'), which produces one irrevocable divorce if intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband says to the agent 'طلقها وجعلت أمرها بيدك' (with waw), how many divorces occur when the agent divorces in the assembly?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) One irrevocable divorce.
C) Two divorces, one revocable and one irrevocable, both becoming irrevocable.
D) Two divorces, both revocable.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' binds the following clause as a consequence, resulting in one divorce; 'waw' creates independent authorizations, resulting in two divorces.

Deriving the Option of the Freed Slave Woman: The Fa as a Causal Particle
Deriving the option of the freed slave woman from the hadith using the fa for cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that al-fa li al-tasbīb (the fa indicates causation) to derive a fiqh ruling from a hadith, and compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i readings of the hadith.

Core Idea
The fa in 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري' proves ownership causes choice; therefore the option applies generally.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi uses a hadith to illustrate how the particle fa functions as a causal indicator. The Hanafis argue that the fa connects the ownership of the private part to the choice, making ownership the cause (illa) for the option. This leads to a general ruling applicable whether the husband is free or slave, in contrast to the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key textual element
Locate the hadith phrase used in the passage: 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري'. This is the primary text from which the ruling will be derived.
The hadith is from Sunan Abu Dawud.
2
Analyze the function of fa
Examine the letter fa (ف) in 'فاختاري'. Determine whether it indicates mere sequence (al-fa li al-ta'qīb) or causation (al-fa li al-tasbīb). The Hanafis adopt the latter based on usul principles.
The commentary explicitly states that the fa here makes the preceding a cause for the following.
3
Determine the cause (illa)
Identify the content before the fa: 'ملكت بضعك' (you own your private part). This ownership is the cause for the choice that follows. Recognize that the cause is general – it applies to any owner of a private part upon manumission.
The illa is milk al-bud' (ownership of the private part).
4
Apply the cause to the scenario
Once the cause is identified, apply it to the case at hand: a married slave woman upon being freed owns her private part, so she has the choice (khiyar) to annul or keep the marriage. Since the cause does not depend on the husband's status, the option applies whether he is free or slave.
This general application refutes the Shafi'i restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قال أصحابنا: إذا أعتقت الأمة المنكوحة ثبت لها الخيار سواء كان زوجها عبدا أو حرا؛ لأن قوله عليه السلام لبريدة: حين أعتقت: ملكت بضعك فاختاري، أثبت ملك البضع للاختيار لا بالعكس.

(4) قوله: [وعلى هذا ... إلخ] أي: على ما قلنا: إن الفاء لترتب ما بعدها على ما قبلها وما قبلها علة لما بعدها لا على ما قاله قريباً إنها قد تكون لبيان العلة كما توهم، فإن المسألة غير متفرعة عليه؛ لأن العلة هاهنا هو ملك البضع للاختيار لا بالعكس كذا في الحصول.

(5) قوله: [سواء كان... إلخ] لأن علة ثبوت الخيار ملك البضع وعدم الكفاءة وهما لا يوجدان فيما إذا كان زوجها حراً فلا يثبت لها الخيار، وفي التعميم إشارة إلى الرد على الشافعي رحمه الله، حيث لا يقول بالتعميم بل يخصص الحكم لو كان زوجها عبداً، كذا في المعدن.
And upon this our companions said: When a married slave woman is freed, she has the option whether her husband is a slave or free; because the Prophet ﷺ said to Buraidah when his wife was freed: 'You own your private part, so choose.' This established that owning the private part is for choice, not the reverse. (4) He said: [and upon this...] i.e., on what we said: that the fa indicates that what follows is based on what precedes, and what precedes is the cause for what follows, not as he said earlier that it may be for clarifying the cause, as imagined. For the issue is not derived from that; because the cause here is the ownership of the private part for choice, not the reverse. From al-Husul. (5) He said: [whether...] because the cause for establishing the option is the ownership of the private part and the lack of compatibility (kafa'a). Both are not present when the husband is free, so she does not have the option. In the generalization there is an indication of refutation of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, who does not generalize but restricts the ruling to when the husband is a slave. From al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage derives the fiqh ruling that a married slave woman, upon being freed, has the option (khiyar) to annul her marriage or keep it. The source is the hadith of Buraidah, where the Prophet said: 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري' (You own your private part, so choose). The fa here indicates that the ownership of her private part is the cause (illa) for the choice. Therefore, the Hanafis hold that the option applies whether the husband is free or slave, based on the generality of the cause. The commentary notes that this refutes Imam al-Shafi'i, who restricts the option to cases where the husband is a slave. Footnote (5) clarifies that the cause for option is the ownership of the private part and the lack of compatibility; when the husband is free, compatibility is present, so the option does not apply – but this seems to contradict the main text. Actually, the footnote explains the basis for the generalization: the Hanafis argue that the cause is ownership, not compatibility, and thus they generalize, while Shafi'i requires both ownership and lack of compatibility. The generalization itself is the refutation of Shafi'i.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The option given to a freed slave woman to annul her marriage or keep it.
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing of a slave.
ملك البضع (milk al-bud'): Ownership of the private part; a woman's right over her own body after manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that a freed slave woman has the option to annul her marriage regardless of whether her husband is free or slave?
A) Consensus of the companions
B) The hadith 'ملكت بضعك فاختاري' with the fa indicating causation
C) A verse from the Quran
D) Analogy to divorce

Key Takeaway
The fa in the hadith is interpreted as causal by the Hanafis, making ownership the cause for the option, leading to a general ruling.

The Meaning of 'Thumma' and Its Effect in Conditional Divorce
Explains the linguistic meaning of 'thumma' as delaying and the intra-madhhab disagreement on the scope of that delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Sahibayn interpretations of 'thumma' and apply them to conditional divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
Thumma indicates delay — but does that delay apply to speech or only to legal effect? The answer determines how conditional divorces fall.

In this lesson, we examine the usul al-fiqh treatment of the Arabic conjunction 'thumma'. While it universally denotes delay, the Hanafi school splits on whether that delay applies to the pronunciation (lafz) and ruling (hukm) together, or only to the ruling. This subtle linguistic disagreement has real consequences in conditional divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Define 'thumma' and its core meaning
Understand that 'thumma' indicates tarakhi (delay) between the conjoined items in the action. For example, 'Zayd came, then Amr' means Amr's coming is later.
The delay can be in time or in legal effect.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that 'thumma' implies delay in both pronunciation (lafz) and ruling (hukm), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the delay is only in the ruling, not in pronunciation.
3
Analyze the linguistic arguments
For Abu Hanifa, the perfection of delay requires both aspects; for the Sahibayn, conjunction requires connection in speech, so only the occurrence is delayed.
Key: validity of conjunction is based on form (surah), not actual gap.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a husband saying to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced.' Determine the outcome under each view.
Non-consummated wife: divorce is effective immediately and counts as one; after first divorce she is no longer a wife.
5
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Abu Hanifa: first divorce conditional, second falls immediately, third void. Sahibayn: all three conditional on entry, falling in order but only first effective if non-consummated.
The practical difference: immediate vs. delayed second divorce.

But we say: The validity of conjunction is based on connection in form, and that exists. (5) His saying: [And the second occurs immediately...] because the second and third are mentioned with the word 'thumma', so it is as if he remained silent after the first and started anew with them, so they are not attached to the condition. The second occurs immediately because the place exists, and the third is void because the place is no longer present (non-consummated wife becomes divorced by the second). (6) His saying: [All are attached to entry] meaning all are attached to the condition, because connection in speech is realized according to them and there is no separation in the expression, so all are attached to the condition whether the condition is delayed or advanced. However, at the time of occurrence they occur in order. If she is consummated, three fall; if not, the first falls and she becomes divorced, and the second and third do not occur due to lack of place.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul rule regarding the particle 'thumma'. It first defines 'thumma' as indicating delay (tarakhi) between the conjoined items in the action. There is a disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that 'thumma' implies delay in both the pronunciation (lafz) and the ruling (hukm), meaning that when you say 'then', you should also speak with a pause, and the legal effect is delayed accordingly. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the delay is only in the ruling, not in pronunciation; the two items can be spoken continuously. The example given is a conditional divorce for a non-consummated wife: if the husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' then according to Abu Hanifa, only the first 'divorced' is conditional on entry; the second occurs immediately (since it is not connected to the condition) and the third is void (since after the second divorce the wife is already divorced, so no place for a third). According to the Sahibayn, all three are conditional on entry because the speech is continuous; so if the condition occurs, all three fall in sequence. The commentary further explains the reasoning and the four scenarios (depending on whether the wife is consummated and whether the condition is advanced or delayed).

Key Terms
ثُمّ (thumma): A conjunction particle indicating delay (tarakhi) between the conjoined items in the action.
تراخي (tarakhi): A gap or interval in time or legal effect between two events or rulings.
لفظ (lafz): Pronunciation or wording; the actual spoken utterance.
حكم (hukm): Ruling or legal effect; the actual occurrence of the action or its consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what happens when a husband says to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced'?
A) All three are conditional on entry; the first takes effect upon entry, and the second and third are void due to lack of place.
B) The first is conditional, the second occurs immediately, and the third is void.
C) All three are conditional on entry; all three take effect upon entry.
D) The first is conditional, and the second and third are void.

Key Takeaway
The usul dispute over 'thumma' — whether delay applies to pronunciation and ruling or only to ruling — directly dictates the outcome of conditional divorce cases involving the particle.

The Hanafi Dispute over 'Thumma' in Conditional Divorce: Utterance vs. Effect
Usul dispute with expansion on conditional talaq scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the effect of 'thumma' in conditional utterances according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the dispute to derive the legal consequences in conditional divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
'Thumma' may delay either the utterance alone or the ruling alone — the dispute changes how conditional divorces are counted.

This passage explores a key usul dispute about the conjunction 'thumma' (then) in conditional divorce. Abu Hanifa held that it delays both the utterance and the legal effect, while his two main disciples argued it delays only the legal effect, keeping the utterance connected. This seemingly subtle linguistic difference leads to dramatically different outcomes in practical talaq cases. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind each position and trace its application to four scenarios of conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic operation of 'thumma'
Determine whether 'thumma' indicates delay (tarakhi) in utterance, legal effect, or both, based on the arguments presented.
Focus on the distinction between lafz (utterance) and hukm (ruling).
2
State the two positions
Clearly articulate Abu Hanifa's view (delay in both) and the Sahibayn's view (delay only in ruling).
Memorize the positions as they are foundational.
3
Examine the proofs
Analyze the dalil for each position: Abu Hanifa's emphasis on the perfection of delay, the Sahibayn's emphasis on the nature of conjunction (عطف) requiring connection.
Note that the Sahibayn argue that separation would invalidate the conjunction.
4
Apply to the four scenarios
Work through the four cases: unconsummated vs. consummated wife, condition before or after the divorces. Derive the different outcomes for each position.
Track how many divorces occur and when (immediate or upon condition).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: the number of divorces and their timing differ between the views.
This difference matters for iddah, remarriage eligibility, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ثم للتراخي لكنه عند أبي حنيفة يفيد التراخي في اللفظ والحكم جميعا. وعندهما يفيد التراخي في الحكم، وبيانه فيما إذا قال لغير المدخول بها: «إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق» فعنده يتعلق الأولى بالدخول وتقع الثانية في الحال ولغت الثالثة وعندهما يتعلق الكل بالدخول، ثم عند أبي حنيفة في الدخول يظهر الترتيب فلا يقع إلا واحدة، ولو قال: «أنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق إن دخلت الدار» فعند أبي حنيفة وقعت الأولى في الحال ولغت الثانية والثالثة، وعندهما يقع الواحدة عند الدخول وإن كانت المرأة مدخولا بها، فإن قدم الشرط تعلقت الأولى بالدخول ويقع ثنتان في الحال عند أبي حنيفة وإن أخر الشرط وقع ثنتان في الحال وتعلقت الثالثة بالدخول وعندهما يتعلق الكل بالدخول في الفصلين.
(1) قوله: [«ثُمّ» للتراخي] وهو أن يكون بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه مهلة في الفعل المتعلّق بهما، فإذا قلت: «جاءني زيد ثُمّ عمرو»، أو قلت: «ضربت زيداً ثُمّ عمرواً كان مجيء عمرو وضربه متراخياً عن مجيء زيد وضربه كذا في "التحقيق".
(2) قوله: [يفيد التراخي في اللفظ والحكم جميعاً؛ لأنّ هذه الكلمة لَمّا وضعت للتراخي، والأصل في كلّ شيء كماله وكمال التراخي أن يكون في اللفظ والحكم جميعاً؛ إذ لو كان التراخي في الحكم دون التكلّم كما قال الصاحبان لكان التراخي موجوداً من وجه دون وجه، كذا في "المعدن".
(3) قوله : [ عندهما يفيد التراخي في الحكم] أي: في وجود الفعل المتعلّق بالمعطوف والمعطوف عليه الوصل في التكلّم رعايةً لمعنى العطف فيه؛ لأنّ العطف لا يصح مع الانفصال؛ وهذا لأنّ الكلام متصل حقيقة وحسا فلا معنی للانفصال، لكنّا نقول : صحة العطف مبنية على الاتصال صورةً وذا موجود، كذا في "المعدن".
(4) قوله: [وبيانه... إلخ] أي: بيان الاختلاف بين أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه: وجه الحصر في الأربعة؛ لأنه إما إن علّق الطلاق بكلمة «ثُمّ» في غير المدخول بها أو المدخول بها، وفي كل واحد إما إن أخر الشرط أو قدّمه، وتفصيل هذه الأوجه مع الأمثلة مذكور في المتن، كذا قيل.
(5) قوله: [وتقع الثانية... إلخ] لأنّ الثانية والثالثة مذكورتان بكلمة «ثُمّ»، فصار كأنه سكت عن الأوّل استانف بهما فلا يتعلقان بالشرط وتقع الثانية في الحال لوجود المحلّ ولغت الثالثة لانتفاء المحلّ؛ لأنها غير مدخول بها فتبين بالثانية فقط.
(6) قوله: [ يتعلّق الكلّ بالدخول] أي: يتعلّق الكل بالشرط؛ لأنّ الوصل في التكلّم متحقق عندهما ولا فصل في العبارة فيتعلّق الكلّ بالشرط سواء قدّم الشرط أو أخره ، ولكن في وقت الوقوع ينزلن على الترتيب فإن كانت مدخولاً بها يقع الثلاث وإن لم تكن مدخولاً بما يقع الأول وبانت به ولا يقع الثاني والثالث لعدم المحل.
[والصفحة التالية:] ولو قال: «أنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق إن دخلت الدار» فعند أبي حنيفة وقعت الأولى في الحال ولغت الثانية والثالثة لأنها بانت بالأولى. وعندهما يقع الواحدة عند الدخول. وإن كانت المرأة مدخولا بها، فإن قدم الشرط تعلقت الأولى بالدخول ويقع ثنتان في الحال، وإن أخر الشرط وقع ثنتان في الحال وتعلقت الثالثة بالدخول. وعندهما يتعلق الكل بالدخول في الفصلين.
(1) قوله: [ولو قال... إلخ] هذا هو الوجه الثاني وهو أنه إذا أخر الشرط وهو أنه لو قال: «أنت طالق ثُمّ طالق ثُمّ طالق إن دخلت الدار» فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله يقع الأوّل ويلغو ما بعده؛ لأنّ التراخي لما كان في التكلّم كأنه قال: «أنت طالق وسكت على هذا القدر فوقع هذا الطلاق فلما بانت بالأولى ولم يبق محلاً لما بعده لأنها غير موطوءة فيلغو الثاني والثالث، وعندهما تقع الواحدة عند الدخول لما ذكرنا آنفاً.
(2) قوله: [تعلّقت الأولى... إلخ ] أي: إذا قال للمدخول بها: «إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق... آه» أعني: يقدّم الشرط فتعلّقت الأولى بالشرط وتقع الثانية والثالثة في الحال عند الإمام لتجردهما عن التعليق لانفصالهما عن الشرط.
(3) قوله: [في الفصلين] أي: في تقديم الشرط وتأخيره لاتصال الكلام مع كلمة «ثُمّ»، فإن قيل على قول الإمام في صورة تقديم الشرط في المدخول بها وغيرها: إذا تعلقت الأولى بالشرط ينبغي أن لا تقع الثانية والثالثة بل يجب أن يلغو؛ لأنّ كلام الثاني لَمّا انقطع عن الأوّل حتى لا يتعلّق بالشرط لا يشارك الأوّل فيما يتم به، وهي كلمة ذلك كالمعاد فيه أيضاً؛ لأنّ ذلك إنما يثبت بشرط الاتصال وهو معدوم فبقي قوله: «ثُمّ طالق» كلام مبتدأ ولو استانف ذلك حقيقةً لا يقع شيء فكذا إذا استانفه حكماً؛ لأنّ الحكمي ملحق بالحقيقي، قلت: على الاتصال وذلك موجود هاهنا فصار المبتدأ كالمعاد في المعطوف فيقع به الثاني ولا يلغو، ولهذا اختص بحرف الفاء الذي يوجب الوصل حتى لو قال: «إن دخلت الدار وأنت طالق» لا يتعلّق بالشرط، وتوضيحه أنه لو قال: «إن دخلت الدار... إلخ» لا يتعلّق الثاني والثالث بالشرط لعدم ما يوجب التعليق وهو حرف العطف، لكن تثبت له الشركة فيما يتمّ به الأوّل الاتصال صورةً، ويمكن ذلك بدون حرف العطف مثل: «زيد عالم عاقل فقيه» بأن جعل خبراً بعد خبر فيقعان في الحال كذا في "الفصول" و "المعدن" مختصراً. صحة العطف مبني
Chapter: 'Thumma' is for delay. But according to Abu Hanifa, it indicates delay in both wording and ruling. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it indicates delay in the ruling only. Its explanation: if he says to an unconsummated wife: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' then according to him (Abu Hanifa), the first is attached to entry, the second occurs immediately, and the third is void. According to the two, all are attached to entry. Then according to Abu Hanifa, upon entry the order appears so only one occurs. And if he said: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house,' then according to Abu Hanifa the first occurred immediately and the second and third are void. According to the two, one occurs upon entry. And if the woman is consummated, and he presents the condition, the first is attached to entry and two occur immediately according to Abu Hanifa; and if he delays the condition, two occur immediately and the third is attached to entry. According to the two, all are attached to entry in both cases. (Footnote 1: 'Thumma' for delay means there is a period between the conjoined and the conjoined-to in the related action. For example, 'Zaid came then Amr' or 'I hit Zaid then Amr' – Amr's coming or hitting is delayed after Zaid's. (Footnote 2: It indicates delay in both wording and ruling because this word was placed for delay, and the perfection of delay is in both. If delay were only in ruling without speech, as the two said, it would be incomplete. (Footnote 3: The two say it indicates delay in ruling only, maintaining connection in speech out of regard for the meaning of conjunction, because conjunction cannot be with separation. But we say the validity of conjunction is based on formal connection, which exists. (Footnote 6: All are attached to the condition according to the two, because connection in speech is realized, so all attach to the condition, but they occur in order. [Next page:] And if he said: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house' – according to Abu Hanifa the first occurs immediately and the second and third are void. According to the two, one occurs upon entry. And if the woman is consummated and he presents the condition, the first attaches to entry and two occur immediately; if he delays the condition, two occur immediately and the third attaches to entry. According to the two, all attach to entry in both cases. (Footnote 1: This is the second aspect: when he delays the condition, according to Abu Hanifa the first occurs and what follows is void. (Footnote 2: When he presents the condition for a consummated wife, the first attaches to the condition and the second and third occur immediately at the Imam's view because they are free from attachment due to separation from the condition. (Footnote 3: In both cases – presenting and delaying – due to the connection of speech with 'thumma'. A question: on the Imam's view, when the first attaches to the condition, the second and third should be void? Answer: connection exists, so the second does not void. The validity of conjunction is based on formal connection.)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute concerning the meaning of the Arabic word 'ثم' (thumma) in legal contexts, particularly in conditional divorce (talaq). It presents the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Sahibayn), on whether 'thumma' indicates delay (tarakhi) in both utterance (lafz) and legal effect (hukm) or only in legal effect. The matn states Abu Hanifa's position that 'thumma' implies delay in both, while the two say it implies delay only in the ruling. The hashiya expands with detailed examples: if a husband says to an unconsummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' Abu Hanifa rules that only the first divorce is suspended on entry, the second occurs immediately (since it is not attached to the condition), and the third is void because the wife becomes irrevocably divorced after the second. The two, however, hold that all three are suspended on entry because the speech remains connected. The dispute stems from a linguistic principle: Abu Hanifa views 'thumma' as implying a pause (tarakhi) in both speech and ruling, so phrases after 'thumma' are like new sentences. The two maintain that the conjunction requires connection, so all parts share the same condition. The hashiya also discusses cases with a consummated wife and different orders of condition and divorce statements. This usul rule affects how multiple divorce pronouncements are counted. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) is that of the two in later fatwa? The text does not explicitly state tarjih, but the detailed defense suggests the two's view is stronger.

Key Terms
ثُمَّ (thumma): A conjunction indicating delay (tarakhi) in the action or ruling
تَرَاخِي (tarākhī): The quality of being later or delayed; the opposite of immediate
لَفْظ (lafẓ): The spoken utterance or wording itself
حُكْم (ḥukm): The legal ruling or effect derived from the utterance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says to his unconsummated wife, 'You are divorced, then divorced if you enter the house,' what is the ruling?
A) First divorce occurs immediately, second divorce occurs upon entry
B) First divorce occurs immediately, second divorce is void
C) Both divorces occur upon entry
D) First divorce is void, second divorce occurs upon entry

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'thumma' as a conjunction in conditional divorce determines whether multiple pronouncements are treated as separate or connected, affecting the number of divorces that take effect.

The Particle 'Lakin' for Istidrak: Legal Application
Analyzing the legal effect of 'lakin' in connecting or separating speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a statement using 'lakin' is connected (wasl) or separated (fasl) and determine the legal effect on rights and obligations in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
When 'lakin' connects speech, it modifies the prior statement's scope; when separated, it starts a new independent statement.

This lesson examines the usul principle of interpreting the particle 'lakin' when used for istidrak (correction). In Hanafi usul, the distinction between a connected and disconnected 'lakin' determines whether two statements are considered one coherent unit or separate, directly impacting legal outcomes in cases of acknowledgment (iqrar) and other speech acts. The passage from al-Shashi's Usul, with commentary, illustrates this through examples from al-Jami'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes: wasl and fasl
Understand that 'lakin' can either be attached immediately to the prior speech (wasl) or be part of a new sentence after a pause (fasl). The legal outcome depends on this distinction.
2
Analyze the debt example
In the example from al-Jami', the first person acknowledges a loan debt; the second says 'No, but it is usurpation.' Because 'lakin' is connected, the negation applies only to the cause (loan), not the debt itself, so the debt stands.
The speech is consistent ('mutasqin') because both agree on the principal amount.
3
Analyze the slave example with connection
When the addressee says 'It was never mine, but it is for X' without a gap, the two parts are one statement: he denies ownership for himself and immediately affirms it for X. Thus X owns the slave.
Connection creates a single coherent statement.
4
Analyze the slave example with separation
If he pauses after 'It was never mine' and then says 'but it is for X', the first part stands alone as a denial, and the second is a separate statement that does not transfer ownership. The slave remains with the first person.
Separation makes the second statement independent; the denial is not overridden.
5
Derive the usul principle
The principle: when 'lakin' is used for istidrak, if it is connected, the negation and affirmation are part of one coherent utterance, so the negation attaches to the affirmation's subject (often the cause). If separated, the two statements are independent, and the negation stands alone.
This principle prevents contradiction and preserves practical coherence.

مجلس: "المدينة العلمية جمعيّة الدعوة الإسلامية
— (201).
Chapter: 'Lakin' for Istidrak. 12. Musta'naf (disconnected). Example: What Muhammad (may mercy be upon him) mentioned in al-Jami': If someone says: 'So-and-so owes me a thousand [dirhams] as a loan', and the other says: 'No, but it is usurpation', the money is incumbent upon him, because the speech is consistent. Thus it appears that the negation was regarding the cause, not the principal itself. Similarly, if he says: 'So-and-so owes me a thousand [dirhams] from the price of this slave-girl', and the other says: 'No, the slave-girl is your slave-girl, but I owe you a thousand [dirhams] as a loan', the money is incumbent. So it appears that the negation was regarding the cause, not the principal. And if there is a slave in his hand and he says: 'This is for So-and-so', then So-and-so says: 'It was never mine, but it is for another person So-and-so' — if he connects the speech, the slave is for the second person; because the negation relates to the affirmation. And if he separates, the slave is for the first person; thus the statement of the first addressee becomes a rejection of the acknowledgment. And a slave-girl married herself without her master's permission for one hundred dirhams, and the master said: 'I do not authorize the marriage,' then he has negated her contract. The commentary elaborates on these examples.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the legal implications of the particle 'lakin' when used for istidrak (correction or exception). The matn (by al-Shashi) states that when someone acknowledges a debt specifying a cause (e.g., loan) and the addressee denies that cause but affirms the debt under a different cause (e.g., usurpation), the speech is considered consistent ('mutasqin') because both agree on the debt itself; the negation only applies to the cause. Similarly, in the slave case, the connection or separation of speech using 'lakin' determines who owns the slave. If the addressee says 'It was never mine but it is for X' without a gap, it is treated as one statement: he denies ownership for himself and immediately affirms it for X, so X gets the slave. If he pauses (separates), his denial stands alone, and the later affirmation is a separate statement that does not transfer ownership. The commentary (hashiya) explains the reasoning: the aim is to preserve consistency in speech; when connected, the negation attaches to the affirmation's subject, preventing contradiction. The example of the slave-girl marrying herself shows that if the master says 'I do not authorize the marriage' and then says 'but I authorize it with 150 dirhams', this is contradictory because he negates and affirms the same action. Therefore, 'lakin' here is interpreted as starting a new statement (musta'naf), not connecting, to avoid contradiction. All examples follow Hanafi legal reasoning prioritizing practical coherence over literal contradiction.

Key Terms
لكن (lakin): A particle used for istidrak (correction/exception); it can connect speech (with prior) or start a new sentence (musta'naf).
استدراك (istidrak): A rhetorical device to correct or add an exception to a preceding statement.
فصل (fasl): Separation in speech; when a pause or disconnection occurs between two statements.
وصل (wasl): Connection in speech; when the second statement is immediately joined to the first without pause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The second person's denial cancels the debt entirely.
B) The debt remains, but the cause changes from loan to usurpation.
C) The first person must pay a reduced amount.
D) The case is dismissed due to contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle of 'lakin' for istidrak dictates that connection creates a single coherent statement modifying the cause, while separation creates a new independent statement, affecting rights in acknowledgments and similar speech acts.

Rule of 'Lakinna' in Connected vs Disconnected Iqrar
States the ruling of 'lakinna' in iqrar when speech is connected or disconnected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of 'lakinna' to determine ownership in iqrar based on whether the speech is connected or disconnected.

Core Idea
In iqrar, 'lakinna' connects to the preceding negation only if speech is continuous; a break makes it a new independent statement.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the particle 'lakinna' (but) plays a crucial role in modifying statements of acknowledgment. The legal effect depends on whether the speech is continuous or interrupted. This lesson examines that distinction and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the iqrar structure
An iqrar typically involves a person (muqir) who first negates ownership then affirms it for another using 'lakinna'. The statement must be parsed into its components.
2
Check for wasl or fasl
Determine whether the speech was uttered continuously (wasl) or with a pause (fasl). This is a linguistic and contextual assessment.
3
Apply the rule
If wasl: the latter part overrides, so ownership goes to the second person. If fasl: the former part stands, and the latter is treated as a separate testimony by an individual.
4
Understand the reasoning
In wasl, the negation and affirmation are linked, making the statement consistent. In fasl, the break disrupts consistency, so the negation remains absolute and the affirmation is weakened.

المقر الأول. ١٢ بلكن المقترن بما قبله بلا فصل. ۱۲ فلان آخر» فإن وصل الكلام كان العبد للمقر له الثاني؛ لأن النفي يتعلق بالإثبات قوله ولكنه لفلان آخر. ۱۲ (۲) المذكور. ١٢ (۳) في كلام المقرله الأول الأول وهو ما كان لي قط. ۱۲ وهو قوله ما كان لي. ١٢ وإن فصل كان العبد للمقر الأول فيكون قول المقر له ردًّا للإقرار، ولو أن المذكور. ١٢ أي بنفسها. ١٢ في المهر. ١٢ وتكذيبًا. ١٢

(۱) قوله: [فإن وصل... إلخ يكون الكلام متسقاً؛ لأنّ مدار الاتساق على ما قيل مجموع أمرين: الاتصال بالسابق في التكلّم وعدم تعلّق النفي والإثبات بشيء بعينه، حتى لا يبقى التناقض والتدافع ولو بحسب الظاهر فقط، فعند فقدان أحد الأمرين لا يبقى الاتساق بل يعدّ كلاماً مستأنفاً، فتدبّر. ۱۲ (۲) قوله: [للمقرّ الأوّل] وهو من في يده العبد؛ لأنّ المقرّ له الأوّل إذا فصل وقطعه كلامه كان نفياً لملكه مطلقاً أي: نفياً عن نفسه أصلاً لا نفياً إلى أحد بخلاف ما إذا وصل، فإنّه وإن كان شهادة الفرد، لكنّه ا أقرّ بالملك للغير متصلاً بالنفي عن نفسه صار الكل بمنزلة كلام واحد، فيكون تقديم الإقـــرار وتأخيره سواء، فيجعل كأنه قدم الإقرار بالملك لفلان صيانةً لكلام العاقل عن الإلغاء، كذا في "المعدن". ۱۲ (۳) قوله: [قول المقرّ له وهو قوله: «ما كان لي قطّ في صورة الفصل ردّاً للإقرار وتكذيباً للمقرّ حملاً للكلام على الظاهر، ويكون قوله: «لكنه لفلان» بعد ذلك شهادة بملك الثالث على ذي اليد، وشهادة الفرد لا يثبت الملك لا سيما إذا كان بلا دعوى الملك فبقى العبد ملكاً له، كذا في "المعدن". ۱۲
Chapter: 'Lakinna' for correction. The first confirmer: 'But the one connected to what precedes without a break...' So if the speech is connected, the slave belongs to the second confirmer because the negation is attached to the affirmation mentioned in the speech of the first confirmer. If disconnected, the slave belongs to the first confirmer, so the statement of the confirmee becomes a rejection of the confirmation. Commentary (1): His statement: 'If connected... etc. the speech becomes consistent because the basis of consistency is, as said, two things: connection to the previous in speech and the non-attachment of negation and affirmation to the same thing... (2) His statement: 'To the first confirmer' means the person who has the slave in his hand... (3) His statement: 'The statement of the confirmee is his saying: 'It was never mine' in the case of disconnection is a rejection of the confirmation...'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage, from al-Shashi's Usul with the hashya Ahsan al-Hawashi, explains the legal effect of the particle 'lakinna' in declarations of ownership (iqrar). The core rule: if someone says 'This slave is for X, but for Y' and the speech is continuous (connected without a break), the slave goes to the last person mentioned (Y) because the negation of X's ownership is directly linked to the affirmation of Y's. If there is a pause (disconnection), the slave stays with the first person (X), and the subsequent statement about Y is treated as a mere testimony by an individual, which does not establish ownership. The commentary elaborates on the conditions for consistency and the implications for legal validity.

Key Terms
لكن (lakinna): A particle used for istidrak (correction/amendment) that either connects to the previous speech or starts anew
وصل/فصل (wasl/fasl): Connection or disconnection in speech; 'wasl' means continuous without pause, 'fasl' means with a break

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the iqrar example, if someone says 'This slave is for Zaid, but for Amr' without a pause, who does the slave belong to?
A) Zaid
B) Amr
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of 'lakinna' in iqrar depends crucially on whether the speech is connected (wasl) or disconnected (fasl).

Rule of 'Lakinna' in Ijazah: Slave Girl's Marriage
Analysis of 'lakinna' in master's response to slave girl's marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for 'lakinna' to determine whether it functions as a conjunction or for starting anew in a legal statement, and derive the resulting legal effect.

Core Idea
If 'lakinna' follows a negation and affirms the same act, it indicates a new start, not a correction.

In this passage, we examine a scenario where a master responds to his slave girl's unauthorized marriage. His statement includes both a rejection and an acceptance with a different mahr. The Hanafi usul analysis reveals that the particle 'lakinna' (but) can either connect or begin anew depending on whether the negation and affirmation refer to the same essential act. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving the correct legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the speech pattern
Read the master's statement: 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 dirhams but I consent to it at 150.' Identify the negated and affirmed elements.
2
Determine if the negation and affirmation refer to the same essential act
Ask: Is the mahr an integral part of the marriage or an accessory? The passage states mahr is subsidiary, so negating the marriage at 100 negates the entire marriage. Therefore, affirming 'it' later refers to the same act.
This is the key analytical step.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for 'lakinna'
Since the speech is contradictory (negation and affirmation of the same act), 'lakinna' cannot be conjunctive. It must be for starting anew (isti'naf). The first part annuls the marriage, the second proposes a new marriage.
4
Contrast with the consistent case
If the master had said 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 but I consent to it at 150' with the intent that the negation applies only to the mahr restriction, then the speech is consistent and 'lakinna' would be conjunctive, preserving the original marriage.
This comparison clarifies the boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤) قوله: [لا أجيز العقد] يعني: أنّ الأمة إذا تزوّجت بغير إذن مولاها بمئة درهم، فقال المولى: «لا أجيــــز النكاح بمئة درهم ولكن أجيزه بمئة وخمسين»، فقوله: «لا أجيزه» نفي العقد وفسخ للنكاح، وقولـه: ولكن أجيزه ... إلخ» إثبات العقد، والإثبات والنفي في محلّ واحد محال، فجعل «لكن» حيئنذ مبتدأ؛ لأنّ هذا نفي فعل وإثباته بعينه، توضيحه لمّا قال المولى: «ولا أجيز العقد» فقد قلع النكاح عن أصــــله و لم يبق له وجه صحة، ثُمّ لَمّا قال بعده: ولكن أجيزه بمئة وخمسين يلزم أن يكون إثبات ذلك الفعل المنفي بعينه؛ لأنّ المهر في النكاح تابع الاعتبار له، فيتناقض أوّل الكلام بآخره، فحملناه على ابتــــداء النكاح بمهر آخر، وفسخ النكاح الأوّل الذي عقدته فيكون كلمة «لكن» ح للاستيناف لا للعطف، ولو قال المولى في جوابها: لا أجيز النكاح بمئة ولكن أجيزه بمئة وخمسين يكون هذا بعينـــه مثـــال الاتساق فبقي أصل النكاح ويكون النفي راجعاً إلى قيد المئة، والإثبات إلى قيد المئة والخمــــــيـن فــــلا يكون في صورة الوصل نفي فعل وإثباته بعينه فتدبّر، كذا قيل. ١٢

أصول الشاشي مع أحسن الحواشي
أي العقد. ١٢ درهما. ۱۲ فصل لكن للاستدراك
بمئة درهم ولكن أجيزه بمئة وخمسين بطل العقد ؛ لأن الكلام غير متسق فإن أي الإجازة. ١٢ في كل واحد. ١٢ أي العقد. ١٢ (۲) قوله: إثباته... إلخ فيه إشكال وهو أنا لا نسلّم ذلك؛ لأنه ردّ النكاح المقيد بمئة، وأجــــاز النكــــا المقيد بمئة وخمسين فلا يكون نفي الإجازة وإثباتها بعينها في شيء واحد، قلنا: بأن المهر في باب النكاح من الزوائد، ولهذا يصح النكاح بدون ذكره ومع نفيه فكان النفي من أصل النكاح، فكان قوله: «لكن أجيزه... إلخ» إثباته بعينه بعد نفيه فلا يعتبر ؛ لأنّ نكاح الأمة كان موقوفاً على إجازة المولى وقــــد انفسخ بالردّ والمفسوخ لا تلحقه الإجازة، فيكون «لكن للاستيناف لا للعطف، كذا في بعض الحواشي. ١٢
(4) His statement: 'I do not consent to the contract' means that when a slave girl marries without her master's permission for 100 dirhams, and the master says: 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 dirhams but I consent to it at 150', then his statement 'I do not consent' is a negation of the contract and annulment of the marriage, and his statement 'but I consent to it' is an affirmation of the contract. Affirmation and negation in the same place are impossible, so 'lakinna' here is considered for starting anew because this is negation of an act and affirmation of the same act. Explanation: when the master said 'I do not consent to the contract', he uprooted the marriage completely and it has no possibility of validity. Then when he later said 'but I consent to it at 150', it necessarily becomes an affirmation of that very negated act because the mahr in marriage is subsidiary to the contract. Thus the beginning and end of the speech contradict, so we interpret it as starting a new marriage with a different mahr and annul the first marriage, making 'lakinna' for starting anew, not for conjunction. If the master had said in response: 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 but I consent to it at 150', that would be an example of consistency, and the original marriage remains, with the negation referring to the restriction of 100 and the affirmation to the restriction of 150. So in the connected form, there is no negation of an act and its exact affirmation. Reflect on this. Thus it is said. 12 Then on next page: 'Ayy al-ʿaqd 12 dirhaman 12 Fasl lakin li al-istidrak bi-miʾati dirham wa lakin ujīzuhu bi-miʾati wa khamsīn baṭala al-ʿaqd; because the speech is inconsistent... Commentary (2): His statement: 'Affirmation... etc.' There is an objection: we do not concede that because he rejected the marriage restricted to 100 and accepted the marriage restricted to 150, so it is not negation of the exact same consent and affirmation. We respond: because mahr in marriage is among the accessories, so the marriage is valid without mentioning it, and with its negation, the negation is of the essence of the marriage. So his statement 'but I consent to it' is an affirmation of the very thing after its negation, hence it is not considered; because the slave girl's marriage was suspended upon the master's consent and it was annulled by the rejection, and the annulled marriage cannot be subject to consent. So 'lakinna' is for starting anew, not for conjunction. Thus it is in some marginal notes. 12
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage examines the usage of 'lakinna' in the context of a master's response to a slave girl's unauthorized marriage. The master says 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 dirhams but I consent to it at 150'. The analysis shows that because the mahr is an accessory to the marriage, negating the marriage at 100 effectively negates the entire marriage, and then affirming it at 150 would be an affirmation of the same marriage after negation, which is contradictory. Therefore, 'lakinna' is treated as beginning a new speech (isti'naf), not as a conjunction (ʿaṭf). The first part annuls the marriage, and the second part proposes a new marriage. If the master had said 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 but I consent to it at 150' in a way that the negation and affirmation are on the different restrictions (qayd), then the marriage would remain valid and 'lakinna' would be conjunctive. The commentary discusses an objection and resolves it by emphasizing the accessory nature of mahr.

Key Terms
لكن (lakinna): A particle that can either be conjunctive (ʿaṭf) or for starting anew (isti'naf) depending on consistency of speech
استدراك (istidrak): Correction or amendment in speech, typically using 'lakinna'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a master says 'I do not consent to the marriage at 100 dirhams but I consent to it at 150', what is the ruling on the marriage?
A) The marriage is valid with the new mahr of 150 dirhams.
B) The marriage is invalid; the second statement is a new proposal.
C) The marriage remains valid at 100 dirhams.
D) The master must accept the original mahr.

Key Takeaway
When 'lakinna' follows a negation and affirms the same essential act, it signals a new beginning, not a correction, and the prior act is void.
