نامية حكماً فإن قيل: ما الوجه في أنّ كلاً من العشر والخراج مسبب والأرض سببه، ومع ذلك اشترط النماء الحقيقي لوجوب العشر ولم يعتبر النماء التقديري له بخلاف الخراج، فإنّه اعتبر
The chapter: Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. By the passing of a year. The cause for the obligation of zakat is ownership of a productive nisab, in reality or by legal consideration [as growth], and the cause applies when the asset is for trade. That is, paying zakat before the year passes. (2) It is an apposition to his statement 'the cause for the obligation of prayer.' That is, the House of Allah, the Ka'bah. It is permissible to pay in advance when the cause exists. The cause for the obligation of hajj is the House, because it is attributed to it. That is, hajj. That is, the non-repetition of its obligation. (3) Which is a condition. And the non-repetition of the ritual once in a lifetime. So if one performs hajj before having the ability, it counts as hajj before ability. Since it is the House, it is not necessary to advance the cause. That is, hajj. By provisions and mount. That [hajj] suffices for the obligatory hajj because the cause exists, and this distinguishes paying zakat before the nisab (due to absence of cause). And the cause for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr is the head of the dependent. (4) The special relationship between cause and effect because the effect is established by the cause; and because attribution is a relation, and the most complete relation is the attribution of the ruling to the effect because it arises from it, not the relation of the contained to the container because the contained is not established by the container. As in al-Ma'din and al-Fusul. (1) His statement: 'ownership of the productive nisab... etc.' means real growth through trade or notional growth through the passage of a year, because through that complete growth of wealth is possible; the year includes all four seasons. Thus the difference between zakat and hajj becomes clear: zakat is repeated upon the passing of a year, but hajj is not, because a single nisab is considered to repeat notionally due to growth, whereas the House does not repeat at all, neither in reality nor notionally, because the sanctity of the House is a single continuous matter. The year is established in place of growth because real growth cannot be observed or there is negligence in seeking growth. Wealth is the cause for zakat because it is attributed to it; it is said: 'zakat of wealth.' So the existence of the nisab is the cause. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to pay in advance...' means: if one owns a nisab, it is permissible to pay zakat before the year passes because the effect is due after the cause exists. If it is said: the cause is ownership of the productive nisab, and before the year the nisab is incomplete, so the cause did not exist before the year, I say: the existence of the nisab is the cause and growth is a condition. (3) His statement: 'lack of repetition of the ritual' – the earlier discussion applies. One could say that the cause of its obligation is the existence of the slave qua slave, and the House and ability are both conditions, not the cause. On this too, repetition does not occur due to the unity of the slave qua slave. If the cause were the House, it would follow that hajj is a collective obligation, performed by some as prayer over the deceased, due to the unity of the cause. There is no sound justification except considering it leniency. (4) His statement: 'the head of the dependent.' It could be said: it has been established that by 'causes' are meant 'effective reasons' (ilal), and the head is not fit to be an effective reason because the effective reason must be a description, whereas the head is an essence. The reply: what is meant by the head is the continued existence of the head. (Page 287) That is, the mukallaf provides for the dependent and bears his expenses. The existence of the head. The chapter: Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. That is, paying sadaqat al-fitr in advance. (2) That is, sadaqat al-fitr. He supports and maintains them. And because the cause exists, it is permissible to pay in advance, so it is permissible to pay it before the day of that head. The mukallaf. The head. The produce. That is, the yield from the land. The cause for the obligation of ushr is lands that are productive in reality by the yield, and the cause for the obligation of kharaj is lands suitable for cultivation, so they are notional productive. And the cause for obligation (6) which is benefit and increase. (1) His statement: 'he supports them' based on his saying (peace be upon him): 'Pay for those for whom you provide for,' meaning 'bear this expense for whom his maintenance is obligatory upon you.' If it is said: sadaqat al-fitr is attributed to fitr; it is said 'sadaqat al-fitr,' and attribution indicates causality, so one knows that the time of fitr is the cause. I say: fitr is not a cause but a condition, and the cause is the head of the dependent. However, this cause does not operate except with this condition. The attribution to fitr is metaphorical because it is attribution to the condition. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to pay in advance.' Know that permissibility of paying the sadaqah (i.e., zakat) before the year ends has been reported in the hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that al-'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) asked the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) about paying his sadaqah before it became due, and he gave him permission. This was narrated by the four except al-Nasa'i, and also by al-Darimi, Ahmad, 'Abd al-Razzaq, al-Hakim, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi, who said it is mursal. It is also narrated from 'Ali by another chain in which he raised it to the Prophet concerning payment of two years' sadaqah by al-'Abbas. This is a sound basis for paying zakat early, and paying sadaqat al-fitr early is analogized to it. (3) His statement: 'lands that are productive.' Because ushr is attributed to land; it is said: 'ushr of the land.' If it is said: obligation is repeated with the repetition of the produce, which indicates causality, so the produce should be the cause. I say: the repetition of obligation is due to the repetition of the growth, by virtue of which the land becomes a cause. Thus the land, due to the repetition of growth, becomes as if it is repeated notionally. Then the secret in ushr is that the produce is a blessing, gratitude for which is due by giving a tenth or half of it. Since ease and mercy are appropriate for the Muslim, the duty was lightened, as for the amount of kharaj (the fifth) and in quality by requiring actual produce for ushr (i.e., the yield) whereas for kharaj it is not required; it is due on land that is productive in reality if something comes out of it, or notionally if it is abandoned, and it is not taken if a heavenly blight strikes it. (4) His statement: 'by real yield' means the produce, so if the owner abandons the 'ushri land, ushr is not due even if it is fertile. (5) His statement: 'so they are notional productive' – if it is said: what is the reasoning that each of ushr and kharaj is an effect and the land is its cause, yet real growth is required for ushr but notional growth is not considered for it, unlike kharaj?
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul with the commentary Ahsan al-Hawashi systematically enumerates the causes (asbab) for several obligatory acts of worship. The author begins by stating the general principle that Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. He then lists: (1) the cause of zakat is ownership of a productive nisab—either actually growing through trade or legally deemed growing by the passage of a full lunar year; (2) the cause of hajj is the House (the Ka'bah), though ability is a condition; (3) the cause of sadaqat al-fitr is the head of the dependent (i.e., the person one is obliged to support), and the day of fitr is merely a condition; (4) the cause of ushr is land that is actually productive (yielding produce); (5) the cause of kharaj is land suitable for cultivation (even if not currently producing). The footnotes address several paradoxes: why zakat repeats yearly while hajj does not—because the nisab notionally 'grows' each year, but the House does not; why early payment of zakat is allowed if the cause is not fully present until the year ends—the nisab is the cause, the passage of time is a condition; why sadaqat al-fitr is attributed to fitr rather than to the head—the head is the cause but fitr is a necessary condition; and why ushr requires actual produce while kharaj does not—because ushr is a thanksgiving for produce, while kharaj is a land tax. The hadith for early zakat is cited from the collection, and analogy extends it to sadaqat al-fitr. The commentary maintains Hanafi usul principles: causes are determined by textual indicators (idafa, hadith) and rational consistency (ta'lil). The teacher should emphasize that understanding the distinction between cause and condition is crucial to avoiding confusions about early performance and repetition.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The cause that entails the existence of the ruling; the factor that directly triggers the obligation.
شرط (shart): A condition external to the cause that must be present for the ruling to apply, but is not the cause itself.
نصاب نامي (nisab nami): A productive nisab; wealth that grows in reality (through trade) or is deemed to grow by the passage of a year.
رأس يمونه (ra's yamunuhu): The head of a dependent; the person for whom the mukallaf provides maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause (sabab) for zakat?
A) The passing of a year
B) Ownership of productive nisab
C) Trade
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The correct identification of causes and conditions is essential for correctly applying many fiqh rulings, especially regarding timing and repetition.

The Causes (Asbab) of Ushr and Kharaj in Hanafi Usul
Defining the causes of ushr and kharaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the causes for ushr and kharaj as per the text, distinguish between actual and notional productivity, and explain why the cause for ushr requires actual produce while kharaj does not.

Core Idea
The cause of ushr is land actually productive by yield; the cause of kharaj is land suitable for cultivation, making it notionally productive.

This section of the passage specifies the causes for two land-related obligations: ushr and kharaj. The key distinction is that ushr requires actual produce (real growth), while kharaj is due on cultivable land even if left fallow. The commentary provides the rationale for this difference, linking it to the nature of each as a thanksgiving or land tax.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause for ushr
The text states: 'سبب وجوب العشر الأراضي النامية بحقيقة الريع' (the cause for ushr is lands that are productive by real yield). Note that actual produce is required.
2
Identify the cause for kharaj
The text states: 'سبب وجوب الخراج الأراضي الصالحة للزراعة فكانت نامية حكما' (the cause for kharaj is lands suitable for cultivation, so they are considered productive notionally).
Suitability, not actual yield, is sufficient.
3
Understand the reasoning for the difference
The commentary explains that ushr is a thanksgiving for the produce itself, so it requires actual yield. Kharaj is a land tax imposed on land capable of production, so it is due even if the land is left idle.
The secret (sirr) is that ushr is gratitude for the blessing of the crop, while kharaj is a levy on land.
4
Apply the principle to related rulings
If the owner abandons ushri land, no ushr is due. If kharaj land is abandoned, kharaj is still due. Also, if a heavenly blight strikes ushri land, no ushr, but kharaj is not waived for such calamities.

نامية حكماً فإن قيل: ما الوجه في أنّ كلاً من العشر والخراج مسبب والأرض سببه، ومع ذلك اشترط النماء الحقيقي لوجوب العشر ولم يعتبر النماء التقديري له بخلاف الخراج، فإنّه اعتبر
The chapter: Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. By the passing of a year. The cause for the obligation of zakat is ownership of a productive nisab, in reality or by legal consideration [as growth], and the cause applies when the asset is for trade. That is, paying zakat before the year passes. (2) It is an apposition to his statement 'the cause for the obligation of prayer.' That is, the House of Allah, the Ka'bah. It is permissible to pay in advance when the cause exists. The cause for the obligation of hajj is the House, because it is attributed to it. That is, hajj. That is, the non-repetition of its obligation. (3) Which is a condition. And the non-repetition of the ritual once in a lifetime. So if one performs hajj before having the ability, it counts as hajj before ability. Since it is the House, it is not necessary to advance the cause. That is, hajj. By provisions and mount. That [hajj] suffices for the obligatory hajj because the cause exists, and this distinguishes paying zakat before the nisab (due to absence of cause). And the cause for the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr is the head of the dependent. (4) The special relationship between cause and effect because the effect is established by the cause; and because attribution is a relation, and the most complete relation is the attribution of the ruling to the effect because it arises from it, not the relation of the contained to the container because the contained is not established by the container. As in al-Ma'din and al-Fusul. (1) His statement: 'ownership of the productive nisab... etc.' means real growth through trade or notional growth through the passage of a year, because through that complete growth of wealth is possible; the year includes all four seasons. Thus the difference between zakat and hajj becomes clear: zakat is repeated upon the passing of a year, but hajj is not, because a single nisab is considered to repeat notionally due to growth, whereas the House does not repeat at all, neither in reality nor notionally, because the sanctity of the House is a single continuous matter. The year is established in place of growth because real growth cannot be observed or there is negligence in seeking growth. Wealth is the cause for zakat because it is attributed to it; it is said: 'zakat of wealth.' So the existence of the nisab is the cause. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to pay in advance...' means: if one owns a nisab, it is permissible to pay zakat before the year passes because the effect is due after the cause exists. If it is said: the cause is ownership of the productive nisab, and before the year the nisab is incomplete, so the cause did not exist before the year, I say: the existence of the nisab is the cause and growth is a condition. (3) His statement: 'lack of repetition of the ritual' – the earlier discussion applies. One could say that the cause of its obligation is the existence of the slave qua slave, and the House and ability are both conditions, not the cause. On this too, repetition does not occur due to the unity of the slave qua slave. If the cause were the House, it would follow that hajj is a collective obligation, performed by some as prayer over the deceased, due to the unity of the cause. There is no sound justification except considering it leniency. (4) His statement: 'the head of the dependent.' It could be said: it has been established that by 'causes' are meant 'effective reasons' (ilal), and the head is not fit to be an effective reason because the effective reason must be a description, whereas the head is an essence. The reply: what is meant by the head is the continued existence of the head. (Page 287) That is, the mukallaf provides for the dependent and bears his expenses. The existence of the head. The chapter: Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. That is, paying sadaqat al-fitr in advance. (2) That is, sadaqat al-fitr. He supports and maintains them. And because the cause exists, it is permissible to pay in advance, so it is permissible to pay it before the day of that head. The mukallaf. The head. The produce. That is, the yield from the land. The cause for the obligation of ushr is lands that are productive in reality by the yield, and the cause for the obligation of kharaj is lands suitable for cultivation, so they are notional productive. And the cause for obligation (6) which is benefit and increase. (1) His statement: 'he supports them' based on his saying (peace be upon him): 'Pay for those for whom you provide for,' meaning 'bear this expense for whom his maintenance is obligatory upon you.' If it is said: sadaqat al-fitr is attributed to fitr; it is said 'sadaqat al-fitr,' and attribution indicates causality, so one knows that the time of fitr is the cause. I say: fitr is not a cause but a condition, and the cause is the head of the dependent. However, this cause does not operate except with this condition. The attribution to fitr is metaphorical because it is attribution to the condition. (2) His statement: 'it is permissible to pay in advance.' Know that permissibility of paying the sadaqah (i.e., zakat) before the year ends has been reported in the hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that al-'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) asked the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) about paying his sadaqah before it became due, and he gave him permission. This was narrated by the four except al-Nasa'i, and also by al-Darimi, Ahmad, 'Abd al-Razzaq, al-Hakim, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi, who said it is mursal. It is also narrated from 'Ali by another chain in which he raised it to the Prophet concerning payment of two years' sadaqah by al-'Abbas. This is a sound basis for paying zakat early, and paying sadaqat al-fitr early is analogized to it. (3) His statement: 'lands that are productive.' Because ushr is attributed to land; it is said: 'ushr of the land.' If it is said: obligation is repeated with the repetition of the produce, which indicates causality, so the produce should be the cause. I say: the repetition of obligation is due to the repetition of the growth, by virtue of which the land becomes a cause. Thus the land, due to the repetition of growth, becomes as if it is repeated notionally. Then the secret in ushr is that the produce is a blessing, gratitude for which is due by giving a tenth or half of it. Since ease and mercy are appropriate for the Muslim, the duty was lightened, as for the amount of kharaj (the fifth) and in quality by requiring actual produce for ushr (i.e., the yield) whereas for kharaj it is not required; it is due on land that is productive in reality if something comes out of it, or notionally if it is abandoned, and it is not taken if a heavenly blight strikes it. (4) His statement: 'by real yield' means the produce, so if the owner abandons the 'ushri land, ushr is not due even if it is fertile. (5) His statement: 'so they are notional productive' – if it is said: what is the reasoning that each of ushr and kharaj is an effect and the land is its cause, yet real growth is required for ushr but notional growth is not considered for it, unlike kharaj?
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from al-Shashi's Usul with the commentary Ahsan al-Hawashi systematically enumerates the causes (asbab) for several obligatory acts of worship. The author begins by stating the general principle that Shariah rulings are attached to their causes. He then lists: (1) the cause of zakat is ownership of a productive nisab—either actually growing through trade or legally deemed growing by the passage of a full lunar year; (2) the cause of hajj is the House (the Ka'bah), though ability is a condition; (3) the cause of sadaqat al-fitr is the head of the dependent (i.e., the person one is obliged to support), and the day of fitr is merely a condition; (4) the cause of ushr is land that is actually productive (yielding produce); (5) the cause of kharaj is land suitable for cultivation (even if not currently producing). The footnotes address several paradoxes: why zakat repeats yearly while hajj does not—because the nisab notionally 'grows' each year, but the House does not; why early payment of zakat is allowed if the cause is not fully present until the year ends—the nisab is the cause, the passage of time is a condition; why sadaqat al-fitr is attributed to fitr rather than to the head—the head is the cause but fitr is a necessary condition; and why ushr requires actual produce while kharaj does not—because ushr is a thanksgiving for produce, while kharaj is a land tax. The hadith for early zakat is cited from the collection, and analogy extends it to sadaqat al-fitr. The commentary maintains Hanafi usul principles: causes are determined by textual indicators (idafa, hadith) and rational consistency (ta'lil). The teacher should emphasize that understanding the distinction between cause and condition is crucial to avoiding confusions about early performance and repetition.

Key Terms
العشر (al-ushr): The tithe due on produce from Muslim-owned land; a form of zakat on agricultural output.
الخراج (al-kharaj): The land tax levied on non-Muslim-owned land (or land that was conquered) based on its productive capacity.
ريع (ray'): Yield, produce, or increase from land.
نامية حقيقة (namiya haqiqa): Productive in reality; actually producing yield.
نامية حكما (namiya hukman): Notionally productive; deemed productive by virtue of suitability for cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause (sabab) for ushr?
A) Lands suitable for cultivation
B) Lands that are productive in reality by yield
C) The produce (al-kharij)
D) The landowner's ownership

Key Takeaway
The cause of ushr is land that actually produces yield; the cause of kharaj is land suitable for cultivation; this distinction determines liability in cases of crop failure or abandonment.

Four Types of Preventers in Legal Causation
Classification of four types of preventers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four types of preventers (mawani') and correctly classify given examples according to whether they block the formation, completion, beginning, or continuation of a legal cause or ruling.

Core Idea
A preventer actively blocks legal effect; its type depends on when it intervenes: formation, completion, initiation, or continuation.

Legal causes (illas) do not always produce their full effect. Sometimes an external obstacle intervenes, preventing the cause from forming, completing, beginning its effect, or lasting. This lesson introduces the four types of preventers (mawani') as classified by Imam Abu Zayd in Usul al-Shashi, with illustrative examples from Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four preventer types: 1) Prevents cause formation, 2) Prevents cause completion, 3) Prevents ruling beginning, 4) Prevents ruling continuation.
Each type targets a different stage of the cause's lifecycle.
2
Match each example to its type
For each example given (selling a free person, destruction of nisab, sale with option, option of majority), determine which stage it blocks and explain why.
Focus on the stage that is interrupted, not just the final effect.
3
Distinguish preventer from missing condition
A preventer is an active presence that blocks; a missing condition is simply an absence. Consider how the examples reflect an existing interfering factor.
E.g., a contract may be valid but an extrinsic factor (option) delays effect.
4
Apply the taxonomy to new scenarios
Given a new legal scenario (e.g., a gift not delivered, a rental before possession), identify which type of preventer might apply.
Use the logic of causation stages from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل أقسام الموانع
فصل قال القاضي الإمام أبو زيد: الموانع أربعة أقسام: مانع يمنع انعقاد العلة، ومانع يمنع تمامها، ومانع يمنع ابتداء الحكم، ومانع يمنع دوام الحكم. فالأول: كبيع الحر والميتة والدم. والثاني: كهلاك النصاب في أثناء الحول وامتناع أحد الشاهدين عن الشهادة. والثالث: كالبيع بشرط الخيار وبقاء الوقت في حق صاحب العذر. والرابع: كخيار البلوغ والعتق والرؤية وعدم الكفاءة والاندمال في باب الجراحات على هذا الأصل.
Chapter: Types of Preventers. The chief judge Abu Zayd said: The preventers are four types: a preventer that prevents the formation of the cause, a preventer that prevents its completion, a preventer that prevents the beginning of the ruling, and a preventer that prevents its continuation. The first: like selling a free person, a dead animal, and blood. The second: like the destruction of the nisab during the year and the refusal of one of the two witnesses to testify. The third: like selling with a condition of option and the continuance of time in the case of the excused person. The fourth: like the option of majority, the option of emancipation, the option of inspection, lack of compatibility, and healing in the chapter of wounds, according to this principle.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This is a passage from Usul al-Shashi defining the four types of preventers (mawani') in legal reasoning. The text is a classification by Imam Abu Zayd. A preventer is something that blocks the operation of a legal cause (illa) or the effect of a ruling. The four types are: 1) A preventer that blocks the formation of the cause itself. Example: selling a free person, carrion, or blood — these are not valid subjects of sale (mahall), so the transaction does not form as a cause of ownership. 2) A preventer that blocks the completion of the cause. Example: the nisab (minimum threshold for zakat) being destroyed during the year — the cause (nisab) was present but its full effect requires completion of the year; or a witness withdrawing after testifying — the cause (testimony) was initiated but not completed. 3) A preventer that blocks the beginning of the ruling. Example: selling with a condition of option (khiyar al-shart) — the contract is concluded, but ownership does not commence until the option period ends; or the continuance of time for a person with a chronic excuse (sahib al-udhr) — the cause (hadath) exists but the ruling (invalidating wudu) does not begin due to the excuse. 4) A preventer that blocks the continuation of the ruling. Example: the option of majority (for a minor who married without guardian), the option of emancipation (for a slave-woman upon being freed), the option of inspection (for a buyer who has not seen the item), lack of compatibility (kafa'a) in marriage causing annulment, and healing of a wound that removes the claim for blood money — these prevent the ruling from persisting after it had taken effect. The footnotes elaborate that some later scholars add a fifth type (preventer of the completion of the ruling), but that is included here in the fourth type.

Key Terms
مانع (mani'): A preventer that blocks the operation of a legal cause (illa) or the effect of a ruling, either in formation, completion, beginning, or continuation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a preventer that blocks the completion of the cause (tamam al-illa)?
A) Selling a free person
B) Destruction of the nisab during the year
C) Selling with a condition of option
D) Option of majority for a minor

Key Takeaway
The four-type classification of preventers (mawani') provides a precise tool for analyzing legal causation: knowing which stage is blocked determines the correct ruling.

The Invalidity of Inference from Absence of Cause (عدم العلة)
Chapter on argument without evidence: types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of inferring the absence of a ruling from the absence of a specific cause and apply the Hanafi principle that a ruling may have multiple causes.

Core Idea
Absence of a cause does not prove absence of ruling unless the cause is exclusive.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, one of the invalid forms of argumentation is inferring that a ruling does not apply simply because a specific cause is absent. This lesson examines this fallacy through concrete examples from fiqh, demonstrating that a ruling may be established by multiple causes. Understanding this principle prevents hasty conclusions in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument type
Recognize that the passage describes an inference from absence of cause (adam al-illa) to absence of ruling (adam al-hukm). This is a common logical fallacy in fiqh.
The fallacy assumes that the stated cause is the only cause.
2
Examine the vomiting example
Vomiting is argued not to nullify wudu because it does not come from the two passages. Note that the Hanafi school considers any impurity exiting the body as a nullifier, so the cause is not exclusive.
The argument would be valid only if nullification were limited to those passages.
3
Analyse the brother example
A brother is not freed if owned because there is no parentage. However, in Hanafi fiqh, freedom can also be based on mahramiyya (permanent marriage prohibition due to blood relationship). Thus, absence of parentage does not negate the ruling.
Mahramiyya is a separate cause for freedom.
4
Review the partner of a minor example
The partner of a minor is not liable for qisas because the minor is exempt. The questioner challenges that in the father scenario, the father's exemption does not exempt the foreign partner. Understand that each case has its own ruling; exemption of one does not exempt the other.
The minor's exemption is due to lack of accountability, not the act itself.
5
Synthesize the principle
Conclude that the absence of one possible cause does not negate the ruling if other causes exist. The ruling stands unless all causes are absent.
This principle prevents fallacious arguments in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الاحتجاج بلا دليل أنواع منها الاستدلال بعدم العلة على عدم الحكم، مثاله القيء غير ناقض؛ لأنه لم يخرج من السبيلين، والأخ لا يعتق على الأخ لأنه لا ولاد بينهما، وسئل محمد رحمه الله أيجب القصاص على شريك الصبي، قال: «لا»؛ لأن الصبي رفع عنه القلم، قال السائل: فوجب فيما إذا قتل الأب ابنه بشركة رجل أجنبي.
Chapter on argumentation without evidence. Its types include: inference from the absence of the cause to the absence of the ruling. Its example: vomiting is not a nullifier [of wudu] because it did not come out from the two passages. And a brother is not freed [if owned] by his brother because there is no parentage between them. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, was asked: Is qisas obligatory on the partner of a minor? He said: No, because the minor is exempted from accountability. The questioner said: So it becomes obligatory in the case when a father kills his son with the partnership of a foreign man.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage discusses a principle in Hanafi usul: inferring the absence of a ruling from the absence of a specific cause is generally invalid, because a ruling may have multiple causes. The examples illustrate this: for vomiting, the nullification of wudu is not exclusive to exit from the two passages (the Hanafi school holds that any impurity exiting the body nullifies wudu, e.g., blood). For the brother, the cause for freedom upon ownership is not only parentage but also mahramiyya (blood relationship rendering marriage prohibited). For the partner of a minor, the minor's lack of accountability does not negate the adult partner's accountability for qisas. Similarly, in the father case, the father's exemption (due to hadith 'You and your wealth belong to your father' and 'A parent is not executed for a child') does not exempt the foreign partner. These show that the absence of one cause does not prove the absence of the ruling.

Key Terms
عدم العلة (adam al-illa): The absence of a specific cause for a ruling.
استدلال (istidlal): Inference or argumentation based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the argument that 'vomiting does not nullify wudu because it does not come from the two passages' considered weak in Hanafi usul?
A) Because vomiting always comes from the two passages.
B) Because the cause of nullification is not exclusive to exiting from the two passages.
C) Because the Hanafi school does not consider vomiting at all.
D) Because it is based on a hadith that is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, inferring the absence of a ruling from the absence of a specific cause is invalid unless that cause is proven exclusive; rulings often have multiple causes.

Inference from Absence of Exclusive Cause and Istishab
Usul rule definition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition for inference from absence of cause (illa munhasira) and distinguish it from istishab al-hal.

Core Idea
Absence of a cause proves absence of ruling only if the cause is exclusive.

This lesson examines two key usul arguments: when lack of a cause can substantiate lack of a ruling, and the presumption of continuity (istishab). The source clarifies that inference from absence is valid only for exclusive causes, while istishab is a weak presumption, not a proof. The examples from Imam Muhammad illustrate the exclusive cause principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text begins by stating that arguing from the absence of a cause to the absence of a ruling is generally invalid, like saying 'he did not die because he did not fall from a roof' (since death has many causes).
This is a fallacy if the cause is not exclusive.
2
Spot the exception
The exception occurs when the cause is exclusive (munhasira) – meaning the ruling has only that one cause. Then the absence of that cause necessarily implies the absence of the ruling.
Exclusivity is key.
3
Apply the rule to the examples
Study the two examples from Imam Muhammad: (a) The offspring of a usurped animal is not guaranteed because the cause of guarantee (usurpation) is exclusive to the animal itself. (b) A witness who retracts in a qisas case is not subject to qisas because the cause (direct killing) is exclusive.
In each case, the absence of the exclusive cause eliminates the ruling.
4
Differentiate istishab al-hal
Istishab al-hal is a separate argument: it presumes that a state of affairs continues until evidence of change. The text notes that this is an argument without evidence, based on the absence of evidence to the contrary.
Istishab is weaker than the exclusive cause argument.
5
Recognize epistemological weight
Understand that istishab is not a definitive proof; it merely preserves the status quo. In Hanafi usul, it is used only when no other evidence exists.
It is a practical tool, not a strong evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الاحتجاج بلا دليل العلة على عدم الحكم، هذا بمنزلة ما يقال: «لم يمت فلان»؛ لأنه لم يسقط من السطح إلا إذا كانت علة الحكم منحصرة في معنى فيكون ذلك المعنى لازما خاص مع انتفاء باقي العلل. مثاله ما روي عن محمد رحمه الله أنه قال: ولد المغصوبة ليس بمضمون؛ لأنه ليس بمغصوب ولا قصاص على الشاهد في مسئلة شهود القصاص إذا رجعوا؛ لأنه ليس بقاتل؛ وذلك لأن الغصب لازم لضمان الغصب والقتل لازم لوجود القصاص، وكذلك التمسك باستصحاب الحال تمسّك بعدم الدليل؛ إذ وجود الشيء لا يوجب بقاءه. وهذا ظاهر.
Chapter on argumentation without evidence: the cause on the absence of the ruling. This is like saying: 'So-and-so did not die because he did not fall from a roof.' Unless the cause of the ruling is exclusive to a meaning, then that meaning becomes a specific necessary concomitant with the absence of other causes. Its example: what is narrated from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, that he said: 'The offspring of a usurped [animal] is not guaranteed because it is not usurped.' And there is no qisas on the witness in the case of the witnesses of qisas when they retract because he is not a killer. That is because usurpation is a necessary condition for guarantee of usurpation, and killing is a necessary condition for the existence of qisas. Similarly, adhering to istishab al-hal is adherence to the absence of evidence, because the existence of a thing does not necessitate its continuation. And this is apparent.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies the exception to the rule: inference from absence of cause is only valid when the cause is exclusive, i.e., the ruling has no other cause. The analogy of death from falling illustrates the fallacy of assuming a single cause (people die from many reasons). When the cause is exclusive, the absence of the cause necessarily implies the absence of the ruling. Examples: the offspring of a usurped animal is not subject to guarantee because the cause of guarantee (usurpation) is exclusive; if the animal was not usurped (the offspring is not usurped), there is no guarantee. Similarly, a witness who retracts after testifying in a qisas case is not subject to qisas because the cause (direct killing) is exclusive. Finally, the text mentions istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) as another form of argument without evidence. It means assuming that a state of affairs continues until evidence of change, but this is not a definitive proof; it is only a practical presumption.

Key Terms
علة منحصرة (illa munhasira): An exclusive cause; a cause that is the only one for a particular ruling.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity; assuming a state persists until evidence of change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition makes inference from absence of cause valid in Hanafi usul?
A) When the cause is known.
B) When the cause is exclusive.
C) When there are multiple causes.
D) When the cause is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, arguing from absence of cause is only sound when the cause is exclusive; otherwise, it is fallacious, and istishab is merely a weak presumption.

The Principle of Not Ruling Without Evidence: Hayd Maximum
Usul rule on maximum hayd and evidence requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul rule that prohibits acting without evidence and apply it to determine the maximum duration of hayd from the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Ruling without evidence is invalid; hayd's maximum of ten days is proven by hadith, so less than ten cannot be declared pure.

This lesson explores a key usul principle drawn from the matn: the prohibition against acting without evidence (dalil). The passage uses the example of menstruation to illustrate that if we were to rule that a woman's period ends before ten days, we would be making a ruling without proof. The hadith establishes that hayd does not exceed ten days, so any bleeding beyond that is istihada. The lesson also highlights a parallel from the law of the missing person to reinforce the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on hayd's maximum
State the Hanafi position: the maximum duration of hayd is ten days, as per the hadith.
2
Understand the principle against acting without evidence
The author argues that if we declared hayd to have ended before ten days, we would be making a judgment without any evidence (bila dalil), which is impermissible in usul al-fiqh.
This is the core legal principle: la yajuz al-amal bila dalil.
3
Locate the dalil
The evidence is a hadith (though with a weak chain) stating that the maximum hayd is ten days. Identify the source and its authority level (zanni al-thubut).
Dalil block shows hadith reference.
4
Apply the principle to the missing person analogy
The hashia mentions the missing person case: we cannot award inheritance without evidence, just as we cannot end hayd early without evidence. This illustrates the same principle.
Analogy reinforces the rule.
5
Derive the usul rule
From this passage, extract the usul rule: 'A ruling must have a valid shar'i evidence; acting without evidence is invalid.' This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحيضها عشرة أيام؛ لأن ما دون العشرة تحتمل الحيض والاستحاضة فلو حكمنا بأن لم نجعل العشرة كلها حيضا. بارتفاع الحيض لزمنا العمل بلا دليل بخلاف ما بعد العشرة لقيام الدليل على أن الحيض لا تزيد على العشرة. (1) أي بخلاف حكمنا بارتفاع الحيض فيما بعدها إلخ. بارتفاع الحيض لزمنا العمل بلا دليل بخلاف ما بعد العشرة لقيام الدليل على تبعيضية. (2) أن الحيض لا تزيد على العشرة ومن الدليل على أن لا دليل فيه إلاّ وهو الغائب الذي لم يدر موضعه ولا حياته ولا مماته. حال فقده لأن حياته ثابتة باستصحاب الحال. حجة للدفع دون الإلزام مسئلة المفقود فإنّه لا يستحق غيره ميراثه ولو مات المفقود. المفقود. أحد. أي ممن مات من أقاربه. من أقاربه حال فقده لا يرث هو منه فاندفع استحقاق الغير بلا دليل ولم يثبت له الاستحقاق بلا دليل.
Her menstruation is ten days; because what is less than ten could be either menstruation or istihada, and if we ruled that we do not make all ten days menstruation, [we would be] ruling that menstruation has ended without evidence, unlike after ten days because there is evidence that menstruation does not exceed ten days. (1) i.e., contrary to our ruling that menstruation has ended after it etc. [If we ruled] that menstruation has ended, we would be acting without evidence, contrary to after ten days because there is evidence on its partial nature. (2) That menstruation does not exceed ten days, and among the evidence is that there is no evidence except that it is the absent one whose location, life, or death is unknown. His state of absence [is treated] because his life is established by istishab al-hal. It is a proof for repelling but not for obligating. The issue of the missing person: he does not deserve others' inheritance, and if the missing person dies... one, i.e., among those who died from his relatives. From his relatives during his absence, he does not inherit from him, so the others' entitlement is repelled without evidence, and for him entitlement is not established without evidence.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn discussing the maximum duration of menstruation (hayd) and the principle of not ruling without evidence. The author states that a woman's period is ten days, because anything less than ten could be either hayd or istihada (non-menstrual bleeding). If we were to rule that not all ten days are hayd, we would be declaring that hayd has ended without any evidence, which is impermissible. After ten days, however, there is clear evidence (hadith) that hayd does not exceed ten days, so it is safe to rule it as istihada. The hashia comments further explain that ruling that hayd ends before ten days would be acting without evidence, whereas after ten days there is evidence (the hadith) that hayd is partial. The hashia also mentions that the evidence includes the hadith reported by Abu Umama etc., though the chain is discussed. The principle is that we must have proof before declaring a state of purity (tuhr) or ending hayd.

Key Terms
الاستحاضة (al-istihada): Non-menstrual bleeding that is not subject to the rules of hayd
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood with minimum 3 and maximum 10 days

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must all ten days be considered hayd?
A) Because the evidence is clear that hayd is exactly ten days
B) Because ruling that some days are not hayd would be acting without evidence
C) Because the woman says so
D) Because istihada never occurs before ten days

Key Takeaway
The fundamental usul principle that one cannot rule without evidence is applied here: all ten days within the maximum are presumptively hayd because ending hayd earlier would lack evidence.

Istishab al-Hal: A Proof for Repelling, Not Obligating
Application of istishab al-hal to inheritance of a missing person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that istishab al-hal is a proof for repelling (daf') not for obligating (ilzam) to the case of the missing person and explain why it cannot establish inheritance rights.

Core Idea
Istishab repels claims without evidence but cannot create obligations.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author addresses a key distinction in the Hanafi theory of evidence: istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) is a proof (hujja) for repelling claims, not for establishing new rights. The case of the missing person (mafqud) serves as a concrete illustration. The hashia then defends the coherence of using this case as evidence for the principle without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Istishab al-hal is a proof for repelling (daf') not obligating (ilzam). This is a foundational Hanafi usul principle.
2
Examine the missing person case
When a relative dies, the missing person does not inherit from them, and others cannot inherit from the missing person without evidence.
The text states: 'لا يستحق غيره ميراثه' (he does not deserve others' inheritance).
3
Understand the repelling function
The missing person's life is presumed by istishab, but this presumption cannot obligate inheritance because it is only a defense against claims that would require proof of change (e.g., death).
Istishab repels claims that contradict the presumed state (life), but does not establish claims requiring evidence.
4
Recognize why istishab cannot obligate
Granting inheritance to the missing person would require evidence of life at the exact time of the relative's death. Istishab provides only a general presumption, not specific evidence.
Thus, istishab is used to repel (e.g., prevent distribution of the missing person's estate) but not to establish rights.
5
Analyze the hashia on circularity
The hashia argues that the case is not used as a proof for the principle (which would be circular); rather, the principle is independently established and the case illustrates it.
The madhhab's position on istishab is predicated on other evidence, not derived from this single case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسئلة المفقود فإنّه لا يستحق غيره ميراثه ولو مات المفقود. المفقود. أحد. أي ممن مات من أقاربه. من أقاربه حال فقده لا يرث هو منه فاندفع استحقاق الغير بلا دليل ولم يثبت له الاستحقاق بلا دليل. (3) قوله: [مسألة المفقود] فإن قلت بناء هذه المسئلة على أن استصحاب الحال حجة دافعة لا ملزمة، ولو جعل هذه المسئلة دليلاً عليه كما أفاده المصنف رحمه الله كان دوراً، قلت: إنّ استصحاب الحال دليل على هذه المسئلة دليلاً على أنّ استصحاب الحال حجة دافعة لا ملزمة فغير مسلّم، بل هـذه المسئلة جعل دليلاً على أنّ المذهب لأصحابنا في استصحاب الحال هو ما ذكرنا من أنه حجة دافعة لا ملزمة، حاصل الجواب : أنّ استصحاب الحال دليل على هذه المسئلة، وأما كون هذه المسألة دليلاً على أنّ استصحاب الحال حجة للدفع فغير مسلّم، بل هي دليل على أن... إلخ.
The issue of the missing person: he does not deserve others' inheritance, and if the missing person dies... one, i.e., among those who died from his relatives. From his relatives during his absence, he does not inherit from him, so the others' entitlement is repelled without evidence, and for him entitlement is not established without evidence. (3) His statement: [Issue of the missing person] If you say: building this issue on the principle that istishab al-hal is a proof for repelling not obligating, and if this issue is made a proof for that as the author indicated, it would be circular. I say: istishab al-hal is a proof for this issue, a proof that istishab al-hal is a proof for repelling not obligating is not conceded. Rather, this issue is made a proof that the madhhab of our companions regarding istishab al-hal is that it is a proof for repelling not obligating. The summary of the answer: istishab al-hal is a proof for this issue, but that this issue is a proof that istishab is a proof for repelling is not conceded; rather, it is a proof that... etc.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage explains the principle that istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) is a proof for repelling (daf') but not for obligating (ilzam). The case of the missing person (mafqud) is used: when a relative dies, the missing person does not inherit from them, nor do others inherit from the missing person, to avoid granting inheritance without evidence. This shows that istishab is used to prevent claims (repelling) but not to establish claims (obligating). The hashia addresses a potential circularity: the issue itself is based on istishab, but it is not used as a proof that istishab is only for repelling; rather, the madhhab's position is independent.

Key Terms
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of a previous state until evidence of change
دافع (dafi'): Repelling, preventing a claim
ملزمة (mulzimah): Obligating, establishing a right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashia, why is it not circular to use the case of the missing person as evidence for the principle that istishab is a proof for repelling?
A) Because the case itself is based on the principle, so it cannot be evidence for it.
B) Because the principle is independently established, and the case only illustrates it.
C) Because the case is from the Quran.
D) Because the case involves multiple heirs.

Key Takeaway
Istishab al-hal in the Hanafi school is a proof for repelling claims, not for establishing rights; it preserves the status quo until evidence of change appears.
